But when it is done with fire, make the cautery cone-shaped like a vulture's beak, which should be made of iron, according to the size that contains a claw from all sides; and let it have a thin edge.
Then place the heated instrument over the nail. Then turn the hand with the cautery around the nail, until the cautery reaches the depth of the nail: and let it be for three days, until it begins to discharge pus. Then apply ointment to it with the juices of wild rue, crushed with salt: and let the dressing be on it at night. For it will be rooted out from its roots.
Then care for the place with a unguent of generated flesh, until it is healed.
But if they are not converted and often occur on the surface of the body, especially in hands and feet: then administer to them a cautery with hot water: and it is, as you take a tube made of air, or iron, or from a bird's feather: and place it over the nail, or the ulcer. Then pour a small amount of hot water into the tube: hold your hand firm and turn it with compressive pressure from your own hand for a little while, so that the margin of the tube imprints itself on the root of the nail, and the hot water finds its way to the depth, to the root of the nail: and let the patient wait for a moment after the stinging of the hot water for one hour.
Then let it be, for the nail is rooted out with its roots.
So do this one after another, until you come to all those that are on the body.
Then care for them after they have been rooted out, with what generates flesh from ointments.
Of the manner of cauterizing in Tremors.
When it happens to someone that they tremble from cold in the nerve, or from quartan fever, or another such cause: then it is necessary for you to cauterize it four times, or five times, over the vertebrae of the back, and between every vertebra, and on his chest, and above his stomach.
Shaking (tremor) ceases with olive oil cautery, and hastens recovery from cold illness.
On the manner of cauterizing: Albathir.
Chapter 57.
Sometimes the foul things, that is, pimples, which are produced from corrupted thick substances, are purged out of the body by Albathir.
It is therefore necessary at first to cauterize all pimples with a subtle cautery and the wood of myrtle, whose extremity has been ignited with fire. Or with the root of Aristolochia longa. Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized to dry up at the beginning of their expulsion according to this method: and they do not increase; and the excess is broken forth, making them whole again.
However, it is necessary for that to be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
On the manner of cauterizing in bleeding from an artery in a wound.
Many times it happens that bleeding occurs from an artery which has been cut at a wound, which occurs externally, or at the incision of an abscess, or from a cautery on a member, and similar cases: and it becomes difficult to stop it.
When therefore it happens to someone, then hasten with your hand to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger on top of it: and press it down much, until the blood is blocked under your finger and nothing else comes out.
Then place small and large olive oil cauterias in the fire, and blow on them until they become very well lit.
Then take one, small or large, according to the wound and place where the artery is disrupted, and put the cauteria on top of the vein, after you quickly remove your finger; and hold the cauteria until the bleeding stops.
If the blood is expelled when you lift your finger from the artery opening and you extinguish the cautery, then take another cautery quickly, prepared beforehand among those that are in fire.
So do not stop doing that one after another until the blood is cut off.
And beware, lest you burn the nerve which is there, and another misfortune occur above the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, then it is not possible to cut it off; especially if the artery is great, unless with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we said. Or with extraction when it is not extracted. For when it is drawn out, its ends contract and the blood is cut off. Or be tied with a strong tying thread. Or put on top of it medicines with the property to stop bleeding, and bind with a decent constriction using poultices.
But he who administers its cutting off with a bandage and binding with cloth, or by placing the things that are burning in similar ways, does not at all benefit it, except rarely.
If however it happens to someone and there is no doctor present nor medicine: then he will start by placing the index finger on top of the opening of the vein itself, just as we narrated, and constricting it very much, until the blood is blocked from the upper part of the wound and above the artery. And the finger should not be removed from above it with cold water of very intense coldness always, until the blood freezes and thickens and is cut off.
And meanwhile he should consider what is needed for the cautery or medicine.
About incision, perforation, and bloodletting in wounds, and similar things.

And I have placed the chapters there from top to bottom.

And because of this, it is necessary to be even more cautious, since in the operation that is in this chapter, evacuation often occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision over an abscess, or opening of an exit wound, or curing of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of these fear, trembling, and death occur in many of them.
And I warn you, let not that fall upon you in which there is hesitation, for sometimes such things happen to you in this art, with regard to men and their various infirmities. For one of them is angry with his own affliction, and death is light to him: because of the intensity of his anger which he finds from his weakness and the length of his wickedness. And when afflicted, it is due to fear that signifies death.
And one of them gives himself over to his own medicine, and enriches you with it, hoping for recovery, and his affliction is deadly.

And do not presume over anything from that, unless after certain knowledge has verified it with you, which confirms the successful succession to what has been reached.

May God guide you, sons, with his understanding, and not take away from you direction and inspiration; for it is in his hand.
And I indeed arranged this Chapter by dividing it into chapters, just as preceded in the chapter on Cauterization, from head to feet; so that what you seek may be alleviated for you, and where you are lacking.
About the treatment of water that gathers in children's heads.
Chapter I.
Know that this weakness often befalls children at birth: or when the midwife tightens the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it happens also because of a hidden cause that is not known.
And I indeed did not see this sickness in anyone other than children: and to all of them from whom I saw it, death has hastened forth: therefore it seemed to me that I should abandon the treatment for it.
And I indeed saw a child from whom, whose head was filled with water, and his head grew in all day, until he could no longer sit upright because of the greatness of his head; and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture indeed either accumulates between the skin and mouth: or it accumulates under the bone above the sinuses.
And the operation on him, if there is moisture between the skin and mouth, and a small apostema is present, then you must make an incision in the middle of the head, making one gap about two nodes wide, until the moisture flows.
And this is the form of a spatula.
So if moisture has been increased and the abscess is larger, then place these two incisions according to this shape.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and you see a sign of it, namely that you see the edges of the head open from every side; and when you press it with your hand towards the inside, and it does not hide from you: then it is necessary to cut in the middle of the head three incisions, according to this method.
And after making the incision, remove the moisture entirely. Then tie the incisions with cloths and lint. Then pack that on top with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage and care for the wound with threads and ointments.
And do not cease to bind the head with equal tightness. And feed the sick person all dry food with a little moisture until the member is comforted and healed.
And another method of incision is, that you should observe where the magnitude of the abscess appears and the collection of water.
Because sometimes it is more prominent on the back of the head, or on the forehead, or on the right side, or on the left.
So ascend with an incision where you see the abscess and filling of water, and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And beware that you do not cut an artery, and a flow of blood occurs, and the sick person dies from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Â§ On the cutting of arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When someone experiences a discharge from their eyes or chest and it becomes old and does not respond to medical treatment: then the ultimate cure for that is by cutting these two arteries.
It is necessary therefore when you wish to cut them that the patient weakly bends his head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth so that the arteries may appear. Then the patient should tighten his neck with any of his own cloths. Then examine where the vein beats. And both locations are places that are behind ears depressed: and it is rarely hidden except in some people.
Then mark with encaustum over each one: and cut both with a scalp-lifter on either side accurately, the incision reaching to the mouth: and make it along the breadth of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalp-lifter under the artery, and cut it to the upper parts with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be nearly as long as two joined fingers.
For when a vein is cut, blood issues forth in pulsatile emission, which flows inward with frequent leaping.
If however the artery does not appear to sense, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear a space of three fingers. Then mark with encaustum, and find down to the mouth.
And that which is necessary to be emitted from blood is 6 ounces, and perhaps you may emit more or less from it. The whole thing according to the quantity that appears to you from the strength of the patient and the filling of his artery.
Then examine the wound. Then if something remains at the mouth from the stitch, then cut it so that a warm abscess does not occur: then bind the wound with linen strips from a cloth.
Then care for him with an ointment until he is healed.
Of the extraction of arteries which are in temples.
Chapter III.
When an old hemicrania or acute catarrah occurs in someone due to acidic moistures and heat in the temples of the hands or feet, or old soda, which is pungent and similar to those: and it is treated with modes of medical cure, and not brought to a conclusion: then we are experienced in these afflictions by extracting arteries from the temples, or cauterizing them, as we have narrated.
And the method of performing the operation for extracting ears is, that the surgeon should shave off the hairs which are on the temples, and direct himself towards the apparent artery in the temple. For it is itself shown to you by its pulse, and rarely hidden except in a few men or in extreme cold.
But if it is hidden from you, then the surgeon should bind his patient's head with any cloth of his own; then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or moisten the place with hot water, until it appears to you in a manifest appearance. Then take the spatula that I shaped a little while ago. Then scrape with it the skin with ease until you reach the artery. Then place a hook in it and pull it out upwards until you pull it out of the skin, and make it slip out from the tunics that are under it on all sides.
If however the artery is slender: then wrap it around with the extremity of the hook. Then cut off from it that part according to the length of its extensions and contractions, and that no flow of blood may occur. For if it is not cut off, and if it is not severed, the blood will not ascend thoroughly.
Then evacuate six ounces of blood up to three.
For if the artery is large: then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with a doubled thread of strong silk or from the sinew of an antelope, so that it may not hasten to putrefaction before the wound is consolidated and a flow of blood occurs.
Then cut off the excess that is between the two ties. Do that at that hour or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cauterizing tool up to the mouth, with a culinary knife, until its ends are cut and it remains in the same place of the work.
And it is better, unless the patient has a fever, or a warm complexional constitution. Because the cautery is one of those things that are useful for drying out humors. Therefore it is better to be beneficial.
And after the removal of arteries, the place should be filled with old ashes; and fine lint should be placed on top.
And after the wound is cleansed, it should be treated with dry medicines that cause flesh to form, and with egg whites, until it heals.
But if while you are operating a flow of blood occurs from an artery, then hasten to cut it off with a cautery, or fill the place with zinc oxide: and bind your hand tight until the bleeding stops. But if none of those things is present to you, then place your finger on the spot until the blood coagulates. And dress the wound with water of very cold temperature, until the sharpness subsides and is tied as it should be.
And since it is easier and less painful to extract an artery, it is that you should cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives: let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to a thin thread. However, they are much less sharp than two knives. Because if they are sharp, like two knives, the cold hastens to both, and do not cut through flesh with speed. When therefore there is a little thickness in them, the heat of fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut through quickly; with this, then, it is better than all work, and easier, and more convenient.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space between the two knives be the thickness of a finger, after you mark out two places with encaustic; and take both hot ones away from each other, until they reach the mouth, and cut through the vein in two places: so that what is between its extremities may be separated. For they are not consolidated with this cautery thoroughly.
Chapter IV.
On the treatment of habitual and stubborn acute eye discharges.
When there are habitual discharges and their course is from the veins above the outer part of the head, and nothing seems to be done for them by medicine with medicines, and you see the patient's face already red, and they feel a sensation in their forehead as if it were the walking of ants: and their eyes are shriveled, moist, so that their eyelashes have already been consumed along with consumption; and their eyelids have become inflamed from the sharpness of the discharges. Then the ancients resorted to this treatment, and called it a cure with a knife.
And one is that you should command the patient to shave off the hairs that are on their forehead. Then cut three incisions on the forehead, equally spaced along the length of the forehead: and let the length of each incision be almost two fingers, and one incision should be equally distant from the middle of the nose on the forehead, and the second according to the length of a little more than the distance between the arms, and the third at the other side; and beware of cutting into the arteries which are on both sides: and stretch out your hand away from the connection of the two jawbones, and let the space of all incision be the length of three connected fingers: and prepare for yourself a piece of sponge or many dry cloths with which you will dry the blood.
Then insert the spatula into the two sharp edges at the ends, whose form is as follows. From the incision that is at the side of the temple to the second incision: and scrape away with it all the skin that is between the two incisions, along with the scab that is above the bone. Then make that also from the middle incision up to the second incision. Then take out this spatula-like thing, and insert another instrument into the first incision again, which is called a sharp knife, blunt on one side, not sharp on the other. And this is its shape.
And place the sharp side of it towards the attached skin with the flesh, and the blunt side towards the bone; and push it until it reaches the middle fissure; and cut off with it all the fatty tissues that descend from the head to the eyes: except for what remains in the incision up to where it appears on the skin.
Then make that also in the middle fissure up to another fissure.
And after an equal amount of blood has flowed out, express the areas from the congealed blood clots.
Then place a linen cloth from old cotton into every fissure, and put over them poultices soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And take care that no warm abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, untie the bandage, and administer a great many infusions with warm water. Then take care of that with balsam ointment and the other things by which wounds are healed, until it is cured.
Chapter 5.
Of the cure for sharp tears and catarrhs to the eyes from internal head afflictions.
When someone is afflicted with sharp, severe, and constant catarrhs, and you see their eyes are sunken, small, and already weakened in appearance; and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall out from them, and there is a sharp, piercing pain deep within the head, causing suffering and subsequent sneezing: then take action against these afflictions, those materials which do not come forth except through many veins.
And the better cure for them is this. And it is, that you should order the ailing person to shave his forehead. Then cut into one groove in the middle of the forehead, or another slightly narrower on either side, and start with the incision from the left temporal to the right temporal area: and make the incision up to the bone. And stretch out your hand away from the temporal bones which move during mastication, until when the bone is exposed, and dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the incision with a hot cautery, or with linen strips soaked in lard. Then tie it on top with wool threads; and soak the wool threads with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you loosen it and see that the hot abscess is already subsiding: then it is necessary to burn the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then care for it with a regimen of cauterization that causes flesh to grow: just as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and prepare an ointment from them, and administer it to the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Of the treatment for that which falls on the Ear.
All that falls into the Ear is of one or four kinds: either a mineral stone, or like a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as beans and peas; or it is a flowing substance, such as water, vinegar, and similar things; or some animal.
When then something falls into the ear that is a stone or a type of stone which does not accumulate or grow inside the ear, simply expose the ear to air. But if you see a stone, then distill something from violet oil or sassafras into the ear. Then administer the extraction of it, and cause to sneeze with condiment, and plug the nostrils at the advent of sneezing, after you put a wad of cloth or wool in the ear canal and stretch the ear upwards.
For this treatment often issues forth.
But if it does not issue forth, then administer the extraction of it with gestures, that is, with small pizzicaroli; whose form is this.
So if it issues forth with gestures, it is good.
And if not, then administer the extraction of it with a small blind hook of slight duplication.
So if it does not issue forth through that, then make a catheter from air, and insert the end of the catheter into the ear canal far in, and plug what is inside the catheter's circumference with a soft wax candle mixed with oil, so that there is no passage except for the catheter: then draw out with your strong wind attraction.
For this often issues forth as we have narrated. But if it does not issue forth in this way: then take from the helkalimbat, or from the veso, that is, prepared glue with which birds are caught, a moderate amount; and place it at the end of the ear canal after you have wrapped a decent-sized cloth around it; then insert it into the ear opening with ease, after you have dried the ear of moisture.
But if it does not issue forth with all that we have narrated, then hasten to divide it, before a hot abscess or spasms occur.
And the method of incision is, that you should make a small wound first in the cephalic part, and then draw out from it according to the measure of its strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn their ear upwards, and find a small wound in the root of the ear at the fatty part of it in the depressed place itself: and let the wound be of a lunar shape until you reach the stone; then take out the stone along with what is possible to you from the instruments.
Then close up the wound with speed from your own hour and cure it, until it is healed.
If something falls into the ear that grows and swells from one grain of wheat: then perform an extraction of it.
But if it does not yield to you at the exit, then take a thin, subtle spatula, according to this shape.
And administer with it the removal of that type of grain from the grains that fall into the ear, and do not do it when you think that grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you place many poultices: then remove them with a hook or with a thin probe or by suction, as we said, for the removal of it will be facilitated.
However, when the water enters the ear, it is necessary for the patient to use gentle blowing with condiments first; and now he has filled his ear with a little cooked old fat; and he lies on top of that ear where there is water. If it then comes out through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many small stones, according to the length of a finger, thin and soft, and warm them with fire for a little while, and the patient should insert one of them into the ear canal of his own ear; and let him sit on one foot from that side, and strike another stone against the stone that is in the ear. And therefore do not cease to do that with stone and stone, until all the water comes out.
And now that the water comes out so, you take a papyrus or a reed, and insert one end of it into the ear; and light the other end with fire, until most of it is burned. Then take another reed and do that many times, until all the water comes out; and remove it with a cannula, as was previously mentioned in the stone.
In extracting an animal that has entered the body, if it is a small creature like a flea and similar to one, treat it as I said in division.
But if it is a large creature of the body that appears to the senses: then administer its extraction with a gesture and a hook; and its outcome is easier for all those held in the ear.
In extracting a worm that has been generated in the ear, when you treat it as we said in division and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then you must look at the ear to the sun. If therefore something appears there from worms, then extract it with a gesture or with fine hooks.
But if nothing appears there, then take a cannula, of this shape, constricted below, broad above; and insert the thin end into the ear according to the quantity that an invalid can tolerate it: then suck with it using strong suction. Do that many times until all the worms go out.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then smear the rim of the cannula with wax, as I said in stone.
But if they do not come out with what we said: then administer the distillations which ancient ones have experienced in the destruction of worms; and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your flow be as when you pour oil and medicines into the ear with this instrument, whose shape is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver or bronze, constricted below, with a small hole, broad above.
So let the pushing thing that is in the concavity of the cannula be made of bronze well done. And if you wish, take a thread and wrap it around the end of it with a decent wrapping: then pour oil, or sap, or what you like from those medicines into the cannula: and let it be in the ear: then insert the thread with the wrapped part above, and express it with your hand compression moderated, until the oil is pushed into the concavity of hearing, and the invalid feels it inside: and that which is poured into the ear should now be warmed with fire for a little while: and beware lest what is distilled in it be very cold: medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
About the care for accidental injury of gold.
Sometimes, certain infants emerge from their mothers' wombs not perforated.
And it also happens to some people that a piece of flesh is torn out from the ear due to a wound or born with it.
And this piece of flesh sometimes forms in the deep hole of the ear which is not visible; and sometimes it occurs near the outside of the hole and is visible.
Therefore, that which occurs in the deep ear and is not comprehended by sight is difficult to heal in many cases.
And for that which is visible, it is necessary to place the ear of the injured person in the sun and look at it. Then, if you see the piece of flesh appearing, open it with a small, thin spatula whose shape is as follows: its end should be slightly indented, with some part being sharp; and the rest of the spatula should be smooth on both sides, so that it does not hurt the ear.
If the piece of flesh is born with the ear, then take it with a small hook and cut it off with great ease until all the flesh is removed.
What if the piece of flesh is in the deep ear? Then take a thin, smooth reed and warm it with fire for a little while. Then put it into the ear.
If you feel the piece of flesh with that which the patient finds with heaviness of hearing, try to open it gently and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place a lint dressing in the ear according to the size of the hole that you wrapped with Egyptian ointment. Make it last for several days until you are certain of the wound's consolidation.
Or take lint and soak it in rose water, then powder zege over it, and use it.
If you see the ear already suppurating: then it is necessary to remove the linen, and let it be wrapped with another linen that has been dipped in a mixture made with cerate mixed with rose oil, until the supuration cools down: then take care of it until it heals.
But if a flow of blood occurs: then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it over the ear: and apply the remaining treatment which stops the flow of blood as described in the treatise on distillations.
The cure for Althel's eyelids.
Chapter 8.
These Altheli which occur on the eyelids of the eyes, are sometimes moist and sometimes dry.
Therefore, you should remove them with tweezers or a forceps, and cut them off at the roots. Then place powdered zinc on the affected areas.
But if blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lenticular cautery.
And it is better to cauterize them after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, the roots are burned away and do not return.
The cure for eyelid granulations of the eye.
Chapter 9.
Many times something occurs on the eyelids which is similar to a granulation in its strength and toughness, and because of this it is named Granum: and it is an accumulation of thick moisture on the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore, the operation is to examine whether it is a Granum that proceeds from it, which appears on the eyelid and moves in all directions, and its nature is easy.
Find therefore a split or incision over it along its length. Then strip it from every part with ease, until it comes out. Then hang it with a hook, and cut it off.
But if the cutting of it is not prepared for you, except after you have pierced the eyelid with a slight incision, then it may cause harm to the invalid.
But if the wound is great: then gather it together with a suture and take care of it until it heals.
And if it's small, no evil will befall you from it. For ointment restores it and fleshes it out.
And if the stone is inclined towards the inner part of the ear, towards the internal surface, then turn back the eyelid and hang the stone with a hook; besides which you will need an incision, and press it from every part.
For if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, it does not cause harm to anything.
Then wash the eye after the stone's incision, with salted water; and take care of the place with what fleshes it out, until the invalid is healed.
About the cure of Axirnath eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is a fatness that is generated in the eyelids of the upper eye lid; and it happens to children most of all: and it weighs down their eyes, and occasions them to have cataracts in the mornings, and they cannot gaze at the light of the sun, therefore, because tears press towards them, and for this reason you see them lying down on their faces always, and sleeping on them, and their eyelids are under their eyebrows damp, which has already been covered by swelling: and the Axirnath itself is indeed visible to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel the Axirnath between them.
And the method of operating on it is, that the invalid puts his head in your bosom.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare it with oil of linden, and make from it an ointment circle according to the quantity that contains Axirnath from all sides. Then place it on top of it and press with your fingers all around, so that the Axirnath may gather in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture with a spatula incidentally splitting fissures by width, and let not that fissure be greater which is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary for you to split the skin entirely, until you reach the Axirnath, for indeed Axirnath proceeds from the fissure according to the shape of white fatty excrescence.
Therefore extract it with the cloth which you had inserted between your fingers at the exterior: and turn your hand to the right and left until it disperses: and beware, lest you add to the fissure, so that you do not come with the spatula to the eye and hurt it.
But if Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, then add a little to the fissure with ease, until Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then immerse the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them over the spot, and bind it with wool.
And there are men who rub salt, and place it in the concavity of the fissure, so that what remains of that moisture may dissolve.
Then take care of it, until it heals.
But if an ulcerated sore occurs on the spot, then care for it with soothing poultices, until it heals.
Of the methods of Ataxmir for the eyes, that is, when the eyelid becomes soft and the eye is inflamed, so that it is red as if it should not be.
Here is the translation:
When eyebrows grow excess hairs that are added besides the natural course under natural hairs, and they age: then they harm the eye, and cause various kinds of afflictions to occur, such as constant tearing, softening of the eyelids, and a white haze, until it leads to the destruction of the eye.
And indeed the eyesight is cured according to four modes. Or by cauterization with fire. Or with acute medicine, according to what has been set forth in the chapter on cauterization. Or Ataxmir is cured by incision and stitching. Or by cannulas, according to what I shall say.
It is necessary that you place the head of the patient on your shoulder. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If it is turned, it is good. And if not: insert the needle into it, where the thread is from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle pierce through with the thread above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid, where hairs grow: and pull the thread up to the upper part with the eyelid, and turn it with the finger.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid, under the added hairs, with a spatula coming from the larger tear duct down to the smaller one.
Then pull out the thread and place a small poultice made of cloth or linen under the eyelid; then mark on top of the eyelid with an encaustum, so that it is like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the figure be made according to the size and elevation of the eyelid; for that already differs among people.
For some of these people are those who need the quantity to be cut from the eyelid which is excessive and softens the eyelid.
Then when you find the spot on the skin above two lines which you marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller one, and let there be a split one near the natural hairs, as is the thickness of the radius.
Then insert the hook into one of the two corners of the skin. Then scrape off the whole thing. Then gather the edges with a suture, using a needle and fine wool thread; and wipe away the blood, and make what remains from the threads stick to the two eyebrows, with certain substances applied, if you wish to do so; and if not, don't bother.
Then let go of the suture and thread for three days or four: afterwards take care of it.
And if you wish, let the wound heal without a suture, and care for it with something that dries and tightens. For the eyelid is raised in cases of cicatrization and its gathering; but it will be better in the future.
The ancient people taught this method of treating wounds.
However, there is concern for the patient who is weak, and it comes from good workmanship, and there is no fear.
And another method is also in Ataxmir: and it is, to mark signs on the eyelid in the shape of a figure like that of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then lift the eyelid with three separate or gathered hooks, according to this shape which follows; then cut off what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this shape, with equal cutting.
But if it is not possible for you to hold the hook and it does not match that; then take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the figure: and insert the thread one near the larger tear duct, and the second near the smaller tear duct: and gather between your fingers the threads with equality.
Then lift up your hand with an equal elevation; and lift up the skin over that which is marked out, as we have narrated.
Then gather together the two edges of the wound with a suture; and take care of it until it heals.
If, however, an abscess should occur at your incision or your cut, cover that abscess with cerate and similar substances, such as ointments soothing to the skin.
And sometimes it happens that the eyelids also turn outward, so that its hairs are turned in the opposite direction.
Therefore you should use what we said about incision on it, but with sutures it is more artificial. Of the care of Ataxmir with reeds.
And so, you will turn back the eyelid and cut through the cleft that is inside, as I narrated. Then make two reeds or two thin sticks by art, whose length will be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width will be less than the width of a spatula: and you have already cut at the extremities on both sides from both parts where you hold the threads.
Then gather both of them with subtlety above what protrudes from the eyelid of the eye, and tie the two reeds tightly on both sides with a strong tightening, and let it be for several days. For a tight skin will die, and become blackened, and rotten, until it falls off by itself.
If then it is delayed, then cut it with forceps. Then take care of it until it heals.
When it has consolidated, the eyelid will be lifted up: and the hairs do not sting.
And this is the form of two reeds which you will make according to this shape and quantity in length and width.

Of the elevation of stinging hairs in the eye with a needle.
Operation is this: you take a fine needle and insert into it a thin silk thread, smooth. Then gather up its ends and tie both with a very fine knot: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then compose another fine thread under it in a short space, and tie up its ends if you wish.
Then the patient should place his head on your lap, and let it be near the sun, that the operation may be revealed: for this is a subtle or minute operation.
Then insert the needle with the thread into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate quickly into the eyelid until you pull it out above a natural hair.
Then pull the hair up to the thread: and insert into the thread that hair, if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair comes out with the thread into the eyelid along with a natural hair.
But if the thread is pulled and the hair does not come out with it: pull the thread downwards with the thread that you composed in it, until the thread comes out from the upper opening, and you will see it with your own eyes: then indeed pull the thread and the filament; and extract both, and shut the eye; and let it be closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is short, then either join a long hair from natural hairs to it, and lift it up with it; or leave it alone until it lengthens after some time, and then lift it.
XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to an eye, it is called Leporina.
And this is Al-Khatarat, either natural or accidental.
Therefore it is accidental, arising from the closure of a wound, or a section, or a cauterization, and similar things to those.
And the method of treatment for it is that you find this closure, and separate its lips, and place a linen cloth between them, and bind it until it heals.
And it is not necessary to use things that dry and constrict for its treatment, for if you do that, Al-Khatarat will worsen than it was. Instead, use things that soften, such as linseed: and mix it with water in which althea seeds have been cooked; and apply a balm of dyaquilon, which has been mixed with some oil; and wrap it with a linen cloth, and care for it with this.
And your treasure of treatment is to strive with all one's ingenuity so that it may not be consolidated, according to its original shape.
And this operation which we have described is nothing but a correction of Al-Khatarat so that it does not return to its original shape entirely.
About the treatment of Al-Khatarat which occurs in the lower eyelid and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Al-Khatarat which occurs below is what is truly called Al-Khatarat. And it is both natural and accidental.
And it is accidental, arising from a wound, or a fissure, or a cauterization, and similar things to those.
And the method of treatment for it is that you take a needle with a thread double, and wet it in flesh, and make it penetrate from the left lachrymal to the right lachrymal, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with a needle, and cut it with a wide spatula. If therefore the figure of the eyebrow is restored as it should be, that is good.
And if not, take a reed, and place it in the cleft, and turn with it the eyebrow; and make two clefts on the inner side of the eyebrow, and let the ends of the two clefts be at the two angles of the incision, until they meet and form an angle: until when they are gathered together, this figure will result, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by as much as its lower acute side is below the eyebrow. Then gather the separated parts: or sew it with a thread of wool.
Then care for them with what we said about softening medicines and liquid ones, until they heal.
And if the flesh becomes inflamed from the cleft or suture or cautery: then it is necessary to make a flat incision beneath the hairs of the eyelids, even as before. Then separate the space between two lips with a liquid substance, according to what we said.
And the summary of the speech about treating Alxatrati is this: when it occurs above or below, is that the operation should run in it according to how it is prepared from the form of Alxatrati.
For it itself is a many-colored thing in form: and the skilled craftsman prepares his skill by whatever means are possible to him, until he makes the figure return to its natural shape or nearly so, and even himself weakens according to how it suits him from the operation and instruments in many dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the eye with conjunctiva or cornea.
At times this consolidation occurs in many people, so that the upper eyelid becomes consolidated with the white of the eye and prevents it from performing its natural functions.
Therefore, you should insert the tip of the radius under the eyelid and lift it up: or extend it with a hook: then cut away the adhesion with a thin spatula, but not so sharp as to cause harm to the sick person during the operation. Rather, let the spatula be slightly dull for a little while, so that the patient is not disturbed by the procedure, and you remove the spatula, and do not injure the eye with its sharpness: and your incision should be like excising that adhesion with ease, until the eyelid returns to its natural shape and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, washing it clean with water: or let it drip onto it from a piece of sief-made, dissolved with water.
Then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen cloth: and place over the eye a woolen cloth infused with lamb's white: and after three days, use sief, and with solidified substances, until it heals.
About the cutting of the nail and the excrescence of flesh tears.
Chapter XVI.
Know that a nail is according to two modes. Or it is nerve-like: and is similar to a piece of sief, hard and subtle. Or it is not nerve-like, similar to frozen white moisture: which when touched by iron, or is grasped with a hook, is cut off, and the hook does not stick into it.
And both kinds do not begin, unless from the larger tear, until it gradually goes away, until it covers the sight, and prevents light, and movement of the eye.
And the method for cutting it is: that the patient places his head on your shoulder. Then open his eyes and lift up his eyelid with your hand. Then grasp the nail with a hook of moderate thickness, and stretch it upwards. Then take an awl and insert a bristle from the mane of a horse, or ox, or goat: and let the thread be strong, and double the extremity of the awl for a little while, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and pierce it with the awl, and tie it with the thread the nail, and stretch it upwards: and scrape off with a bristle the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you were sewing it with a bristle up to its posterior.
Then cut away the remaining part at the root of the tear duct with a fine forceps or small scissors: and let the natural flesh of the tear duct be left, so that no continuous flow of tears may occur as a result of the incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone, or with a thread, as we said. Then scrape off with a gentle forceps whose shape this is.
And beware that the forceps does not touch the horned tunica, lest there be a tear and the pupil is perforated.
Then after the incision, throw something crushed salt into the eye, or made from juniper, and let it remain until another day. Then care for it with what is needed until it heals.
But if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert an awl into it, nor does a hook fit into it, then this kind of nail is often cured with medicines, and heals as I narrated in the division.
But if you wish to cut it, then open the eye of the patient and take a fine, gentle, blunt forceps, with this shape. And scrape the nail above with smoothness and subtlety. And if you see that it is rarefied and broken off, and leaves an impression on it, then drip from your hour onto the eye made with juniper or red powder, or crushed salt: and close the eye until another day.
Then proceed over that same operation until everything is removed from it. Unless the opposite eye has a hot abscess: then indeed leave that one alone and cure the abscess until it heals.
Then proceed with the operation on that one with caution, until it is healed.
However, if the prominence of the eyelid flesh causes great harm to the eye, it should be suspended with a hook and cut off from that part; and do not make the incision too long so that the flow of tears does not occur.
Then treat the tear duct whether it is red or made from zinc, until it heals.
Of the incision of Alguardenic and the flesh born in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
It sometimes happens in certain people's eyes that red flesh is formed, either composed or superimposed, until it covers the pupil, approaches, or stretches over the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles a rose bush.
And the operation in that case is also to have the weak person place their head in your bosom. Then open his eyes and gather up that whole flesh with suitable hooks; and hold it with one forceps or instrument.
Then cut the flesh first, until the entire thing is finished with a cutting tool. And beware of the eye so that you do not harm it during the operation; and let your cut be such as you make it, or with a spatula, as we narrated in the cutting of the nail: or with small forceps, with which the abscess is gathered, according to how its shape will come after this chapter.
When therefore your cutting is complete and the Alguardenic is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt: or distill into it red powder, and similar things from corrosive medicines; and place a covering of wool with egg white on top of the eye to secure it from hot abscess.
If something remains from the almond gall and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess; then let go of the eye and care for it with that which soothes the abscess.
Then proceed over it with an operation until it is healed.
And do the same with added flesh that occurs in the eye of this kind.
However, it is necessary that you let others treat a weak eye from a natural or accidental cause by means of iron, and do not insert your hand into any of these operations until you see that the member can tolerate that treatment.
Of the Collection of Pus from the Eye.
Chapter 18.
Pus are red veins that form over the eye and prevent their own operation; and they weaken the eye with the passage of days.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider whether the eye that has pus is strong and not troubled by any illness other than the pus; then collect its pus.
And it is necessary for you to instruct the weak one to place their head on your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one or two hooks, according to your discretion; and the hook should be thin and of double plaiting, in this shape, or there should be two hooks gradually arranged in one body, in this shape.
Then collect those veins with fine forceps, with subtlety, and wash away the blood an hour after the hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye and dissolved; and beware not to injure the eye with the sharp edges.
And let your operation be at noon before the sun, and be very firm in your operation so that you do not cut anything but those veins.
And when you have finished, then pour into the eye whether green or red, so that its sharpness may corrode what remains of the pus.
If it is not possible for you to gather all of that in one hour, then bind the eye with a poultice that soothes the pain of the eye: and let him be for some days until the pain subsides: and keep it safe from warm apostema. Then go back over it in the same way until he is healed.
And this is the form of a forceps.
About reducing an arrow to the nose.
Chapter 19.
The doctor of the arrow wound, which is treated with a cauter or a burning medicine that cuts sharply, according to what has been told beforehand.
And if it does not heal, then there is no talent in it: unless you lay something over the suppurating abscess at its birth and remove all the moisture that is in it and pus, until the mouth is uncovered.
When therefore the mouth is uncovered, and you see in it a corruption or blackness, then scrape it away with an instrument whose form is this.
And it is called Scapocephalus.
Make it from iron hardened in the fire, and let its head be rounded like an asher, which is pierced with a fine file point or a delicate file point: and place it over the corrupted bone site.
Then turn it back between your fingers and press your hand on it for a moment, until you know that the corruption has already been scraped away: and do this several times.
Then restore the place with medicinal astringents and styptics.
If therefore the place is consolidated and flesh grows in it, and the course of healing runs its natural course: and remains for forty days, and does not renew itself, nor suppurate, nor any accident befall it: then you will know that it has already been healed.
And if not, then there is no talent in it, unless you reduce the abscess to the nasal opening, according to this form.
And it is, that you remove from bone second with iron, or with sharp medicine: and when the bone is detected, then take a chisel second in this form, having an iron extremity, triangular; and the wood of it be a narrowed pyramid down to the extremity, as you see.
Then place that on the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct; and extend your hand a little from the eye. Then revolve your hand with the chisel, until it penetrates the bone, and feels weak by it; because it finds air to come out of it, when he holds his hand above his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh grows and the place hardens: then the matter that was flowing outward returns to the nose: so it is lighter on the sufferer.
Paragraph on the Reduction of an Elevation of the Eye.
Chapter 20.
When the Eye protrudes with its entirety and does not cause harm or diminution to vision: then it is necessary to release the sufferer: afterwards bleed him: afterward place poultices on his neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place a liniment above the Eye that is made from acacia, and aloe, and galbanum, and sarcocola. Then bind the Eye on top with a plaster having many wool fibers.
And the method of wool fibers is: take soft cloths, many, and double each cloth with four of them; make many from them; then place one on top of another, according to the size needed by the Eye; then bind above them with a strong strap.
And repeat poultices with suction without scarification.
Then remove the poultices and leave the eye bound day and night.
If however the ligament becomes soft again, then it is necessary to tighten it much. Then remove the ligament after day and night, just as we said.
If then you see the eye has returned to its skin layer, that is good. And if not, then apply another poultice, and swellings, and tightening, and windings, until it heals.
Chapter on Cutting the Skin Layer of the Eye.
Chapter 21.
When a disruption occurs in the skin layer of the eye, then it protrudes and proceeds outside the eyelids like an eye granule, and disfigures the human form because of it, and one wants to cut it out: then the operation is in that, according to what I will say: and it is, to insert a needle into the root of the eye from the lower part to the upper part.
Then insert another needle into which there is a double thread from the tear-duct side, and make it penetrate; and let the first needle follow its own course.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the eye around the needle; and tighten it much.
Then pull out the needle, and place a thread soaked in the whites of an egg over the eye; and leave it until the thread falls off with the skin.
Then care for the eye with that, which comforts it, until the wound heals.
And sometimes tighten the skin of the eye all around with a bandage of cloth, until the eye becomes greatly prominent.
Then perforate it with a thin spatula that reaches to the depth of the eye, and let the whitish humor flow, and let the eye go down, and enter immediately; then tighten it until it heals.
Chapter on Curing an Alconmat.
Chapter 22.
This affliction which is called an Alconmat is not anything except a discharge that gathers in the eye, similar to water flowing down, and it is not itself.
And the method of operation in that case is, that you make the patient sit on a prepared seat or a direct one.
Then take his head in your hands from both sides, and move it until you see blood reaching the lower eyelids with your eyes. Then it is fixed and does not descend, and the light is blocked: and let the patient see things as he used to see them; and if it does not go down below, you will know that it is water.
But if it is not prepared to descend with the bleeding as we said, then make the patient sit between your hands. Then take a thin splinter and insert it with him above the fatty fold for a short time at the junction of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic on the crown, until the bleeding goes out.
When it has gone out, then pour warm water into the eye that you mixed with honey: or water in which you have boiled Greek hay and honey: then take care of him with the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
About the cure for water that descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter 23.
Now we have spoken of the kind of water for dividing and what is suitable for piercing, as explained and demonstrated by speech; then take that same thing according to health.
Then it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands square, opposite to the light, near the sun; and bind his eye with a bandage, and pull him tightly; then cover the eyelid of his eye with your right hand if the water-filled eye is on the left: or with your left hand if the eye is on the right.
Then take your right hand as a bandage if the eye is left: or your left hand as a bandage if the eye is right.
Then place the end of the bandage near the crown at the thickness of the radius on the apparent whiteness of the small tear gland of the eye; then push the bandage with force: and you revolve with him your hand, until it penetrates into the white of the eye: and feel that the bandage has already reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that there be a measure of the path to the depth, a measure of space from the pupil to the end of blackness: and this is the crown of the eye. You will see these things in the very vision of the eye itself, on account of the brightness of the corneal tunic.
Then place the almagda up to the point where there is water. Then press it downwards with one stroke after another.
If therefore the water flows down immediately, then the invalid will see that thing which opens his vision immediately, and the almagda is in his eye.
Then rest a little while. If therefore the water flows down, then remove it secondly, except that the almagda goes out.
When therefore it is fixed and does not rise: then extract the almagda with ease: and you revolve your hand slowly.
Then dissolve something from clear salt of a gem in water: and wash the eye inwardly with it. Then place all of this stuff outside the eye, or wool infused with rose oil and egg whites: and bind it to a healthy eye.
But we, and those who are of our time, place upon it pounded cinnamon with egg white.
And if the almagda does not obey you at all in entering the eye because of its hardness, since it is made by men whose eyes are very hard: then it is necessary that you take a spatula-like thing which is called Alberid, and this is its shape.
Therefore join it to him with a connecting piece just so much, and do not put your hand too far into the opening: and it is only necessary that you make a way for the almagda to its subtle place.
Then introduce the almagda, as we said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare for the invalid a well-made bedchamber, in which he will sleep on his back in a dark room; and let him be prevented from all movements, and from coughing: and give him that food which soothes his nature: and do not allow him to move his head at all to the right or left.
And let it be a ligament according to its own arrangement until the third day.
Then loosen that in that dark house and test the appearance of it: and make him see things. Then reduce the ligament to its original state until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting for him to do that at the hour of healing or immediately after the incision: rather, let someone else do it: because water rises quickly through a violent appearance.
But if a warm abscess occurs: then it is fitting that you loosen the eye before the seventh day: and straighten it with what calms the abscess, until it is calmed: then release his sight, and place over his face an oral device by which his vision may be regulated for several days, while he remains in that dark house.
Then let him go out from the house gradually and turn back to his own tents.
And know that in greenery a student is not excused from sight several times: then indeed he may venture upon the operation.
And now indeed one has come to us from those who are of Alayrach, who said that in Alayrach a perforated Magdan was made, through which water is sipped.
But I did not see anyone on your land who did that: nor do I read it in any of the old books: and it is possible that it may be new.
And these are forms of species from Magdan, so that they stand over those who do not know them.
Let them be made from bronze properly: and let their extremities be with this same subtlety triangular, and the acute end.
Chapter XXIV.
Â¶ About the care of a nasal polyp.
Sometimes there arise in the nose diverse added flesh, from which is something that resembles a scorpion with many feet: and from it is the cancerous, stony, reddish flesh; and from it is what is soft, not reddish.
So then, on the person who has a wound from these meats that is gentle, not deceitful, nor cancerous, it is necessary to make the patient sit between your hands opposite the sun, and open their nostrils, and put a hook in those fleshy parts: then draw them outwards, and cut off what you have taken with a thin, sharp scalpel from one side, until you know that all the flesh has been removed.
And if something remains of which removal is not possible: then scrape that off with one of the fine instruments with ease, until nothing remains from it. But if blood rules over you, or a warm abscess occurs: then place what is needed opposite it; or if it is from malicious abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it, until the blood ceases and all flesh is removed.
Then a mixture of vinegar, water, or wine should be poured into the nose after the incision. If however the nose opens and moisture proceeds from it all the way to the palate, then you will know that he is already healthy.
If however moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you will know that within him is flesh that has grown in the upper bones of the nose, to which the cutting instrument did not reach.
Then it is necessary that you insert a linen thread with some thickness and tie many knots in it: and place between every knot the length of a finger or less: and let the patient, as he will, put one end of the thread into his nose with a reed or whatever means possible for him after he has done so similarly to ease: and draw out his mucus, until it reaches the throat and comes out over his tongue.
And many people indeed do this similar to that young boy Attomus in schools: and it is an easy thing for one who wants.
Then place two ends of the thread, one that goes out above the nose and the other that remains on the nose. Then tie the thread around the flesh with knots which are found in the thread. Do that until you know that the flesh is now tied with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread and put it in the nose after bleeding has stopped, with the licorice that you have soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more, until the ointment has seeped into all that remains of the flesh.
Then put a lead tube at the end of it in the nose, for several days, until it heals.
But if it needs drying treatment, use that one.
To make a cupping instrument for the head, by which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make one from silver or bronze, which is similar to a small lamp, open like a shell, and its channel similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed tube, like a reed.
And the bowl of the instrument for cupping should be full and open, with a handle at its end, by which you will hold it when you heat oil or what you want from resin and flowing substances in it.
Chapter 25.
A boil often arises at the end of the nose and grows, increasing with days, until its appearance becomes foul.
And because of this, it is necessary to cut off that boil at the beginning of its appearance and root out all of it. Then place something over the spot, or a cautery, or a medicine that burns where it is.
If then the cutting off of it is delayed until it grows large, then consider whether it is hard, rough, and has a dull color, with little sensation; then do not touch it with iron, because it is a cancerous abscess.
I have often seen someone who had cut open an abscess on himself and as a result suffered great harm to himself.
And if the abscess is a gentle touch, not of deep color, and you see that cutting it open is possible throughout its entire extent, then empty the patient, and cut into it without fear, without anxiety: and care for the place with what cleanses and constricts, until he is healed.
Book 26.
On the Suture of the Nose, Lip, and Ear.
You should know that when there is a separation of continuity in one of these cartilages, this operation is not very helpful unless it occurs in some people.
Therefore, when something happens to someone from that, you should consider whether it is a fresh wound with its own blood. And then gather the edges of the wound with a suture, and care for it until he is healed.
And if the separation of continuity has already occurred and each part is now healed, then you should remove all visible parts from their skin until it bleeds. Then gather the two lips together with a suture and tie them up, and sprinkle superimposed on it dragon's blood powder and powdered olibanum: and place above the powders a piece of diaphanic plaster or another consolidated plaster, and let him be bound for two days or three. Then loosen it and change the medicine, and let him go until the threads fall off by themselves. Then care for him with an ointment until he is healed.
The method of suturing is to gather the separation of continuity either with hooks, as we have described in the suturing of the belly: or to gather the separation with a thread, as I have made you know.
Book 27.
On the Extraction of Lip Knots.
To many people indeed, swellings similar to grains of herbs occur in the inner parts of their lips, some being smaller and others being larger.
Therefore, it is necessary to turn the lip, and search over every knot of it, and suspend it with a hook, and cut it from all parts.
Then fill the place after the incision with powdered papyrus, until the blood is stopped.
Then apply vinegar to it and treat the place where there is inflammation, until the wounds are healed.
Book XXVIII.
On the incision of the flesh growing over the gum.
Much flesh grows above the gum, which the ancients called abuluss.
Therefore, it is necessary to suspend it with a hook or hold it with forceps and cut it at its root, and allow the pus or blood to flow.
Then place powdered papyrus or other astringent powders on the spot.
For if that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does return, then cut it again and cauterize it; it will not return after cauterization.
Book Twenty-Nine.
On the filing of Teeth with Iron.
Sometimes they gather on the surfaces of teeth from both inner and outer parts, and between the gums rough, foul, and yellowish crusts form; sometimes they are worn away, and become bright green, until that corruption reaches the gum, and the teeth are made foul by it.
Therefore, it is necessary to make the patient sit between your hands, and his head in the hollow of your shoulder, and file and trim the tooth in which you see crusts resembling sand; until nothing remains of them, and similarly do this for blackness, greenishness, and yellowness, and other things until they are cleaned.
If then what is in them is taken away from the beginning of reason, it is good: and if not, then iterate over them a reason on another day, the second, and third, until finally reaches the study in that which you wish.
And know that the tooth needs razors of diverse forms, many figures: according to how you prepare your operation: therefore, because the razor with which you scrape away the tooth from the lower part is one thing, and the razor with which you scrape between teeth is another, in other forms.
And those forms of razors should be prepared by you, as you see them.
Chapter 30.
Of the extraction of toothaches.
You should cure the tooth from its pain with all your skill and delay in removing it. For it is not as if its place can be filled when it is removed: since it is a high substance: until then, when there is no excuse for removing it, then you should remove it when the weak person is compelled to do so, in order that he may be strengthened and assured before you about the painful tooth.
For indeed many times the pain deceives the weak person and makes him think that it is in the toothache and removes it: then the pain does not cease until the tooth is removed from the weak person.
For we have already seen this from the operation of bloodletters.
When then the painful tooth itself is certified to you, it is necessary that scarifices be made around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is something of power, until the gum is loosened from all parts: then move it with your fingers, or with fine forceps at first slowly, until you shake it.
Then place the large forceps in a good location on it, and have the head of the weak person between your knees, which you have already held firmly so that it does not move: then pull out the tooth according to its straightness, so as not to break it.
If therefore it does not emerge, then take one of those instruments and introduce it under him from all parts with ease, and try to move it as you did at first.
But if the tooth is perforated or corroded: then you must fill that hole with cloth and tie it with the end of a thin reed so that it does not break during your constriction upon it with forceps.
And you must be careful to cut with scarification all around the gum from every side a great deal.
And guard your strength so that you do not break it and a part of it remains, and return to the suffering person from the greater evil which is more painful at first.
And beware that you do not do what ignorant cuppers do in their haste and the boldness of theirs towards its removal, besides what we have narrated.
For they often cause harm to people's great infirmities, which are more difficult to break and for all the roots of it to remain, or some of them; or for them to extract it with certain bones of the jawbone: as we have testified many times about this.
Then it should be washed after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
What if bleeding occurs from the places (which often happens) then take something from alum and fill it with that in the place; and if not, cauterize it if alum does not seem useful to you.
The forms of fine forceps with which the tooth is moved at first should be long on their extremities, short on their handles, thick so that they do not bend when pressed upon a tooth: let them be like those you see of gross handles until when you tie them up, they do not bite themselves nor bend: with short extremities and made of tempered iron or well-made bronze, with straight extremities: and in their extremities should be teeth, some of which enter into others and tighten with a decent, strong constriction.
And sometimes they become extremities like the form of a bird's beak that is called a ciconia, and are strong constrictions.
Chapter 31.
On the removal of teeth, and the extraction of fractured jaw bones.
When something remains to be removed from a tooth that is broken, then you should place a poultice with butter on the spot for one or two days, until the area becomes soft; then introduce into it an algast or forceps, whose tips are like the form of a bird's beak that is called a ciconia. The shape however is that of a forceps.
Let the extremities of these be made like a file inside, or like a rasping instrument.
So if it does not yield to you at the exit with these forceps, then you should, after having cut away the surrounding tissue, and exposing all the flesh, insert a small instrument called Palanca, which has this form.
Let the short extremity be slightly thickened, and not be too wet, so that it may not break.
So if the root emerges through this, it is good; and if not, you should help with other instruments which have these forms.
The form of the first instrument, a triangular extremity, with some thickness to it.
The form of the triangular extremity is fine.
And with other instruments and tools that have preceded in the filing of teeth.
And also it helps with this instrument which is like a great hook, having this shape, a twisted triangular extremity, with some thickness to prevent breaking, and not too wet.
And know that the instruments of teeth are many: and similarly, other instruments do not count, and a skilled craftsman, subtle in his art, finds an instrument for himself according to what the operation demands and the illness itself.
Since there are illnesses that ancient physicians did not mention and for which they did not invent an instrument, on account of the diversity of those diseases.
But if the bone is broken from the jawbone or from other bones of the mouth, or it has putrefied: then examine over it in its place with that which corresponds to it from the instruments and forceps I mentioned for extracting roots, and help with gestures, whose form is this.
Let something of roughness be applied gradually to it, so that it may bind or hold with the bone, and not flee away and go, until the bone comes out: and restore the place with medicines corresponding to it.
But if there is a small putrefaction in the bone, then scrape away from its putridity and cure it, until it heals.
Chapter 32.
On the removal of prominent teeth above others.
When prominent teeth do not follow their natural course and become foul because of this shape; and especially when it happens to women and girls, namely those with venereal afflictions.
It is necessary therefore that you consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth and does not fit in place, or its removal or trimming is possible; then pull it out: and if it is connected to another tooth, then cut it off with this instrument, whose form is this.
It is like a small plow, and it is made from iron, very sharp at the end.
And let your cut be such that you cut it over many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another one from the teeth is not disturbed.
But if it is prominent and exposed to its trimming: then trim it with a file from iron, whose form is this.
Let it be all of iron, and the handle of its small points very sharp, like a file with which needles are made: with which the tooth is slowly filed over many days with ease, so that the tooth does not become disturbed and falls; then gently polish it, and finally scrape it off with some rasps.
If there is a tooth that breaks off and injures the tongue, then it is necessary to trim it also, until the roughness of this fracture is removed and smoothed out.
Chapter 33.
On the Replacement of Teeth with Golden Threads.
When the front teeth are shaken and disturbed by impact or fall, and an invalid cannot hold what he has eaten without it falling out; and after treating them with medicinal washes, the treatment does not take effect: then there is a skill in them to be fastened with golden thread: because silver becomes green and putrid after days: but gold is never changed.
And let the thread be of a middle size, fine yet not too thin, according to its quantity that it can fit between the teeth.
And the form of the retension is, that you take a thread, and insert double its length between two healthy teeth: then weave it with both ends of the thread between the moving teeth, whether one or more, until you reach the healthy tooth on the other side. Then iterate the weaving until you reach the part where you started: and tie your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it does not come loose at all: and let your constriction be such that you bind the thread around the roots of the teeth, so that it does not slip out.
Then cut off the superfluous ends of the thread with scissors: and gather both together, and convert them with a knot: and hide them between the healthy tooth and the moving tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue: then let it be left tightly bound until it remains.
But if it comes undone or is cut, bind them with another thread and they will remain thus for the whole time.
And this is the form of teeth, and the figure of the retention of two healthy teeth, and two moving teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one tooth or two returns to its place after a fall of each in their own locations, and remain as we described, and are retained; and this only happens through the skillful, prudent, and experienced artisan.
And sometimes a piece of bone from the shin fits together; and it forms into the shape of a tooth, and is placed in its original location, secured by the tooth and bound tightly, as we said: and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter 44.
Sometimes this membrane that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, resulting from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation is to open the mouth of the patient in your lap, and lift up his tongue; then cut that membrane-like ligament through its width, until the tongue is released from its constraint.
But if there is something hard and knotty in it, and that is due to the healing of a wound: then put a hook into it and find it through its width, until you cut the ligament and loosen the knot.
And beware that there is no tear in the depth of the flesh, and you do not cut an artery there, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then wash the incision with rose-water, or vinegar, or cold water.
Then put a linen cloth under the tongue, which the patient should wear every night: so that it does not consolidate again.
If however a flow of blood occurs, then put powdered vermilion over the place. If however the blood overflows, then cauterize the place with a lentil-like cautery that is suitable for it; then care for it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter 35.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue, similar to a small frog, which prevents the tongue from performing its natural function; and it may become so swollen that it fills the mouth.
And the operation in this case is to open the patient's mouth before the sun and examine the abscess.
If you see it to be dark-colored or black and hard, and the patient does not sense it; then do not touch it, because it is a tumor.
And if it is turned towards whiteness, and there is moisture in it; then throw a needle into it, and cut it with a thin scapel, and free it from all parts. What if blood flows from you during your operation hour; then place a powdered ligature on top of it, until the bleeding ceases: afterwards return to your operation and remove it with its contents.
Then bind it with vinegar and salt: and care for it with the remaining suitable treatment until it heals.
On the cure of abscesses of both tonsils.
Chapter 36.
Sometimes abscesses occur between the throat's glands, which are similar to those that occur outside and are called two tonsils; which when you cure with what we said earlier and they do not heal, then investigate.
If it is a hard abscess, dark-colored, with little sensation; then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red-colored and the root of it is thick: then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood; rather let it be, until it matures. And then either pierce it or let it burst by itself.
And if it is white-colored, rounded, and the root of it is thin: then you should cut it.
And the operation is as follows: before performing your own operation, consider whether the apostema of that person has cooled down completely or decreased to some extent. Then make the patient sit in front of the sun, and have his head resting on your shoulder; open his mouth, and let a servant receive it between their hands, and press his tongue downwards with an instrument, which is shaped as follows.
Let it be made of silver or bronze, so that it is as fine as a knife.
When you press the tongue with it and the apostema is shown to you, and falls upon it according to your sight: then take a hook and fix it in the amygdule, and pull it out as far as you can, except that you draw something from the tunics along with it.
Then cut it with an instrument, which has the following shape: It is similar to pincers: however, its extremities should be curved, so that the opening of each one is in a straight line with the other's tip: therefore take care that it is made of tempered iron or horn.
But if this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, which has the following shape: Be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you have cut one amygdule, cut the other one according to this same form of cutting.
Then after the cutting, the patient should gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
But if a bleeding occurs, he should gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds have been boiled: or with myrtle leaves and similar things from styptics, until the flow of blood ceases: then take care of him until he is healed.
And sometimes other apostemas are born in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Cut them therefore according to what I said about cutting the two tonsils equally.
And I myself was already treated for a woman suffering from an abscess that had developed between her throat and the chin, which pulled towards disfigurement, with few sensations, because it had almost closed off her throat: and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and had now forbidden her food and drink of water: and she had reached death's door if left for one or two days.
And the abscess had risen so far that it had discharged two branches until they emerged above two nostril openings.
