I therefore hastened and fixed one of them with a hook. Then I pulled it out and removed a large portion from it.
Then I cut it off where it was grasped through the nostril opening.
Then I did that to her which had protruded from the other nostril opening.
Then I opened her mouth and pressed her tongue; and afterwards I fixed the hook into the abscess; then I cut off a part from it, and only a small amount of blood flowed out: thus the woman's throat was cleared, and she began to drink water immediately, then received food.
So I did not cease to cut into that abscess many times over a long period of time; and the abscess succeeded in the place where I was cutting, until it was prolonged too far with it.
I therefore applied and cauterized the abscess between her throat.
It then ceased to increase: afterwards I walked away from it, and did not know what God had done with her after me.
Chapter on the cutting of a uvular abscess.
Chapter 37.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula and abscesses, and is long: then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round: then it is called uvula.
When it is cured with what we have said in the division, and the cure does not agree: and you see that the abscess has cooled down from it and becomes fine: then it is necessary to cut into it.
And what is attached to it being round, and it has no length, and is blood-red or dark-colored or black, and has no sensation: then one should make an incision on it. For there is fear in her over the weak.
Therefore, when you see her according to the manner I said from whiteness and length, make the weak person sit before the sun: and press his tongue with the instrument whose narration preceded: then place a hook in it, and pull her down below: and cut her with one of the two instruments which I mentioned for cutting both amigdalar.
And it is necessary that you do not cut from there unless what is added is above the natural limit without addition.
For if you cut too much from there, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use what we narrated for cutting both amigdalas: and take care of her until she is healed.
But if the patient is terrified by the incision, then you should use your skill in cauterizing him without fear and dread.
And the method of cauterization there is not, unless with sharp water: and it is, as you put the patient's head in your arm. Then press his tongue with the instrument we mentioned. Then take the water which I mentioned to you in the chapter on cauterizing, and fill with it a burnt hoof not extinguished, and put it not dense, nor thin, and fill from it the concavity of this instrument, whose shape is this.
Let its extremity be at the end where the medicine is placed, like the concavity of the ear canal of the radius.
And put the instrument with medicine on the same uvula: and let the patient lie down on his side, so that the saliva may flow from his mouth internally through the medicine, so that nothing from it descends to his throat and harms him.
Then take your hand with medicine. And you place it on the wound as much as a quarter of an hour, until you see it is already healed and the pungency of medicine is quieted.
And if you wish to take a ligature, and wrap it around the end of the radius, and pour medicine onto the ligature, and insert the radius with the ligature into the canal above, until the ligature adheres to the wound.
Do that many times, until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the wound. Then let it go: for it indeed dries up and falls off after three days, or four.
What if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after cauterization, wash around the wound with a soaked ligature dipped in butter, and cleanse with what is on its circumference from the medicine: and anoint with cold water.
And treat it externally with ointments, and internally with gargles, until it heals.
Therefore this mode of treatment is more wholesome by an incision and longer in removing fear.
And sometimes the wound is also cured by something that is finer than caustic and incision: it is cured with fumigation, in this way. Take calament, and hyssop, and origanum, and rue, and sseah, and chamomile, and abrotanum, and similar herbs to these: and gather all of them together, or some of them in a pot, and submerge them with vinegar, and let them boil: and the pot should be coated with clay well: and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot, above which an instrument will be placed according to this method.
Make it from silver or bronze: and insert the end, where there is a berry, into the mouth of the patient, until the vapor ascends to the wound above the canal, until the wound becomes very dark.
Then iterate over her many times until she is relieved.
And beware, lest you perform this treatment at the beginning of an apostematic arrival. For it often adds to an apostema: and it is not necessary for you to do that unless upon the decline of his apostema's heat.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take a reed and compose its bark at the extremity of it, so that the sick person's mouth is not burned; because the reed's bark prevents vapor from burning the mouth: and this is good treatment with salvation.
On the Extraction of Thorns and Bones from the Throat.
Chapter 38.
A bone or a fish spine often adheres to the throat many times.
Therefore, you must remove what appears to be on top of it and falls into view, after pressing the tongue with an instrument under the sun, and it will be revealed to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, you must make the sick person vomit before food is digested in the stomach. Perhaps indeed something attached will come out through vomiting.
Or the sick person swallows a piece of cress, or a root of lettuce, or swallows a dry biscuit: or takes a piece of dried sea sponge, and binds it with thread, then swallows it. When therefore he arrives at the place where the spines or bone are, and is extracted, then it will come out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we said: then use a lead instrument in this shape.
Let it be a roughness greater than a radius for a little bit, and at the end of it let there be a curvature which he inserts into his own throat with ease: and he should have his head lifted upwards.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis, lest he be seized by a cough, and let the doctor push with him, or the bone, or introduce himself with his own hand.
But if the patient introduces himself, this is better: on account of his knowledge of the location of the attached thing; and let him push downwards or draw his hand upwards with an instrument, all according to the quantity which is prepared for him, until he emerges.
Of the Extraction of the Leech.
Chapter 39.
When the leech is treated with what I said about cures with medicines and does not concur, then look at the patient's throat before the sun or behind it after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument that I told you about.
If then your sight falls on the leech, remove it with a small hook or with gentle and subtle gestures.
What if it is not possible with these: then take a hollow reed and insert it into the patient's throat up to the throat; then insert the heated iron rod from the fire into the hollow of the reed. Do that many times: and let the patient abstain from water all day.
Then let him take a full bladder of cold water and open his mouth into it; and let him moisten his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it: and let him move the water one hour after another with his own hand. For the leech immediately falls when it feels the water.
If however it does not emerge with what we narrated, then fumigate the patient with fleas or with a feather using the instrument that I told you about for fumigating the uvula.
Do that many times, because it will fall.
And the method of operation in fumigating is that you take a pot, in which are alive prunes with fire; and the pot is covered with a lid, in whose middle is an opening; and it should be composed in that opening at the end of the instrument.
Then place a suffumigation and let the patient put his mouth at the end of the cannula and close his mouth so that the suffumigation does not go out until you know that the suffumigation has reached the blood-sucking place. For the blood-sucking place will immediately fall.
But if it does not fall, then repeat the suffumigation many times.
And let the patient endure thirst, and eat salted foods, and others, but not drink water.
Therefore it is necessary for him to go out with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the blood-sucking place is extracted from the throat when it falls on its surface.
It resembles forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature which is inserted into the throat. And its end is similar to the beak of a bird called a stork, in which there is a roughness like a file: when it grasps something, it does not let go entirely.
Of the opening of aposthemes, and their division, and care in general.
Chapter 40.
The species of aposthemes are many, of many kinds, according to the recollection of them, namely one and one in this book: and they are diverse in their perforation and division into two modes.
One of which is a type of apostheme that is in itself, and because it contains moisture from humidity.
And the second mode is due to locations where they occur in the body.
For an apostheme occurring on the head is one thing, and different from an apostheme occurring in the anus; and an apostheme occurring on a fleshy part is another thing, and different from an apostheme occurring at a joint: and each of them has its own judgment according to operation.
And there are aposthemes that should not be perforated unless after maturation of the pus in them and its completion; and among these are those that must be perforated while they are raw, and do not mature according to their completion.
As abscesses which are near joints.
Because when an abscess occurs near a joint and it prolongs itself until what is inside it putrefies, perhaps the surrounding ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: therefore, it is a more immediate cause of that member's contraction.
Or it is an abscess in the main member: because if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either that one or through the main member.
Or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it unripe: and it is not proper to open up a raw abscess that is not yet fully digested, except when it is near the anus, so that it does not putrefy deep and extend to the internal parts of the anus, and become a fistula or remain in its end without healing.
You should know the time for perforating an abscess when it has matured according to its completion; and this is at a time of sedation of pain, recession of fever, diminution of swelling, pulse, and sharpness of the head of the abscess: and other signs.
And you should make the same perforation in the lower part of the abscess and more prominent elevation: and let the perforation go along the length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of hands and feet, and elbow joints, and tendons, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflexion.
But in those that are reflected, proceed with the perforation according to that member.
When however the abscess is in fleshy places: then it is better to delay its perforation until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to its completion.
Because if you perforate it before that, the course of the pus is prolonged from it, and there are many impurities and foulness, and perhaps the edges of it become hardened, and its depth.
And certain abscesses are pierced according to the breadth of the body in necessity or according to what a member requires.
And it is necessary to administer a small incision to small abscesses and a large incision to large ones; or multiple sections according to the similarity of the size of the abscess.
And there are abscesses from which the skin must be poulticed; and when it has been made like a cloth, and has become dead at the edge, as happens to many things that are dubious, and boils.
And from them are those which are cut with an incision having three angles. And from them is one that is cut like the shape of a leaf of myrtle.
Like an abscess in the groin.
And from them are those in which a circular incision and a lunar incision are administered: and similar ones from the incisions.
However, that which has no head, such as mild and superficial abscesses, should be pierced with a superficial or flat incision only.
And it is necessary, when the abscess is large and has already collected a great deal of pus, to pierce it so that you do not hurry and extract all the pus at once; rather, extract some from it. Then bind the abscess with an opilum until another day. Then extract something else from the pus again.
Do this many times according to degree until it drains out: especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an old decrepit person: for the spirit of the animal often dissolves from one animal to another with the discharge of pus: and perhaps the patient will die, and you will not notice.
Therefore beware this chapter with great caution.
And after your incision, by which you pierce abscesses, it is necessary to clean the wound and observe. If then there is a wound of apostema small, or it is a flat incision, then administer linen oil from flax, or old cotton. And if there is a great apostema and many perforations are present, it is necessary then to insert linen into every perforation until they reach the inside.
And if you have excised a part of the skin from an apostema, or in its surrounding area you have made it concave: then it is necessary to fill it with old cotton, or as when cloth is sewn, or it is removed with flax fibers without moisture: and tie it so that it remains for three days.
Then throw it away, and care for it as needed with an ointment until it heals.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation hour, then administer cold water and vinegar, after the cloth has been soaked in them: and place it repeatedly over the site of the bleeding.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary to apply a treatment, and cure, and powders that I have described in many places of this our book, and from division.
But if it is in the time of winter, and the site of the apostema is over many nerves: then it is necessary to soak wool with wine and hot oil: and place them on the sites.
And if it is in the summer, and the sites are fleshy: then it is necessary to place wool soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and all that is cold. Until the third day, as we said, then it is necessary to open the apostema, and clean it, and apply to its cure what is fitting for it, until it heals.
This therefore is what is necessary from universal knowledge of the treatment of apostemata.
According to the path of true distinction, I have already spoken about every apostema, how is the way to its cure chapter by chapter individually.
Head 41.
On the removal of boils that occur on the scalp of the head.
Boils occur on the scalp of the head which are small and are of the type of nodules, contained in sacks, that is as if they were the eggs of a hen.
And there are many kinds of them.
Some of them are fatty; and some which contain moisture similar to mud: and some which contain moisture similar to that of fresh cheese and curds: and there are others like these, and some are hard and stony.
And in the cutting of all and the removal of them, there is no fear, as long as the artery does not interfere with their cutting.
And the operation in cutting them is that you should examine them with an instrument of which the shape comes after these, which is called an Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it contains moisture, then cut it with a shallow incision according to the length of the superficial cut: in this shape.
And begin with a cut from the line b to the line g. When the moisture has evacuated, then remove the crust that contains that moisture, and do not leave anything entirely.
For it often returns if something remains of it.
Then immerse the cut in Egyptian ointment if it is present: and if not, in water and salt: and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For what remains of the crust will rot.
Then repeat the process over the cut with Egyptian ointment a second and third time, and if you need it, until you are confident that nothing remains of the crust; then indeed apply care to the wound with ointment until it heals.
What if the boil contains a fatty nodule? Then cut it according to this shape, with an incision having the form of a cross: and place lancets between the divisions of the incision, and remove everything from all parts: and try your best in extracting the sack that contains it.
If however an artery makes opposition to you, then do that which I shall tell you afterwards.
And similarly do this in an ulceration, if it is a bony one, by incision and the same treatment, according to what we have said.
And the incision above a bony ulceration is easier: because it has few blood and moisture. And sometimes there are some of these ulcerations which are on the head of certain people, in which there is not at all moisture.
Which is because I cut above an ulceration on the head of an old woman, and I found an ulcer like a hard, solid, rough, white stone that could not be broken; and if someone would strike it with a blow while falling, it would wound him.
However, in other ulcerations that occur on the head, apart from those which occur on the heads of children and at the roots of ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial one.
Or make a perforation in them always from the lower part of them, so that the flow of pus may be eased downwards.
Then the cure is done with it as is fitting for each one according to its treatment.
Of the cutting of Strophulae on the neck.
Chapter 42.
These ulcerations often occur on the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And above those that occur on the neck, there is one and many, and they are generated from each other; and every Strophula is within its own fold, just as it is with nodes and head ulcers, as we have narrated.
And the kinds of these Strophulae are many. And among them there are those that are bony, and those that contain moisture, and those that are malignant, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, when you see a lump that appears to have good structure in touch, which is clearly close in color to the skin, and moves freely in every direction, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the gullet, nor to an artery, nor is it deep: it is necessary to cut it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body along the line, b. to the line, g. and remove it from all parts: and stretch two flaps of skin with forceps, or with many forceps if you need them, as we said in head abscesses, and pull it out gradually.
And it is advisable to take care that the vein or nerve is not cut.
And let the spatula be not too sharp: so that it does not cause you to cut unnecessarily with your hand at the incision, or trouble a weak person, and cut what is not necessary.
But if you cut a vein or artery, then stop the operation at once, and apply to the wound some powdered zinc or certain powders that coagulate blood, and tie up the wound, and leave it alone until the inflammation of the abscess subsides and the wound becomes soft: or else it may begin to putrefy.
For the blood is coagulated by a poultice. Therefore, return to your operation until you are finished.
Then examine with your finger if any small stumps remain; therefore cut them and clean them.
But if a large vein is in the root of the stump: then it is necessary not to cut that stump from its own root; rather, it is necessary to tie it with a double strong thread and let it go until it falls off without harm.
Then fill the wound with the old lint that you had previously soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then care for it.
If Strophula is entirely incised, then it is necessary to gather the two lips of the wound and immediately set him up, after you see that there is no longer any surplus.
But if you see that there remains surplus because of the size of Strophula, then it is necessary to turn your attention to incising its root and pulling it out: and apply the suture which we mentioned.
And that which contains moistures from Strophula, pierce it with a superficial perforation: so that the location of its maturity appears to you; and place the perforation below that part of the body that follows downwards, as we said.
Then apply licinia with ointment and similar substances after the perforation, so that what remains from the corruption is absorbed; until you see the wound to be clean again. Then care for it with generative ointment of flesh, until it heals.
Head 43.
On the section of the Epiglottis from an abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients said that this section is of the Epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone on earth who has done it; and this is the writing of their discourse.
However, in those having synancheum, it is necessary to remove the section of the Epiglottis; since no benefit will come from that because all the veins and lung are weak.
In those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no sickness in the canal, it is necessary to use the section of the Epiglottis, to escape from the destruction which comes from obstruction.
Therefore, when we wish to do that, it is necessary to cut the Epiglottis under three or four circles of the Epiglottis, by a small section through its width, in what is between two circles, as much as possible, so that the section be in the mucous membrane, not in cartilage: and this place indeed is suitable for the section, because it is deprived of flesh, and the vessels of blood are distant from it.
If the surgeon is timid, then he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he finds the skin until it reaches the canal; he empties the vessel of blood if he sees anything from them.
Then he cuts the siphon as we have described: and it signifies above the cutting of the canal from the phlegm that flows out from it, when it is torn apart and the voice is cut off.
And leave the open wound for some time.
When therefore the hour arrives that fears suffocation, gather together two lips of the wound and sew it up by itself without cartilage.
Then administer medicines which make flesh grow until it is healed.
Albucasis says. The sum of this discourse that we have narrated is not explained, except when they see that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the sick person's throat: and the sick person has been lifted up to death, and wants to be cut open for his breathing: they go to cutting off the Epiglottis, so that the sick person breathes over the place of the wound by some respiration, and is saved from death: and because of this they have ordered to leave the open wound, until the malice of the sickness is consumed, and it is a three-day evil: then therefore they have ordered to sew up the wound, and cure it, until he is healed.
And what I saw through myself is that one servant took a knife and sent it with into her own throat, and it happened to her with him as far as the part of the lung's canal.
And I was called to her healing, and when I found her moaning, like the mooing of one who is dying.
So I removed the wound, and found a small amount of blood that had flowed out from the wound. So I was confident that she had not cut the vein, nor stopped up the breathing: and breath was issuing forth from the wound.
Therefore I hastened, and took care of my wound, and cured it until it was sound: and nothing happened to the maidservant, except that she became hoarse in her voice: and she returned after a few days to better management of her own affairs.
Therefore I say: that there is no fear in cutting away the Epiglottis.
Of the Cutting Away of a Throat Hernia.
Chapter 44.
This hernia named Hernia gutturis is a great swelling, according to the color of the body: and it is found very much in women.
And it is according to two modes: either it is natural or accidental.
In the natural one, indeed there is no fault.
The accidental one however is according to two modes: of which one is similar to fatty nodes. And the other mode is similar to an abscess that occurs from arterial constriction. And in this case there is fear, and one should not presume to cut into it with iron, unless it is small; and you must test it and investigate it with an instrument. If therefore you find it to be similar to a fatty node, and it is not suspended from any vessels; then cut into it, just as one cuts into nodes: and extract it with what contains it from the chest, if it is in the chest; and if not, then thoroughly examine all of its contents. Then care for the place according to what is needed for healing, until it heals.
Chapter 45.
Of the Cutting Away above Fatty Nodes.
There are many kinds of nodes, and I have already spoken about all their kinds in division: and it is necessary for me to narrate here the difference between a node and an Exitura: since it causes doubt.
I say therefore that when an Exitura is present there is heat, fever, and severe pains, until the effusion of excess is stilled and putrefaction is completed: then indeed the fevers and heat subside.
When there is no knot [i.e. wound], nor heat, nor fever, nor pain, and they contain them with a kistis called sifachin, which is their own bag, and it is according to the color of the body: and it is the taking in of them like beans, and it becomes like batecha, and greater and lesser.
And they are according to two kinds: either fat or containing moisture. And the colors of moisture are many, according to what I said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the treatment of a knot [i.e. wound], you must examine it and inquire into it first with the instrument that is called Intromissum, according to what its form will be in the chapter that follows this one.
And the method of inquiry for all apostemes and knots is this: you should take this instrument and insert it into a more humid place which you find in the aposteme; and then revolve your fingers with it gradually, until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then your last finger according to the measurement of the size of the aposteme.
Then withdraw the Intromissum and consider what comes out as a sign of it.
If then moisture running with any color comes out, then cut it off with a superficial section, according to what I said about other apostemes.
And if no moisture comes out in the sign of the Intromissum, then you will know that it is fatty: therefore cut off above it with a section having the shape of a cross, according to this form, as I taught you; and suspend it with hooks, and scrape off the skin from all parts with ease: and preserve the kistin if you can over it, so that you may draw out the healthy part with the Knot.
But if the kistin is torn apart during the operation and it is not possible to draw out the healthy part, for indeed that often happens, then draw it out in pieces, until nothing remains of it.
If anything remains from it a little, or much, returns to the Knot according to most.
If it overcomes you and something remains from it a little, then fill the wound at your completion with certain corrosive sharp powders: and close the wound, and place above it what sits as warm pus, and care for it along with the rest of the cure until it is healed.
If therefore there is a Knot great, then split its two lips and care for it with what enfleshes.
But if there is a vein to you that pulsates or does not pulsate: then hasten and fill the place with trodden sesame, and leave it tight for two days or three, until the wound putrefies and the ebullition of blood ceases: then return to cutting into what remains from the Knot.
Chapter 46.
Chapter on external instruments for cutting and piercing.
There are shapes of Instruments of Introduction, and they are of three kinds.
From them there is a great one, and from them there is a middle-sized one, and from them there is a small one.
Let them be made of iron with rounded edges on the outer extremities, so that they hasten entrance into the abscess.
And these are forms of Probes, and no, that is, piercing ones: and one of them is called Beris, that is, piercing; and they are of three kinds.
From them there is a great one, and a middle-sized one, and a small one.
They congregate to be investigated through these abscesses, and outlets, and fistulas, and boils from what is between them, that is, from the bones.
And others are made rounded, smooth, and gentle, like large needles which serve as seats, of course, from citron wood, or air, or iron, or silver.
And better among them are those that are made from citron.
And experiments are also made from black lead, which are suitable for testing fistulas whose concavities are adapted to be reflected with their own softness along with those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. Because from them there are long ones, from them middle-sized ones, and from them short ones, according to the size of the fistula's amplitude and its constriction.
The form of bandages, and they are many kinds.
Because from them there are simple ones, namely those with a single curvature: and they are three kinds as you see, Great, Middle, and Small.
And from them there are bandages that are blind: and they are three kinds.
And from them there are bandages that have two curves, and they are three kinds: and from them there are bandages that have three curves, and they are three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of scalpels, with which knots and abscesses are cut and lanced: and they are three kinds: because from them there is a great one, a middle one, and a small one.
Their ends where the section is made are sharp, while their other ends are not sharp: and they are not put to use unless for the purpose of providing benefit in the lancing of knots near the fear of incision of a vein or nerve: and so that the infirm may be healed through them, and find tranquility shortly after the burning which is found in the lancing of an abscess.
They are made of bronze, and they are similar to Radiorum, with which one makes alcohol: and at the broad end there is a hidden puncture of Spathium: and within it flows towards the inside and outside when you wish, as you see.
The form of skin excrescences which are hidden between the fingers at the site of an abscess: and those who are weak do not perceive them: and they are of three kinds, great, middle-sized, and small.
The forms of windsores with which bleeding is stopped: and they are of three kinds, great, middle-sized, and small.
Let them be made round from air or citron, about as long as you see, and fine to subtlety.
And it is necessary for these forms of windsores to be at hand with you, great and small: so that you may stop blood flow through them with speed in necessity, and then when medicine is not present.
However, you should not use them for stopping blood flow in every part of the body, and you should only use them on fleshy parts: such as arms, thighs, and shoulders, and breasts, and belly, and groin, and similar areas.
And sometimes they are made into small instruments similar to the rinds of pomegranates, according to their shape: with which blood is stopped when it flows from the site of phlebotomy, or at the incision of a vein, or artery; and sometimes they are made round in this shape.
Chapter 47.
On the care of men's breasts which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men become inflamed at the completion of sleep (that is, when they begin to emit seminal fluid during sleep) until they resemble those of women: and they remain foul and swollen.
Whoever abhors this, should apply the lunar caesarean technique to the breasts, according to this shape from line "b" to line "g". Then remove the entire fatty accumulation. Then fill the wound with medicinal ointment. Then gather two lips of the wound with a suture, and care for it until it heals.
But if the nipple inclines towards the lower parts and softens due to its size, as happens in women: then it is necessary to cut into two upper sides of his own two similar sections resembling a crescent shape, each containing one with another at the ends of both, until there is a line larger containing the smaller one, according to this form from b to g. Then remove the skin that is between the two lips and take away fatness, and administer what we said about suturing and powders, which is necessary for healing, until it heals.
But if you do not complete the incision as it is necessary for you to make, because of the patient's restlessness or due to bleeding: then it is necessary that you fill the wound with a poultice immersed in corrosive ointment, and let it go, until what remains of fatness has been consumed. Then care for it until it heals.
About the piercing of an abscess that occurs under the titillate.
Chapter 48.
These abscesses that occur under the titillate are of the kind of hardened Strophulae which contain pus, and from these some contain moisture.
As for those that contain moisture, it is necessary to open them with a lunar incision, according to this form, from line b. to line g. When therefore everything that is in the abscess is emptied of pus, then fill it with old poultice and let it go until another day.
Then remove the poultice and treat it with a suitable ointment for it, until it heals.
What if the abscess is of the kind of nodules of Strophulae? Then cut above it, as we said in equal sections over the Strophulae.
If however a wound is extended so that it discharges pus, then apply a cauter to it, according to what we said previously.
Chapter 49.
On the cutting off of an apostema which occurs due to the artery and vein.
When an artery is torn and the skin above it becomes hardened, it often happens that an apostema occurs there; similarly with a vein, so that an apostema and inflammation occur.
And the signs by which it is known that an apostema and inflammation are due to an artery and vein are these: because when an apostema is caused by an artery, it is long and aggregated in the depths of the body; and when you push on the apostema with your fingers, you feel as if there is a hissing sound.
And that which is caused by a vein is rounded in the visible part of the body.
And the cutting into this apostema is an error, and especially when it occurs under the armpits, groin, throat, and in many parts of the body; and it is very great. Therefore it is necessary to avoid its cure with an iron instrument, and that which extends from them also to the extremities or head, one must avoid.
Therefore on that which arises from the inflammation of the artery's opening, make an incision in the skin in a longitudinal direction. Then open up the incision with a hook, afterwards the artery; and free it from its coverings until it is fully exposed.
Then insert a needle under it and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie off the artery with a double thread in two places, just as I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temporal regions.
Then puncture the spot that is between two ligaments with a spatula-like instrument, until the blood that is in it all comes out and the apostema is resolved.
Then administer treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall apart: afterward, care for it with suitable ointments until it heals.
What if an abscess arises from a vein section, then you should keep your hand as clean as possible from the abscess with skin. Then insert the needle under the spot where you held your hand, and make it penetrate; and let a thread be twice as long, until you draw it out from another side. Then tie with the abscess itself a good knot: according to what I said to you about tying an olive knot, from two sides with two ends of thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with a different thread under the entire abscess near the cut of the needle; press and tie your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in half, until what is inside it comes out; then cut away the excess skin and let go what has been tied off from it.
Then place over it a poultice which you moistened with wine and oil. Then administer treatment that is done with lichen and ointments, until it heals.
Book 5, Chapter L (Latin).
Of the abscess that occurs from twisting of Nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in Arteries and Veins, similarly an abscess occurs in Nerves: which occurs from a blow or from excessive labor, and similar causes; and it happens most often in the places of aiders, or heels, and in every place where joints are moved: and is a grave abscess.
It resembles its color to the rest of the body, and it is in many dispositions without pain: and when it is tied with weakness, the weak person feels like numbness in it.
And it is not an abscess accumulated deep within the body, but rather is beneath the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not go towards the front or before, nor back to the rear.
What is therefore present in joints should not be touched with iron. Perhaps indeed contraction will occur.
However, as for what is present in the head or forehead, find a skin crease with a spatula. So if there is a small abscess, then hold it with forceps and cut it off from its root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and scrape off the top; next, remove it, and gather the wound together with a suture; and care for it until it heals.
Chapter 51.
Of the excision of an abscess that occurs in the belly.
Some people are afflicted many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies with certain things which are called Fungalia, similar to mushrooms.
Whose root is fine, and whose head is thick, and their lips have turned inward. And it is small among them; and there is one that is greatly enlarged.
And I myself have already seen a man, and in his belly were two thelils, which were more similar to things other than fungi: there was no difference between them, white and small roots, and their lips turned inward, and they were filled, and moisture always flowed from them.
I therefore cut them open and found that one weighed almost 10 3/4 ounces and the other almost 6 ounces. And the operation of cutting them open is this: first consider whether the patient is humid, and if the color of the thelil is white, moist, and fine root; then cut it with a broad spatula, and have cauteries ready in your fire.
For indeed much blood is often expelled during the cutting of it; therefore, be prepared if blood overflows you, then cauterize it.
If you see an infirm person frightened and timid about incision with a iron tool, then take a thread made from lead, well-made and tie it with athel to him. The method is as we have said, and leave him for two days.
Then add the leaden strip. Therefore you should not stop tying on the lead until it is removed and falls off by itself without any effort.
But if the root is thick and large, then there is something to be feared and dreaded in its incision, especially since it is in the belly.
However, it is possible to cut it in half. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then take care of it until the wound is healed.
And beware not to presume to cut away a root that is of dull color, lacking sensation, and foul appearance: for it is an apostematic cancerous thing: and I come with the reminder of Cancer after this.
Chapter 52.
Paragraph on the care of a Umberlical Hernia.
A hernia occurs at the navel from many causes. Or from fissures of the si-face, which is above the belly: and it comes out through the umbilical cord, or intestine, according as it happens in other ruptures. Or from blood which flows from a vein, or an artery, according to what has been mentioned before. Or from the constriction of the intestines in that place.
If however it is due to a fissure of the si-face and the umbilical cord, then it has a color similar to that of the body's apostema: and it is smooth, without pain: and appears in different positions. And if it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, then its position is as we have described, with a greater diversity. When therefore you press with your fingers, it becomes hidden; then it returns, and perhaps it comes with a sound; and it is greatly magnified at the entrance of the bath, and a violent effort.
If it is because of moisture, it is soft; then it does not become hidden when you press it with your hand, nor does it constrict, nor is anything added.
What if it is because of blood; then when these signs appear, a abscess turns black from the swelling.
But if it is because of natural flesh: then it is a grave abscess, large, hard, and stays according to one size.
If it is because of flatness, then the touch of it is soft.
And the operation in that case is, that you should then consider whether the protrusion of the navel is due to blood from an artery, or a vein, or flatness; then it is necessary for you to refrain from treating it. For in that case there is fear and dread, just as I taught you in the chapter where I said that abscesses are due to an artery and a vein.
What if the protrusion of the navel is due to the intestine or a tumor; then you should hasten to warn the patient that he should hold his breath and stand upright with his body extended. Then signs are made on the circumference of the navel with a cauterizing iron. Afterwards, instruct him to lie flat between your hands on his back. Then encircle with a broad spatula around the navel in the area you marked with the cauterizing iron. Afterwards, extend the middle of the abscess upwards with a large hook. Then bind him tightly around the circumference with a strong thread or with a cord of linen tied firmly; and let the knot be adjusted.
Then open up the extended middle of the abscess above the ligature. If then you find the thing already captured by a ligament: then draw out the other parts, and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine towards the interior of the belly: and if you find a knot, then take it with a hook, and cut off its superfluity.
But if there is against you an artery or vein: then surround it much. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads: and insert two needles into the surrounding part which you made around the abscess in the shape of a cross, making them penetrate.

Then care for it with what is needed from the ointments, until it is healed.
What if a swelling of the navel is from fatty flesh in it, or from moisture: then you should have the abscess drained, as we said. And draw out the blood or moisture that you find in it.

I couldn't heal anyone from that: nor did I see another before me who reached it.

Of the cure of Hydrophobia.

Then take a thorn-like spoon, in this shape. The two sharp, long parts are like the thorn-like spoon, having an acute extremity. However, there should be something of shortness gradually in it, so that you do not pass through with it during the operation up to the intestine and injure it.
Then consider. For if it is a case of hydrocephalus generation from the part of the intestines, then you should lengthen with the incision from the navel to a distance of three fingers' breadth, directly downwards above the chest.
If the generation of hydrocephalus is due to liver disease: then your incision should be on the left from the navel, three fingers' breadth.
And if the generation of it is due to the spleen, then the incision should be on the right side: and it is not necessary that the incision should be also on the side where the patient wants to lie down, lest superfluities flow to that weak place.
Then pierce through with an instrument all around. Then insert into that incision and lift your hand with the thorn-like spoon between the skin, and scrape, as if you were stripping it away: and let the amount that is stripped away be the size of a fingernail or about that.

And it is similar to a cannula: made of silver, or copper, or bronze, smooth and bright: with a small hole at its lower part, and three holes on either side, two on one side and one on the other side: and the end of it is cut off, according to the shape of a calamus, as you see: in its upper extremity there is a band.
When the instrument reaches water, then it immediately descends upon the instrument; therefore, it evacuates from water a medium quantity in an hour.
Since if you evacuate more from it than is proper, the patient may perhaps die in an hour by the resolution of his animal spirits; or syncopes occurs which approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the strength of its virtue, and according to what the patient's dispositions indicate to you through the strength of his pulse and his condition.
Then remove the instrument, and retain the water.
Which is because it is retained immediately by the skin which retains the orifice that is above the siphon: about which I spoke, in order to pierce it according to that method.
Then insert the instrument again on the next day, if you see the patient is able to tolerate it; and also evacuate a small quantity from the water.
Do that for several days without fear, and guard yourself against error: until there remains little or no water left.
But if you fear for the patient and dismiss much of the water: then take care of him with burial in hot sand, and profuse sweating in a bath and under the sun, and wait until his thirst is quenched: and take care of him with medicaments that dry out, until he is healed; and cauterize over the stomach, liver, and spleen, after the removal of water: according to what I spoke previously.

Chapter 57.
On the cleansing of infants and the cure for any mistake that may have befallen them.
Circumcision is not the breaking of continuity, like other wounds: yet since it is our operation, our will, and its use is peculiarly in children, it is necessary that we mark with it a better operation and a path leading to salvation.
I say therefore that the ancients did not write about circumcision in any of their books, because they do not use it in their laws, and it is nothing but what we have acquired through experience.
For it is because I have found the majority of surgeons and bloodletters to use cleansing by circumcision with a scalpel and forceps, and they treat those affected, bandaging with a thread, and cutting with a nail.
And I indeed have tried out all these methods, and I did not find a better cleansing with forceps and bandaging with a thread.
Because in the cleansing with a scalpel, the skin often escapes many times. Because the skin of the foreskin has two layers: therefore perhaps the upper layer is cut and the lower layer remains: so it is forced to be cut again, and future pain will result. And with the cleansing that is done by laying on of hands, that is, manual manipulation used by surgeons, the cut at the end of the male member is not secured: because perhaps it may enter into its orifice.
In the process of cleaning with a spatula, perhaps iron will escape and ruin your operation: and indeed the skin of a child is naturally short-lived, and many times they are born in such a way that they do not require cleaning; and I have already seen this.
But in the process of cleaning with forceps and tying with a thread, I have obtained a good result: because forceps are proportional to the incision: therefore, since the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below. Therefore, when you place your hand, two sharpnesses are proportioned and they fall according to the operation at one time; and it makes a compression similar in shape, containing the skin of the wound from every part, with which there is no error whatsoever.
And the proper method of operation is to make the child think that you are not doing it, especially if it is one who understands a little, that which you do not do except for tying the thread on its wound just so much: and then let it go until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be happy in every way that is possible to you, because he receives his own understanding. Then make him stand between your prepared hands, and let him not be sitting; and hide the forceps in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the child may see nothing of them, nor anything from the instruments.
Then insert your hand into his wound and blow on it, and draw it up until the end of the head of the wound emerges. Then clean it because impurities are accumulating in it.
Then bind the marked place with a double thread. Then bind below, for a little way, another band. Then take hold with your index finger and thumb, and use good retention to cut between the two bands. Then lift up the skin quickly and extract the head of the wound. Then let the blood flow slowly (for it is better) and place powder above: and on top of the powder, a cloth that has been spread with yolk of egg decocted in rose water, pounded with rose oil. Recent good: and let him be above it until another day: and care for it with the rest of the treatment, until he is healed.
However, the shape of a forceps for purification is this.
Let there be two blunt ends and let them meet together: so that no branch of a key may come out; and let the length of both sharp parts be equal to the length of two equally matched arm-lengths.
The error falls in purification is this: because perhaps the inner skin may altogether escape, or a part of it at the incision.
Therefore, you should stretch it forth from your hour with your own finger before the place becomes inflamed: and cut it according to equality.

Therefore cure that with what we said, until he is healed.

Chapter 62.
About cutting on a water-filled hernia.

And if it is equal to you, so that there is one operation, then do so: afterwards wipe away the blood. And introduce a piece of cloth soaked in oil or rose oil into the incisions: and put another cloth that you had submerged in wine and oil, and spread it over both testicles, and also on the belly: and place above double cloths, which are called poultices: and bind them above with a bandage, which has six sections according to this form.
Then two cloths are taken and moistened, and filled with the linen cloth that has been bloodied, according to this similarity: and make the ends which are pressed out from the belly, or damp wool: and gather them together with a binding in the loin of the arms: and one end takes hold above the anus up to the back, and the second takes hold at the part of the chest: and two ends above the two lower hips, and two other ends above the two groin areas, and gather the binding whole in the loin of the arms.
And when this type of surgery is performed artificially, the ramus (branch) is treated with a cauterizer in place of sectioning with iron. It should be done thus: take a cauterizer that is a fine knife, and cut with it the skin of the testicles; and it will be ignited, as we have narrated. Until the white membrane containing water is revealed, then take the cauterizer according to this form.
Are assimilated Greek letters.
Then pierce with it that membrane, and it is ignited, until the moisture goes out entirely.
Then stretch that membrane with a hook, and scrape it off with a burning fine cauterizer, and cut it as far as you can, until you have eradicated the entire thing.
And beware of oil, lest the fire touch it.
And know that this operation is more salubrious, and longer-lasting in preventing bleeding, and better than an operation with cutting.
If however, contrary symptoms appear to you during the time of cutting or cauterizing, such as a warm abscess or bleeding, or something else: then it is necessary for you to treat what is causing the contrariety from that with the cure that suits it, according to what has been mentioned.
But if the patient is fearful and does not await this operation on account of its horror: then perform a perforation according to what I narrate, and it is.
When you are certain of this in your presence, as we have mentioned the signs, that the ramus is watery: then it is necessary for you to make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then pierce the abscess on the lower part of it with a broad spatula: and place the perforation according to the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water flows out.
Then place a compress on the place, and bind it, and let it be until the remaining water flows out: then care for it, until the wound is healed.
If however the contrariety is of water flowing out at the hour when you are perforating it; and this does not happen except because a white cloth makes a contrariety in the orifice of the wound, and prevents the exit of the water: then it is necessary to insert a feather into the wound, or an instrument with which water is drawn off, whose shape has preceded: or add something to the operation on the wound for a little while.
And know that the water returns and gathers after six months, or about those. When it has gathered, then it is purged in the same manner and passes through on a sick day.
Chapter 63.
Of the care of Fleshy Wounds.
Know that in the incision above this Fleshy Wound, there is a fear leading to harm in many dispositions. Therefore it seems to me that its removal will heal from it.
And I am recalling the operation on it and the appearance of suppuration.
I say therefore that many suppurations occur in bodies, from which testicles are composed: and this happens for many reasons, namely.
Or from excessive or sharp fluid that flows to the testicles. Or from striking.
And the appearance of the suppuration is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it: and it is a grave suppuration.
And perhaps it is a stone-like suppuration, whose color is dark, and there is no sensation to it.
And sometimes it happens from the constriction of arteries and their inflation, just as I have already recalled; or from the inflation of veins.
What therefore is from them by way of inflation of arteries, is known by the appearance of the suppuration, when you compress it with your fingers. And it is not necessary for you to touch it directly.
What occurs through venous inflation is not scattered when compressed with fingers.
And now it is possible to make an incision above this type and above other flesh; and that is, as you would cut the skin of testicles. Then stretch the womb upwards and pull it out with a white cloth: and free the suspensory ligament from the vessels, and bind the vessels; and cut the suspensory ligament after you have freed it from all parts of the womb.
If therefore the womb is consolidated with those born flesh, then it is necessary to grasp the womb and cut it.
And if there is adhesion between something from the peritoneum or in what is between the vessels: then it is necessary to free all that adhesion and cut it with a round incision.
But if there is birth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind: then it is necessary to cut all of that and pull out the womb, as we said.
When therefore your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool infused in rose oil and wine: afterwards care for it with other treatment, until it heals.
Chapter 64.
A treatise on the care of Gouty people who are afflicted with Vine.
Vine is a swelling wrapped up with some wrapping, similar to a boil, with softening of the testicles. And it occurs difficult for an infirm person to move and exercise, and to walk.
And in the treatment of this illness is from fear, due to what was previously mentioned.
But it is necessary for us to say how one operates in it according to what the first ones did.
I say therefore that it is necessary for you to make the infirm person sit on an elevated seat. Then push down the suspensory ligament of the testicles. Then grasp the skin of the testicles with your fingers, along with the veins that are nearby the penis; and let another assistant hold them, and stretch them out with a strong extension.
Then cut with a sharp-bladed spatula across the width of the veins in a straight section, until the veins are exposed.
Then scrape off all parts, just as we told you to do with the arteries that are in temporal regions.
Then insert into them a thread which is double, and tie it at the first point where they occur; and tie it again at the last of these. Then cut them in a straight middle section according to the length of the body, and remove what is gathered from corrupted, fetid moisture.
Then care for the wound with the treatment of other wounds, in which pus is generated, so that the veins that nourish one testicle may fall. For it is not bad by that.
But if it happens that the veins are all injured, then it is necessary to remove the testicles with the veins: so that the sperm may not be deprived of nourishment due to the incision, and wither away or dry up, and not be beneficial along with them.
Chapter 65.
Of the Treatment for Intestinal Ramrods.
The exit of this Ramrod is from a section that occurs in the siphac extended above the belly in the part of the groin from the ventral abdomen.
Therefore, the intestines descend through that rupture to one testicle. And this rupture is either from the siphac or its extension.
And these two kinds occur due to many causes. Or from impact. Or from a fall. Or from a blow. Or from the elevation of a heavy object and similar things.
And a sign is when it comes from an extension of the siphac. It is what happens gradually over a long period of time, and does not happen suddenly; and it is an equal apostema towards the depth: therefore, because the siphac compresses the intestine.
And its sign is when it comes from the section of the siphac, because it makes happen a sharp pain suddenly from the beginning; and it is a different apostema, appearing under the skin nearby: and that is due to the exit of the intestine and its course towards the exterior of the siphac.
And sometimes it issues forth with an intestine wrapped around; and this is called the Intestinal Ramus and Zirbia: and sometimes it happens with inflammation.
And sometimes it runs into the intestine with feces and is retained there; and it is accompanied by the destruction of a sick person: because it makes happen a bad pain, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the treatment of this disease's species with iron there is fear; therefore it is necessary to beware of accidents in that.
And the method of the operation is, to force the sick person to reduce their intestine by hand to the inner concavities of their own, if they obey. Then it should be raised above his neck between your hands: and lift up his legs.
Then extend the skin that follows the skin to the upper parts, and cut the scrotal skin entirely along its length. Then place in the two lips of the incision, hooked instruments, according to your need to open the incision with them; and the incision should be such a size as it is possible for an egg to come out from it.
Then strip away the siphac and what is under the skin of the testicles, until when the white siphac is uncovered, hard from every part: then introduce your index finger into it, following the testicle, in that which is between the white siphac, which is under the skin, and between the second siphac and without sticking to it which is in the posterior part of the testicle.
Then move your right hand to the inner side of the testicular skin: and with this extend a white cloth upward, with your left hand. And lift the egg up with the cloth to the part of the sectioning: and instruct the assistant to extend it upward: and you loosen the adhesion which is behind the complete solution.
And search with your fingers to see that nothing from the intestine is wrapped around the hard white cloth. If however you find something [there], then push it into the lower belly.
Then take a needle with a thick thread made from ten threads, and insert it into the back of the cloth, that is, the tunic that is under the skin of the testicles which follows the section; then cut off the ends that protrude from the thread until there are four threads. Then place some of them on top of the others in the shape of a cross, and tie with them the white cloth, about which we spoke, because it is under the skin of the testicles tied tightly, until nothing from the vessels that nourish them can reach there: so that no hot abscess may occur. And place another ligature outside the first ligature, two fingers less long from it. And after these two ligatures, let go from the white cloth, which is under the skin of the testicles, as much as a finger's length; and cut off the remainder entirely in a rounded shape, and remove the egg.
Then cut off the lower part of the skin of the testicles by sectioning, from which blood and pus flow out: just as we described above.
Then apply a bandage soaked in oil to the wound: and apply the ligature which we described. And sometimes the white cloth that we mentioned is cauterized after its incision, fearing a flow of blood. That often happens indeed. And let go of the ligament until it falls by itself.
But if its case is delayed, then let it be anointed with hot water; and place over it what we said, until it falls.
Then take care of the wound along with the rest of the wound treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
About Wounds from Thorns.
In this type of thorn wound, I have not seen anyone who has dared to treat it with iron.

Chapter 67.
About Disruptions that Occur in Groins.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, as we said, and the swelling appears and does not go down to the testicles; and if it goes down, it is slight and returns to its place in all hours: but if it persists with this condition, a fissure forms in the scrotum until the intestines descend into the scrotum.
And that happens from the stretching of the scrotum, which is in the groin, as we said. Which is because the scrotum stretches: then it becomes soft and a boil forms, and swells.
And its cure is with a cautery, as was previously mentioned about it.
And sometimes it is cured with iron, according to this method: and it is, that the patient lies on his back between your hands. Then cut the area of the rupture's boil and swelling, with an incision through its width, according to the size of three fingers.
Then tie the alsifak and those that are under the skin until the white alsifak is uncovered, which is under the skin following it.
Then take a radium and place it on top of the prominent part from the alsifak, and press it deep into the belly.
Then take two prominent parts from the alsifak and place them on either side of the extremity of the radium, and connect one to the other with a suture; then withdraw the extremity of the radium.
And do not cut off the alsifak entirely, nor stretch the skin, nor anything else, as I have taught you in the cure of intestinal hernias.
Then care for that wound with a wound treatment. When therefore you cut the threads, clean that and restore the wound until it is consolidated.
For the sisch is contracted and no prominence of rupture is added.
And cauterization is better in this place, because it is closer to health.
Chapter 68.
A treatise on the softening of the skin of the testicles.
The skin of the testicles is often softened in some people, because of which the flesh that is between them becomes soft and their appearance is marred.
Therefore it is necessary for the one who is asked to cure this to make the patient lie on his back and cut off all the softened skin that is over the natural skin.
Then gather two flaps with a suture: and if you wish, add a surplus of the softened skin in three or four sutures, and make the suture firm.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then care for the remaining wounds with a treatment until it heals, and the threads fall away.
Chapter 69.
A treatise on Castration.
You should know that Castration is excommunicated by our law: and because of this, I would have been allowed not to recall it in my book, and it is not necessary to recall it unless for two reasons.
One of which is that a physician should know when he is questioned about it, and how to treat one who suffers from it.
And another method is that we often need to perform castration on certain animals for our benefit through them, such as sheep, wild goats, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore I say that castration is done in two ways. Or through emasculation. Or through incision and cutting.
In the one that is done through emasculation, the method of operation is this: we make the animal sit in hot water until its testicles become soft and relaxed, and then we cut them off with your hand, until they dissolve and do not appear under the touch.
In the castration done through incision and cutting, it is necessary to hold the animal firmly and press with your left hand on the skin of its testicles.
Then bind the suspensory ligament and cut above one end of the section, until two testicles come forth: then cut them after you have removed both, and do not leave anything except for a thin skin that is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than the one done through emasculation, because emasculation perhaps leaves something of the testicles, which is why the animal desires to copulate.
Then take care of the wound until it heals.
Chapter 70.
On the Treatment of a Hermaphrodite in Two Ways.
A hermaphrodite is formed from males in two ways. One of them is, because it appears as if there is a space, or in the skin of the testicles, between two testicles, with a figure resembling a woman's vulva, with hair: and sometimes urine flows from what is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is a kind, and it is the vulva of a woman over the pubis bone just like that of a small boy's penis entirely, protruding to the exterior: one of which is like a man's priapus, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of three kinds, two from men and one from women, is: because it is necessary to cut away excess flesh until the impression of those parts disappears. Then the care for these wounds is by the same cure as for other wounds.
However, in the second kind of men's genitals from which urine emerges, which is on the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor its cure at all.
Chapter 71.
On the cutting of the clitoris and the flesh protruding in women's vulvae.
The clitoris is perhaps added to the natural organ until it becomes foul and its appearance is repugnant; and sometimes it grows large in certain women to such an extent that it expands like a man's penis, and reaches as far as copulation.
Therefore you must grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or a hook and cut it away; and do not linger too long in cutting, especially at the root of it: lest bleeding occur.
Then care for it by the same cure as for wounds, until it heals.
However, the flesh that arises is the flesh that grows in the maternal orifice until it fills it; and perhaps it emerges to the exterior according to the likeness of a tail: and on account of this some call it a caudate affliction. Therefore you must cut it away just as we cut away the clitoris, and care for it until it heals.
On the cure of the Alraticia, which is an unbroken vulva of a woman.
Chapter 72.
The Alraticia is an unbroken vulva of a woman or one with a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is from the preceding illness: and it itself comes about either from innate flesh added on, or sifach subtle, or spisso: and it occurs either in the depth of the matrix, or in its sides, or above it, or below it: and it prevents copulation and conception, and childbirth, and perhaps menstruation.
And knowledge of this is obtained through the observation of a midwife if the obstruction appears to be nearby.
But if it is not apparent, then search for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is from subtle sifach and is near two lips: then hasten and tear it apart.
And you should place poultices on both lips.
Then place your fingers of both hands together, and the woman should be placed on her back with her legs spread apart. Then stretch open the two lips with force until the subtle sifach is torn apart and the obstruction is opened.
Then take a piece of wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the spot; and let the woman sit every day so that the place does not harden again.
But if the sifach is thick and dense: then it is necessary to cut it with a spatula similar to a leaf of myrtle.
But if the obstruction is from innate flesh, then suspend it with a hook and cut it open: and be accompanied by medicines that calm the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and lily-aggregate with owl's white.
Then administer a leaden cannula, wide, so that the wound does not quickly become inflamed, and keep it for several days: and then apply linseed from dry flax, and take care of the remaining treatment until it is healed.
And when the birth of other flesh occurs in the womb: it is necessary therefore that they be cut according to this method: while not an apostematic cancer exists apostemata cancerosa are not made contrary with iron entirely.
Chapter 73.
On the care of hemorrhoids, Athelila's and Bothor's red boils on women's faces.
Hemorrhoids indeed are the opening of venous openings, until much blood flows out of them constantly.
When therefore hemorrhoids are aged, they become thickened; and I said previously in the division of their kinds and signs; and I will say here which of them receive treatment and which do not.
I say therefore that hemorrhoids which are in the vaginal opening and do not appear to the sense: then there is no cure for them with iron.
Those however that are in the vaginal opening above which sense falls are those that are cured.
Therefore one should put the woman into a cold house. Then extend Athelila with forceps or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then powderize over them at the flow of blood from one cut powder without cauterization: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and thistle, and similar things.
Then push her feet against the wall for an hour. Afterwards put her into cold water.
What if the flow of blood persists: then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and walnut bark, and galls, and similar things.
Then take barley flour and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a poultice with it on her back.
If therefore the blood is cut off with this, it is good; and if not, make poultices stick to her back, and breasts, without cauterization.
Then take wool and immerse it in the juice of tarar, or the juice of the tongue-licked reed, that is, aglantine, Pontic wine, and make it stick to the wound.
When therefore the swelling subsides, then care for the place with ointments until it is healed.
The albarmuth, however, is red and resembles the heads of hedgehogs, and has a rough appearance.
Therefore it is necessary to cut off what appears from it, according to what I said in equal proportions; and care for it until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
A passage about perforation of the uterus which occurs in the womb.
Many kinds of swelling occur in the womb, such as cancer, stone-like swelling, ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is, herpes, abscesses, fistulas, hemorrhoids, ragadiae, and thelils, and hot swelling.
