Therefore it is necessary that you consider. Then if the pain of the swelling in its beginning is sharp with percussion and heat and fever, and you see it red; if sensation falls upon it, then do not hasten with perforation of it; and care for it along with it as long as it is beneficial for maturation, until the acuteness of the accident subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a chair which has two armrests, and recline or lean back against her back, and gather her feet to the lower part of her belly; and her hips should be spread apart, and she should put her arms under her knees, and bind each one with suitable binding to it.
Then the midwife will sit on the right side and administer the instrument with which the opening of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you insert it with the radius deep into the woman's womb: so that what enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the womb, and does not harm the patient.
But if the instrument is longer than the depth, then it is necessary to place pillows under the covering of the depth of the womb: so that the entrance of the entire instrument into the depth of the womb may be prevented. And it is necessary to place a cushion which runs along the instrument on the upper side; and to hold the instrument and to have the servant turn the cushion, until the depth of the womb is opened.
When therefore the Exitura appears and is touched by hand and is soft and smooth: then it is necessary to cut it at its head with a broad spatula.
When therefore the lochia is evacuated, then it is necessary to place a lint soaked in rose oil or green oil, in which there is some stimulating quality, into the wound; and put the lint, and draw out the section in the depth of the womb.
And place it externally on the womb, and on the back a clean woolen cloth soaked in water in which mulberries have been boiled.
Then care for it after two days with an ointment that is suitable for it, until it heals.
And when the womb and wound are washed, so that they are anointed with water and honey: then with water in which lily root or long aristolochia has been boiled; then return to the ointment.
But if the wound is hidden internally in the womb, then it is necessary to refrain from treating it with iron, rather cure it with that which we said in the division.
Head 70th.
Teachings of Obstetrics, how midwives should treat living embryos, according to their unnatural form that has emerged.
It is necessary for the obstetrician to know first of all the natural form of birth.
Therefore the sign of it is, that when you see a woman compressed downwards and desires to breathe air, and is relieved above what is above her in childbirth, and hastens the exit of the embryo: then you will know that this birth is natural and its issue is over its head, and the umbilical cord with it, either suspended or hanging from the navel.
When therefore you see these signs, then it is necessary to compress her belly so that the embryo may come down quickly.
For when it descends over its head, the umbilical cord also descends with it, and is purged from those excessences by a good purgation.
But whoever is outside of this birth is unnatural, unpraiseworthy.
For sometimes the fetus emerges under its own feet, and its body emerges before its head, and its feet and hands together, or one foot: or it emerges with its head and hands; or it emerges ventrally; and perhaps it turns over onto its neck, and similar things from unnatural forms.
It is necessary therefore for the obstetrician to be prudent and subtle in all these things, and to beware of error and deception: and I am showing every form among them, and how it is a sign in it, and what it signifies by that, and stands over it.
When the fetus emerges over its own head in its natural birth, and that thing becomes strong above the woman, and childbirth becomes difficult for her, and you see its strength already weakened: then make her sit on a seat, or women hold her; and warm her abdomen with a decoction of fenugreek and with moist oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small wooden spatula between her fingers and finds with it a second one, and scrapes it with her own nail, until what is inside from moisture flows out; and presses the woman's womb, until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then it is necessary that the woman be cleansed with fenugreek mucilage mixed with sesame oil.
Then instruct her to be compressed after the enema; and make her cough with a condiment, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will exit immediately.
And if the fetus's hands emerge first, it is necessary to reduce them with ease and gradually. But if it does not obey during the introduction, then place the woman on a chair and lift her feet up; afterwards, strike the chair against the ground: and the woman must be held so that she does not fall with the shock.
So if the hands do not emerge and the fetus dies: then cut them, and extract the remaining fetus: and bind its hand with a cloth, and extract it evenly: for it will come out.
Exit of the embryo on its feet. When the fetus emerges over its own feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards; afterwards, turn the fetus slightly; afterwards, straighten it.
Then take one of his feet and stretch it out slightly.
When they emerge up to the hips, then instruct her to be compressed, and make her cough with a condiment: it will come out, if God wills.
And if it does not emerge as we have narrated, then reduce the fetus gradually, until you place it in its natural shape. For it will come out then with easy exit.
But if it is obstructed in all that we have narrated: then take a mixture of alum, and sesame oil, and gum solution: and strike the whole thing well with a mortar; afterwards, bind it to the woman's womb, and below her lower abdomen. Then make her sit in warm water, until hypothermia is reached.
When therefore you see that already the lower parts of hers are warmed, then make her sit in myrrh: and place it on. When however she holds the poultice for one hour, then make her sit down. Then make her cough: and bind her mouth and nose: and press the lower part of the belly with easy compression. For fire will come out immediately.
The exit of the fetus above the knees and hands. When it exits in this shape, then she will recover by gradually inserting her hands. Then the woman should be laid back on her neck, and her feet should hang down below: and she herself should lie on the bed, and wrap her hands around. Then press above her belly gradually. Then remove what is under her feet from the bedclothes, until she is prepared in the lower part. What if the fetus does not exit, then take her feet and move them with a strong motion. Then press what is above hypothermia gradually, until the fetus ascends to the higher parts. Then she should insert her hands and equalize the fetus gradually: and instruct the woman to compress until the fetus exits.
The exit of the fetus hanging breadthwise from the hands. She will recover by reducing her hands. But if she cannot reduce it, then lift up the woman and make her walk.
But if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed: and strike her legs with a strong shaking.
Then administer the ointment that you prepared from mucus.
What if she accepts the birth, then make her sit on a seat. Then strive to reduce the swelling of the hand, and shape it according to its natural form. And make a woman sneeze, and tell her to press down on herself until she expels it.
The exit of the fetus over its mother's shoulder, with outstretched hands, and its face turned towards its mother's back. It is necessary for the obstetrician to hold the fetus' hand, then gradually turn it over and adjust it with ease.
When it is adjusted, then place over its mouth what we have previously narrated, made from oil and mucus.
Then tell her to move slightly. When you have done that, then make her sit down on a seat and tell her to lean to the right side. Then tell her to press down, and make her sneeze with condiment.
The exit of the prepared fetus over the side. When it descends into the mother's opening according to this method with a cord, then cut the cord with your finger, until it is evacuated from moisture.
If it is suspended over the face and neck of the fetus, then cut it off from the navel so that it does not remain attached and dies.
Then push the fetus inside until you place it as needed, and tell the woman to press down a few times: for indeed it will emerge easily.
The exit of twins, or many fetuses. You should know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four are born. However, this is a rare occurrence and does not often happen.
But five is something outside of nature and they do not live at all; and it contains them, although they may be multiplied, with one cord: and separates them through a siphac, distinguishing between each one bound by its own umbilical cord.
And sometimes they go out like a single fetus goes out naturally and easily; and they come out with an unhappy outcome, just as we narrated.
But if some of them are difficult during birth and arrive at what we mentioned in unnatural forms, then cure them as we narrated: and let the obstetrician be subtle, and do what is done with ease and slowness, and shape according to every form with what is prepared for it from a guiding ingenuity towards salvation.
A reminder of what is formed in the womb from embryos, and those that are aborted.
Sometimes one is formed in the womb, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and more than ten: and it has been verified with me, that a woman having an abortion has expelled seven, and others fifteen, all of them formed.
What has been done through the measurement of a glorious wise person.
Therefore, this should be entirely in the knowledge of an obstetrician. Perhaps indeed it will come to her similar to that one: let her oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
A treatise on the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you care for a dead fetus as I said in the division of medication, and it does not come out, and you are forced to use an operation with your hand, then you should consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound virtue and is not afflicted by any of the accidents from which one fears loss over her: then it is necessary that the woman be placed on a bed behind her back, with her head inclined downward and her legs raised; and let someone support her on both sides, or be bound to the bed, so that her body may not be pulled away during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with oily liquids mixed with aloes and fenugreek seeds, and linseed.
Then the midwife anoints her hand with those oils and mucus-like substances; then she inserts it into the womb with ease. Then he seeks with her the place where he will grasp the umbilical cords of the fetus, and the suitable places for that.
Therefore consider then, if the fetus is prepared to come out over its own head: then grasp the umbilical cords at his eyes, or on his neck, or on his mouth, or on his palate, or under his beard, or in the crease of his skin, or in nearby places along the ribs, and under the armpits.
But if the descent is over his feet, then in the flank and middle of his ribcage.
And it is necessary to hold the cord with the right hand; and place its curvature between the fingers of the left hand; and insert it with ease, and fix the cord in one of those places which we said, until it reaches the empty space.
Then fix another or third cord opposite to it, if necessary; so that the contraction is equal and does not lean towards one side.
Then it should be extended with equal extension, and not just straight ahead, but rather move in all directions to facilitate its exit, as happens when a tooth is being extracted.
And it is necessary between that, that the extension be softened. And if a part is retained from that, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with oil and insert them from one side to turn back what is being retained.
And if a part of the fetus emerges, then the cords should be moved to another higher location: do this until the entire fetus has emerged.
And if the arm emerges first and it is not possible to bring it back due to its contraction: then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped over it. Then take it out until the entire thing emerges, cut it at either the joint of the foot or at the elbow; and so you should do with the other hand and two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or if there is a constriction at birth, or if water has gathered on its head; then it is necessary to insert between the fingers a spathula with thorns, and cut with it the head, and draw out the water: or break it off with an instrument called Almisdac, whose shape comes along with the shapes of other instruments in the chapter that follows.
He should do similarly if the fetus has a natural large head; then it is necessary to remove the afterbirth: or take it out with a misdac, as we said. Then grasp the bones with forceps.
But if the head emerges and is constricted at the neck; then it should be cut, until the moisture that is in the chest flows out.
But if it does not unfold or comply with birth; then cut the head in pieces, as much as you can.
But if the lower part of the abdomen is apostematic, or there is dropsy within; then it is necessary to puncture it, until the moisture flows out from it.
But if the fetus is above its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction towards the mother's mouth is light.
But if it is constricted in the belly or chest; then it is necessary to take it out with a cloth that you wrapped around your hand: and cut the belly or chest, until what is within them flows out.
But if the remaining limbs are removed, and the head returns; then the obstetrician should insert their left hand. So if the mother's mouth is open, then insert your hand into the depth of the uterus and seek out the head with it, and grasp it with your fingers up to the mother's mouth: then insert one or two hooks into it, and pull it out with it.
If the womb's opening is obstructed due to a hot abscess that has occurred to it: then one should not work on it, but rather administer a flowing of moist things, fatty substances. And apply poultices. And have them sit in waters that soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, then it is possible to adjust it; then what we said for a living fetus should be administered.
If that is not possible, then cut quickly and extract it.
And one should not leave anything of the afterbirth entirely inside.
When you have completed your operation, then administer what we said for hot abscesses that occur in wombs.
If there occurs bleeding, then let it flow into cooling waters, and administer the remaining treatment.
And I myself have already seen a woman whose womb contained a dead fetus; then she became pregnant with another one in its place, and again the other fetus died. Then an abscess occurred to her after a long time in her navel, and it swelled until it was opened, and began to discharge pus.
So I was called upon for its treatment, and I treated her for a long time; and the wound did not heal.
So I placed on it some poultices of strong attraction, and the bone came out of its place. Then a few days passed for her, and another bone came out. I was amazed therefore, since there is no bone in the womb.
I thought therefore, because they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
I therefore examined the wound and extracted many bones from her, while she was in better condition than before; and now after a long time has passed, she is sending forth pus from the site.
And I did not confirm this foreign thing here unless there is knowledge and assistance for what the medical craftsman administers with his hand in healing.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
About the shapes of necessary instruments in extracting a fetus.
The shape of the Handle by which the womb is opened.
Here is the shape of the Straightener, with which books are adjusted, having two handles at the two ends of two woods. However, this handle ought to be subtler than the Straightener; and it should be made either of ebony or of busse wood.
And the width of all wood should be almost two fingers; and the edge of each of them is almost one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and half; and in the middle of the two woods are two additions made of the same kind of wood, which are already fixed on both sides, whose length is half a palm and a little more; and the width of each is almost two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are introduced into the maternal opening, so that it may be opened with them when you revolve the two Handles.
Another form similar to that one, subtler and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony wood or busse wood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at the end of it there should be two additions, just as you see here above: and the length of all these additions is almost a palm; and the width of each one is two fingers.
When therefore you wish to open the womb with this, then make her sit on a bed, with her feet hanging down, and the opening between her thighs. Then introduce these two additions joined together into the womb; and you are holding the end of the instrument below between her hips. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with forceps, equally according to the quantity which you wish from opening the mouth of the matrix, until the midwife does what she wants.

The form of a hook having one spine.
The form of a hook having two spines.
The form of Spatumilis having two extremities for cutting off the head.
The form of two wide Spatumilii.
All these instruments are exercised and the more their kinds multiply and are prepared by the craftsman, the swifter they are for their operation and higher among people, because of their quantity.
Therefore do not despise any of these instruments, but let it be prepared with you. For the necessity of them is inexcusable.
Chapter 78.
Of the Extraction of the Secondina.
When the Secondina is retained at birth, then it is necessary to hasten the weak [or feeble] so that it may retain its breathing. Then make her sneeze with what you have condensed: and bind your hand over her mouth, and nose itself. If it therefore goes out through this, it is well.
If not: then take a pot, and pierce in the cover a hole; and put into it herbs opening the womb, such as calament, rue, and anise, and chamomile, and sassafras, and cedar wood, and centaury. Take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them in water, and put them over fire.
Then put the reed's gum on top of the hole in the pot's cover, and another end into the womb; and hold it until the steam reaches the drying of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For indeed the Secundina will come out with ease.
But if it remains after this treatment and does not come out: then instruct the midwife to dip her hand in sesame oil or aloe juice. Then insert her hand into the womb, and search for the Secundina with it. When she has found it, then take it and stretch it out gradually.
But if it is attached deeply within the womb, then insert your hand, as we have described, until you find the Secundina and pull it out gradually straight ahead, so that the womb does not fall under strong attraction. Rather, it should be pulled with ease to the right and left sides: then add it in proportion to the attraction. For then it will obey, and be saved, and depart from its adhesion.
And if the womb is constricted, as we have already described: cure it with sneezing and by means of the pot's medicine with herbs.
But if it does not come out with all that we have described, then beware lest you tire yourself over her in repeated extractions.
True, it is necessary to bind what comes out from the womb to the woman's hip. Then anoint it with the tetrapharmaco ointment. For this will corrupt it after a few days and dissolve it, and it will be eliminated.
But when it putsrefies, a bad smell arises from it to the stomach and head, and injures that sick person. Therefore suitable fumigations should be administered to it.
However some of the ancients have already experienced the fumigation of tansy and dried figs.
The shape of the instrument with which a woman is fumigated during the retention of the menses and secondines.
Make it from a piece of vitriol similar to alcama, or from cinnabar; and place the broad end on the fire: and let the fumigation be placed above the coals; hold it until that fumigation is consumed, and then repeat another.
Chapter 79.
On the healing of an unperforated hernia.
Many boys are born with unperforated hernias, which have been bound by the thin membrane of sifac. Therefore it is necessary that the midwife perforates that membrane with her finger: and if not, she should perforate it with a sharp spatula; and beware of getting a burn, lest she touch him.
Then place over it a cloth soaked in wine and oil. Then care for it with ointments until it is healed.
But if you fear that it will strangle, then place a lead tube into the hole and leave it for many days, and remove it when the child wants to urinate.
And also hernia occurs from the consolidation of a wound or an abscess. Therefore it is necessary to cut open that consolidation, and then it should be healed with what we said earlier about healing and lead.
Here is the translation:
On the healing of fistulas which occur in the lower part.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part are Nodation and Grossitude which occur near the anus outside or in a space of one part. And it is a fistula, one or more.
When therefore that Nodation becomes old, it opens and a white watery moisture or thin pus flows out from it.
And sometimes these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore the penetrating ones are recognized by what comes out through stool and vomiting when an infirm person uses a toilet: and perhaps a worm comes out from them.
And there are fistulas among these when they are penetrating into the space up to the bladder and up to the urethra. And there are fistulas among these that penetrate as far as the joint of the hip and as far as the node of the tail.
But from this by which you can know whether the fistula penetrates to the anus or not, is when you insert your index finger into the anus and also insert a thin probe made of air or iron into the fistula, when there is no twisting.
And if there is twisting in it, then insert into it a thin probe made of lead or a brush made of horsehair, until you feel the probe or brush with your finger.
And if you do not feel it thoroughly, nor does anything come out from the orifice through stool, vomiting, or worm as I said: then you will know that it is not penetrating: therefore hasten to treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrating to the bladder or to the urethra: then its significance is the flow of urine from it and prevention of this so that the place is consolidated with medicines.
If however it is penetrating to the joint of the hip or to the socket of the hip: then its sign is that the probe reaches there if there is no twisting up to the socket: and finding pain around the part, and pus always coming out from it: and that medicine does not work in it, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And for these fistulas that penetrate everywhere, there is no cure at all. And the cure of them is toil and futility for foolish physicians, who are formed against him.
But for those that are not penetrating and not old, there is hope of a cure with a probe: according to what I tell you, and I have already tried it; and thus the sick man lies between your hands on his back: and lift up his legs as far above his hip-bones, and let his hip-bones be inclined towards his belly. Then insert a lead or air probe into the fistula, if there is curvature in it, until you know where it reaches.
But if the sick man feels it around his anus, then it is necessary for you to insert your index finger into the anus.
Therefore, if you feel with your finger that the probe has already penetrated by itself and detected, besides what you feel between your finger and the skin or flesh: then you are certain to know that it is penetrating. Therefore, do not trouble yourself about it, since there is no cure for it, as we said.
However, it is said that the flesh is healed in some people through medication and the benefit that is hoped for from it. The method is to apply a fine cautery iron according to the width of the fistula, as previously mentioned, and insert the burning iron into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat that twice or three times until you know that all those charred and filtered flesh pieces, which are similar to a bird's feather canal, have been completely consumed.
Then treat that with linen bandages wrapped in butter until those charred flesh pieces emerge: then treat it with ointments that produce flesh and consolidate.
If it is healed, it is good.
And if not, then it will never be cured with any other treatment.
And if it protrudes out and does not reach up to your finger that is in the anus, and there is a thick skin or membrane of flesh between it and the protrusion or fistula: and you see a fistula on the surface of the skin, then cut the skin at the beginning of the fistula, and you go cutting with the tenta that is in the fistula, until the incision reaches where the end of the tenta ends, and the tenta is released and falls.
Then cleanse those filtered flesh parts, which are similar to a bird's feather, so that nothing remains of them.
But if blood overcomes you and prevents you from operating between yourself and the operation, so that you must cut those flesh parts: then it is better to do so and provide more rapid relief by cauterizing it with fire or with sharp medicine.
For the cauterizing with fire brings together good habits, adds dryness to those added flesh parts, and absorbs blood and dries up moisture.
Then care for that with linen cloths wrapped in butter or crushed cauls, until the place discharges pus, and those dry flesh parts emerge into pus.
Then care for the wound with consolidating ointments that produce hard flesh, and there are medicines that have astringency and drying properties.
For when it is restored, then it is already healed; and you should not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula has already reached to the depth of the anus and is distant from the surplus of the body: then insert your finger into the anus and investigate with it.
So if you feel the tenta and between you and it is a membrane of flesh or skin, and it is near to penetration: then there is no operation in that case, unless according to the way of faith and hope.
Which is because it is administered in one of three ways: either you cauterize it as we said: or cut it until you reach its concavity, so that it becomes possible for you to insert a linen and effect a cure from nearby. And do not proceed too much with cutting, so that you do not cut the arm containing a vein, and a bad outcome of evacuation occurs without will. Then take care of it as we have said.
Perhaps it will be healed; or stretch the skin over it and cleanse it after you have cut it up to the vein, as we said, or with a tent, or with another sharp instrument. Then cleanse those filtered flesh parts that are in the cutting, as far as you can with probing.
Then attempt to restore the wound of the entire section and the upper opening where it is consolidated: and a fistula will remain open on the inside part of the anus, and it will be easier for the weak one.
And sometimes the fistula is suitable according to this method: and it is when you insert a tent into the fistula: and it is on the side of the anus, around the surface of the body with skin and the margin of the anus: then take the perforated extremity of the tent, which is like an awl, that is, preparing skins, according to this form. And insert a thread wrapped with five threads into it, and around it. Then insert the tent with the thread into the fistula, until it reaches its concavity.
If then it is piercing the margin of the anus on the inside near, then pull out the thread from that opening: so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and gather both ends together: and tie each one, and leave it for one or two days: and as much as the thread goes into the flesh, tie it well, until those skins which are between the two ends of the thread come to be cut and fall: then take care of the wound, until it is consolidated and healed.
If however the opening of the fistula is not piercing: then make it pierce somehow as you have prepared for yourself, except if it is very deep. You don't have it so that you would do that on account of the elbow, so as not to cut it.
Then do with her as we said, until she heals.
The shape of the Spatholimus spine is as follows: it has a very acute curvature and one side is not acute, so that you do not cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter 81.
On the suture of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows after an incision has been made in them, and on the cure of fissures.
There are hemorrhoids in two ways. Either they are within the anus, resembling charcoal vesicles, or rather they are like clusters of grapes. And there are small ones and large ones; and blood flows from them always, and it is one, and there are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are for the most part few in moisture, from which yellowish water or a little blood flows with constant flow, and they are according to the color of the body.
And the cure for that which is within the anus is, to cause a patient to sit and strain, until it comes out through the anus, and appear to you as an atonement. Therefore and suspend them with a hook; or hold them with your finger. Then cut them at their roots.
But if they are not retained by hooks, because of the moisture in them and their softness: then take them with a rough cloth or with a strip of coarse linen, and extract them with your fingers. Then cut them and powderize some hot powders over them after the incision, so that they may fill their place as a cauterizing agent. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment until they are healed.
If the patient does not obey the stool's exit, then use an enema on the sick person with a small amount of medication, so that they may be cleaned out with what is in their anus, and quickly obey the stool when the patient expresses themselves.
But if they are external from the anus, then their condition is light, easy; and it is as if you would take them with your finger, or take them with a pincer, and cut them off. Then treat them in the manner we have said until they are healed.
And whoever shuns cutting with iron, then it is necessary that you apply a stitch to them according to this method. Take the wrapped thread and insert it into the needle. Then pull the thread out to the top and make it penetrate with the needle into the root of it on one side: and wrap two ends of the thread around the lower part of the needle, and be lateral; and tie the athelil tightly strong. Then knot the thread and pull out the needle.
Do that with all the athelil, and leave one; do not bind it so that excess blood does not flow from it.
Then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or safflower oil over the anus, and place it on top of the anus; and instruct the patient to rest. Then let it be, until it falls off.
When therefore the athelil fall off, treat them with ointments and the rest of the treatment until they are healed.
Of the Treatment of Fissures. For many times fissures occur due to constipation and natural constriction.
When therefore they become old and medicine is not effective in them: then it is necessary that you scrape with a spoon or your finger until they become moist, and remove the upper crust that prevents them from consolidating. Then take care of these until they are consoled according to what is necessary.
But if they do not consolidate: then repeat them with a more vigorous reason than the first, until they bleed much, and then take care of them, until they heal.
Of the cure of Almirmas of turned men, and not turned men: and Aethelils sores, and Formicas.
Chapter 82.
Know that Almirmar is nothing but a rounded knot according to the color of the body, like the head of a key: and it occurs throughout the whole body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes: and it happens from them pain at walking.
Therefore, what is around the head of the key should be cut, and held with forceps or a hook, and pulled out from its root.
Then take care of that thing; and if you wish, cauterize it as was done previously in its own chapter.
From Aethelils sores, one is a small, rough, filtered, rounded prominence above the surface of the body. And it often occurs in the hands of boys.
And its cure is to stretch out the Aethelil, and cut it: or tie it with a silk thread: or with a hair, until it falls. And if you wish, cauterize it as was previously mentioned with fire or with sharp medicine: and now the Aethelils, both those that are turned and not turned, will be eradicated with the instrument that I shall say.
Formica is also a small, filtered, rough prominence above the surface of the body, going down very deeply into the skin. And when it finds cold, there is felt in it like the sting of a flea, and it occurs throughout the whole body: and most of it occurs in the hands.
And its cure is to take a tube made from the feather of a goose or a strong vulture's wing: and place that tube over the Formica, until it contains a circle of the parts of it. Then revolve your hand with a catheter, until you cut out what is in the circumference of the ant: and put it in deep flesh, and uproot the ant from its root.
And if you wish, do that with a hollow tube of air or iron, according to this shape.
Sit the upper end of the catheter above, drawing it upwards to unwind the numbness wrapped around, so that its relaxation and rotation may be felt on the grasping finger.
And if you wish, cauterize the ant according to what is mentioned before in the cauterization of Atthelia in the chapter on cauterizing.
Chapter 83.
A chapter on the forms of instruments that are used for enemas in ailments of the bladder, and for the purgation of the bowels, and for colic.
Sometimes an enema is made from silver, or from golden bronze, or from melted air, or malleted.
And sometimes it is made from these instruments, small and great, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore that which is administered in the treatment of small children, let it be small: and whose bladders are weak or painful, so also their enemas.
Great Enema.
Its tube, that is, the upper head where the womb is tied up, be broad according to this shape: and let there be a distinction so that the womb may be tied as you see, and its lower extremity which enters the anus, be mute, that is, not open, gentle, very soft, drawing down to subtlety: and on one side of it be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And let the width of the hole be according to the thickness of the threads, and slightly thick, and let the womb in which medicines are placed from animal bladder: or from a sheep's parchment, which is according to the form of an asparagus stalk: whose root is one palm.

Chapter 44 of the Head.
Now I have spoken in the division of wound treatment about what was fitting for the book, and I will say here the treatment of those wounds with hand and with medicines according to need.
I say therefore that wounds are differentiated according to what causes the wound and according to the place on which the wound falls.
Therefore, the causes of wounds are often like a thrown stone: either from a knife's cutting or a razor's cut; or from a blow with a lance, wood, or arrow, and similar things from many other objects.
But wounds that are caused by locations on the body are, such as a wound that falls on the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or hip: and similar ones from those other members.
And I am telling the treatments for some wounds, in order to set forth a reason and rule over the remaining wounds.
And I begin with simple head wounds, since the recollection of compound ones will come at the beginning of chapter three.
I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs on the head and is not accompanied by bone fracture: consider then whether it was caused by a thrown stone or something similar, and only has broken the skin; and if it's a large wound and you fear a bad outcome from suppuration, then hasten to perform a cephalic bloodletting immediately, and don't delay; and take out of his blood according to the quantity of its virtue: unless some obstacle prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood flowing out of the wound is scant. And let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the patient beware of fullness, and wine, and heavy foods.
But if something prevents cutting off, then let less be taken from food, and let him not approach flesh nor wine.
And place on the wound if a warm abscess occurs to him, a poultice infused in rose oil alone, or with wine having purging properties.
But if you are free from a warm abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent and still with its own blood, and has not been disturbed by air: and that powder which I will tell you afterwards; and if it has already been disturbed by air, then place some ointments upon it until it heals.
But if the wound is large, or is from an incision with a sword, and similar: and its edges are not brought together by poutlices, then bring them together with suture, according to what I will tell you about the sutures of their wounds in the belly.
But if the wound is already detected by bone and hangs, and there is no retention of it except with a small sling: then cut it. Then care for the wound with medicines and restore hard flesh to its place under the skin. And especially instruct the patient to eat foods that are heavy, such as heads and cooked wheat, until he is like a pulse: and similar things.
What if you find putrefaction on the skin and it does not adhere to the bone: then cut out the putrefaction entirely and throw it away. Then care for that one.
But if there is an artery or vein from which blood flows in these wounds, and it is not cut off by medicines, then inquire about the artery. So if you find it not severed, then sever it with a Spatulum or tie it. What if necessity demands when what we said does not help, then cauterize it until the blood is cut off.
If however these wounds are small, simple, then the remedy for them is easy, light: so that you may sprinkle powder over them this powder because it consolidates them before they change with air.
Mode of the Powdered Incarnate. Recipe: Take one part of olibanum, two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of unextinguished lime. Grind it well and sift it through a sieve. Fill the wound with this mixture and bind it tightly until it adheres well to the skin and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind the wound above with lint, and let it be, nor loose it until it remains sound, so that it does not suppurate. For this medicine is not to be removed from the wound until it has healed.
And sometimes the lime alone does this work when you do not have olibanum and dragon's blood in front of you. And it also performs the same operation on many large wounds when it is suitable to be placed on them and bound up, and they are recent wounds with their own blood which has not been altered by air.
But if the air has already altered them in some way, especially during the time of summer, then there is no intention to use this powder: therefore, care for it with what we have said, so that you place over it certain digested ointments or put on it poultices made from barley flour mixed with water and honey until they discharge pus. Then care for the rest of the cure, until they are healed.
If, however, a fracture occurs with the wound in a bone and it is small, then extract that with Gesta: and I will now speak of the cure for fractures of the head, as future.
The Wounds of the Neck.
If a wound occurs on the neck then there is no difference between it and a wound of the head when it is simple.
If however the nerve of the neck has already been cut, or the artery: then if it is a nerve, there is no art in it except to remove the cure of it with what causes it to make a strong stiptication, such as ziniar, and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm nerves, and contract them.
And do not put anything cold whatsoever on the wound: because the substance of nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is a higher part of the members: and that which cures it should be from gentle medicines, such as lime washed with sweet water several times infused with oil, or with rose oil, and tuthia, when it is also washed with sweet water: and other mineral stones, according to this method, and moist ointments.
If however the wound is large, then administer a suture: or bind its edges with lint. Then take care of it until it heals.
If the wound has a depth or occurs on the lower part of it where pus has already accumulated, then puncture it in a more depressed area.
And if an artery has already been cut in the wound and its flow of blood has followed: then separate it, or cut it off, and tie it off: or cauterize it, if necessity demands that.
If however the vertebrae of the neck have already been cut, or the whole thing, and the ligaments are saved: then bring together the edges of the skin with a suture over a canna of the neck, and do not touch the neck itself, but rather equalize it, and return it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a bandage that is fitting, and let it go for several days. Then take care of it with ointments in which there is stiptication and drying, until it heals.
If however the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then search for it; then if there are fragments of bone within it, remove all of them or as many as you can and leave what is not prepared to rot until the wound becomes putrid, separates, and its discharge subsides.
If the wound has been recent, then powder the surface of it with the powder I have described. But if a bone appears in the wound and a fistula forms, then treat it with that remedy which is mentioned in its own chapter.
On Wounds of the Knives.
If there is a puncture from a lance or dagger, and you see its depth, then consider it. If therefore pus is discharged from the wound and a sick person breathes heavily because of it, then you will know that the wound is mortal.
What if there is no great depth and it is recent with its own blood? Then do not put powder on it from the first hour, nor bind it up, so that the blood may not be retained in its depth and turn back to the heart and kill the sick person: but put a attracting ointment on it and open it twice a day.
But if you do not have an ointment present, then put old lint into the opening of the wound, so that it may suck up what discharges from it through moisture: and make the sick person sleep over the wound, so that what forms in it may flow out.
But if already three days or more have passed since the wound occurred, and it does not happen to the sick person that he has a spasm, nor a bad trembling of the heart, nor constriction in his breathing, and you see its other conditions are good: then you will know that the wound is sound. Therefore heal it with liniments and all other healing remedies until it is healed.
If indeed his healing is delayed and it always opens up, then you should know that already a fistula has formed. So cure him according to his chapter.
And if the wound is from a cut made with a sword or dagger and it is simple on the surface of the chest or back: then cure him by sewing it up as before if it is large; or with powder if it is small.
But if he has already made an impression on the bone and fragments have fallen out: then examine the wound and hurry to remove those fragments if they are separate. If they are not separate, then leave them alone until the wound becomes putrid, for their removal would only be an annoyance.
As for other accidents of wounds in other limbs, the judgment in treatment is that which we have mentioned.
On Wounds of the Belly and Exit of Intestines and their Issues.
The disruption that occurs in the belly is great, moderate, and small.
Therefore from a great disruption sometimes the intestine or the number of intestines goes forth.
Wherefore their introduction and suture is more violent and difficult.
And a small disruption also becomes difficult in another way. Therefore, it follows that their reduction becomes more difficult if you do not hurry to introduce them into their place immediately, since they are inflated, opened, and swollen; therefore the entrance of them becomes difficult.
Therefore, it follows that a medium-sized disruption is better: since with it the reduction of the intestine does not become difficult, as it does in these two cases.
Know that the suture of the belly is according to four modes.
Two common sutures meet in the suture of the belly and in the suture of other wounds of the body; and two other sutures properly communicate with other wounds. However, these are more proper with stitches for the belly; and I will tell you each one with explanation and declaration.
I say therefore, because when a wound is small and something from the intestine protrudes from it, and it becomes difficult to reduce it then, that is due to one of two methods. Either because of the narrowness of the disruption, as we said: or because there is inflation within the intestine due to cold air.
When therefore that is so, then it is necessary to warm it up, so that you would immerse a sponge or a wet cloth in warm water alone. Or boil wild madder, and cypress, and asafoetida, and mix with them the intestine, until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes it makes that wine which has astringency, and it is better than water alone for the resolution of inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which alum or mulberry has been cooked. For by that it is alleviated its entrance with a small quantity.
If however it resists return after this cure, then find a little bit in the disruption with an instrument used to cut off hemorrhoids.
And the first cure is better when prepared than when cutting: and there is no forcing to cut after necessity and deficiency of the instrument.
Let that part of it be one which is twisted, acute, and the other part not acute, and let the extremity be subtle, but not with the subtlety of a Scythian, rather be somewhat obtuse: and that is what resembles a caiada, as you see.
When therefore the wound is enlarged and enters the intestine, then it is necessary that its reduction should be according to its natural shape in its own proper place, if possible above it: because it is better.
When the disruption is wide in the lower part of the abdomen: then it is necessary that you make the patient lie on their back, and place their legs higher than their head. And if it is in the upper part of the abdomen: then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if there is a disruption in one of the two parts of the abdomen: then place your intention and your attention always on this, that the part where the wound is should be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary to do so in great wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
But in small wounds, do it as you are prepared for.
Then have a pious servant present between your hands, who holds the entire disruption with his hand and gathers its edges. Then remove something from it to the one who follows in suturing, something after something: and this is the common method of suture for one.
And it is, that you take a needle or a number of needles according to the size and depth of the wound. Then let go from the edge of the disruption the length of a ring finger, that is, of a doctor: and fix one needle so that you insert it into the skin on both sides at the edges of the cutaneous layer, with two edges of the sifac, as much as is under the skin from the inside out, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have already gathered together the two edges of the skin and the two edges of the sifac: and there are four orders.
Then tie with a double thread around the circumference of the needle several times from both sides at once, until the two edges of the wound are gathered together in a decent manner.
Then let go of the thickness of the finger and fix another needle in them: afterwards tie it with a thread, just as you did with the first needle. Therefore you should not stop doing that with him whom you need from splints until you complete the constriction of the wound altogether.
And they should be of medium thickness between grossness and subtlety: because very fine needles quickly pierce flesh: and thick ones have difficulty entering the skin: therefore they ought to be of medium thickness in subtlety and grossness.
And it is not necessary for the needle to be stuck in two nearby parts of the skin: so that the flesh may not be pierced quickly and the wound may not be opened before its own consolidation, and also when sutured, in order that the wound may not prevent its own consolidation.
Then cut off the ends of the splints so that they do not harm the patient in his sleep: and place small pieces of cloth from all parts on them which hold the extremities of the splints: and let them be, until you know that the wound is now consolidated.
And this kind of suture with splints is indeed more suitable for small wounds. Because it is enough for the suture to have one or two needles, or roughly that many.
However, the second suture is common, that you gather with one suture four extremities: two of skin and two of silk wound in one place, with a needle in which there is an evenly wrapped thread of equal subtlety and thickness.
Then when you have pierced with the needle these four extremities, withdraw the needle from the part where you began, so that the thread is released from the upper part of the wound: in order that it be a suture, like a suture of cloth by which cloths are sewn together, and place between every suture the thickness of a small finger.
People know this kind of suture.
And with this suture I closed the wound that had occurred to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and there was a larger disruption than the width of one palm: and something like two palms had come out from the middle intestine, and there was a disruption in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I made it repair after his intestine stood outside the wound for 24 hours.
The wound was therefore consolidated in so much time as it was, being around 15 days; and I took care of it, and he recovered: and he lived many years after that, living according to his habits.
And the doctors had judged that he would not recover.
And it was a miracle, since I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where I could not find any ointment, nor anything from medicines. So I placed a piece of old linen cloth on the wound twice a day, and persisted with washing it with honey water until it was healed.
However, I will mention two kinds of stitches suitable for this narrative.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp stitch: and insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore the needle enters the skin and follows the course of a tendon going according to the length and width of the entire body: let the end be left at the side where you inserted the needle, and make it pass through the other extremity from the inner part to the outer in the opposite direction.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate according to this in the same extremity of the mirror on the outside to the inside: and leave the end at that side, and make the needle enter the other extremity from the inner part to the outer: and make it penetrate with your penetration as you make it penetrate into the edge of the mirror which is on its part, until it penetrates into the whole.
Then begin also from this same side, and take it along with the end which is from the edge of the mirror on another side, and pull out the needle from the skin as far as it has penetrated.
Then bring it back into that skin and join the end of the mirror which is on the other side with this end: and pull out the needle from the skin as far as it has penetrated from its part, and do it in reverse once and again: and do it in reverse after reverse, until you have brought all the external parts together according to that example.
And the highest point of this suturing is that you should make your sutures like those of pelt-makers who sew pelts: so that on one side of the skin, you join it together with a single thread from end to end; and then leave it off on another side and tie it back again on another until you complete it.
The second kind of proper suture, which Galenus said.
This is the way he said it should be done, by likening it to how certain physicians sew together, so that they join all parts together with their corresponding natural counterparts: and they apply the end of one piece to the end of another, and the end of one thing to the end of another. And this is done according to what I am telling you.
You should insert the point of the needle into the end of the thread that is nearest to you on the outer part, and make it penetrate only as far as the inside; and then let go the two ends of the thread. Then turn back the needle and make it penetrate from the outer part to the inner part on both ends of the thread.
Then turn back both and make them penetrate from the inner part to the outer part on the other two ends of the thread, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is a better and easier common suture, and it is the suture which was previously mentioned.
This then is the word of Galenus also.
And certain people said. The suture of the belly is rectified with two hooks. Which is to say that you should insert one thread into both of them: and begin with the insertion of the needle from where it is at your end, and make it penetrate until the other side, according to the suture of those who sew leather evenly.
And know that when there is a disruption in the middle of the belly: then the suture of it is more difficult than of other places of the belly.
The judgment of wound treatment is the judgment of other wounds.
For when you follow a recent wound with its blood, before it is altered by air: and bring out the intestine, and put it back in its place, and bind it tightly; then place it on humectated powder.
But if the air has already altered the wound: then place on it certain ointments that are present to you, until they cause pus; and let the strings fall off, and be consoled by sifac, and myrrh. Then take care of it as you would with other wounds, until it heals.
But if you do not have medicines present, then place on it immediately, as soon as it begins to emit pus, old cotton; and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it heals.
For you do not need other medicines in many cases when wounds are simple.
But if you are afraid that the wound may spread to principal limbs with injury, then it is necessary to immerse a soft woolen cloth in temperate warm oil or rose oil: and place it around the areas between the groin and armpits.
For if it feels pain, or punctures, or putrefaction in its intestines (for this often occurs) then cleanse it with a stiptic wine, black and warm: especially if the putrefaction has already reached the intestine and is making an exit that penetrates to its hollow.
And know that what comes from the thick intestine is easier to heal from: and what comes from the thin one is more difficult to heal from.
However, the intestine which is called Jeyun does not accept healing from a wound that occurs in it at all: and this is due to its multitude, which comes from veins, and the magnitude of those, and due to the subtlety of its body, and proximity to the nature of nerves.
If however that which proceeds from the wound is pus, and you subsequently find it fresh: reduce it according to your reduction, whereby you reduce intestines evenly.
But if a time has passed and it has become green or black: then it is necessary to bind it with thread above the place that has been discolored from it, so that bleeding does not occur. For in pus there are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is outside of that ligament and place two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it: so that its attraction to you may be alleviated, and its extraction at the case of the pus and discharge of the wound.
Memorial of a wound which occurs in the intestine.
When a disruption occurs in the intestine and it is small: then it is possible to be restored in some people: therefore, since I have seen someone who was wounded in his belly with the blow of a lance, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the old wound had been closed: and a fistula formed, and a discharge and flatulence occurred.
So I applied the treatment until I was not confident in his healing: nor did I cease to fine-tune it until he was healed and the affected area had consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I was afraid for the patient, lest a subsequent evil occurrence should happen to him in his belly: and it did not occur to him from that subsequent evil. And he remained in better physical condition, eating and drinking, and engaging in sexual activity, and bathed himself, and exercised according to his own habits.
And some experimenters have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small: then it is necessary to bind it according to this method. And it is as if you were taking ants from large heads. Then gather two lips of the wound, and place a formication on one of them that has an open mouth above the two lips of the wound. When it therefore takes hold on those and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and is not loosened.
Then place another ant near the first one, and do not cease to do that with an ant after an ant according to the size of the wound as a whole.
Then reduce it [the formication] and close the wound. For those heads remain attached to the intestine until it is nourished by the intestine, and is healed, so that no harm comes to the patient completely.
And now it is possible to suture the intestine also with a fine thread: which has been taken from an animal's intestine, and attached to it after it has been inserted into the wound.
And it is, that you take the extremity of this thread from the intestine and scrape off a lot. Then tie the fine linen thread to its end. Then insert that thread into the wound and close with it the intestine. Then reduce it to its place.
And this method of suture with ants and intestine is not except by the way of confidence and hope.
You should know that a wound which is great, wide, especially if it is on one of the thin intestines: then there is no skill except to make it finer, nor is its healing at all.
Â¶ On the treatment of Ulcers, and Fissures.
You should know that every wound or apostema when it becomes old and worn out, and turns into an ulcer, and does not heal, and always discharges pus, and is not cut away: then it is called universally whatever member it may be in a Fistula, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula indeed is a true knot, filtered, hard, white: with which there is no pain, like the hollow of a bird's feather. And because of this, some call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And it is in certain hours watery, which discharges pus. And perhaps the moisture is broken up in certain hours. And sometimes this moisture is much, and sometimes little; and it is thick, and it is fine.
And I have already spoken of the division, when I mentioned wounds, that every ulcer which is not healed, nor does flesh grow over it, is not that except for one reason out of nine causes.
One of which is because there is a lack of blood in the body: or because it is evil itself: or because there is hard flesh between it and its edges, preventing the growth of flesh: or because it is filled with many foul things and filth: or because the wound within itself is putrid, and the matter sent to it is of bad quality: or because the medicine used in its cure is unsuitable: or because a corrupting influence falls upon the region, such as occurs in Caesaraugusta, where digestion of illnesses is difficult and healing of ulcers delayed by nature: or because there is one bone or a number of bones.
And I have already shown all these causes and signs of them, and their cures with medicines in division: and it is necessary for me to make you know the cure of a fistula and zuke, which occurs through the way of operation by hand.
You should know that a fistula occurs in all members of the body. For some of these are fistulas that reach the great veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinews: or the intestines, or bladder, or to some part of the ribs: or to some part of the vertebrae, or to some joint composed of joints: such as hands and feet: and among them are those that go to the proximity of the main member, and similar.
What therefore is among these abscesses, difficult to heal, is from illnesses that do not receive healing, and the occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
But if it is from an abscess which depth does not reach the places I mentioned: and you hope, and trust in its cure, and wish to come to knowledge of it, then take a tent made of air or iron, if the abscess extends straight forward, and explore it with itself.
But if there is twisting in the abscess, then investigate it with a fine leaden tent. Because lead is eased by the softness of its body and curves around the twisting shape.
For if the abscess has many openings, it is not possible for you to investigate above it with a tent. Therefore, inject a medicament through one of its own openings.
For the moisture with which you inject it goes towards other openings and flows out from them.
Then, by diligent inquiry, find out how it is possible for you to know that there is an opening or a nerve, and that the abscess is far or near, or whether it is one abscess with many openings, and standing above all that, with the utmost fortitude of yours: with your questioning of the patient, and discovery of pain at compression by your hand on the spot, and similar signs and causes of apostema.
When therefore you have stood above all those things with a true understanding, then go to the cure according to your confidence: and it is, as you consider then, if the abscess appears near or in a safe distant place from the joint, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places I told you about: then cut the abscess according to what was previously said in my narrative, and remove what is in it through filtration, and corrupted flesh, which arises in its opening from the flesh, or fat, and similar things: and care for it until it heals.
If however a fistula is of great depth and straightness: then it is necessary to cut into its depth as much as you can. Then cleanse it from all the corrupted flesh of it.
Then apply a linseed wrapped in sharp medicine: and insert it as far as the concavity of the fistula that you have grasped with forceps: do this several times, until the sharp medicine has eaten away all that remains in the concavity of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that produce healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If however it does not heal through this, then cauterize it as we have described.
If therefore the cause of the fistula is a bone, and you have identified it in yourself: then investigate it, and cut into it as I have told you: if nothing prevents you from doing so on account of a vein, or nerve, or principal member, as we said.
If therefore a bone is revealed to you and it has something of corruption and blackening: then scrape it away until the entire corruption is removed. Then care for it with what helps to cover it, until it is healed.
What if it does not heal and discharges pus as before: then you will know that you have not reached the total eradication of the corruption.
Therefore remove again above it, and make a thorough examination of its condition, and purification of itself with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If therefore it is healed, that is good; and if not, then you will know that the corruption is in a deep recess of the body, which you have not reached. It is therefore not within your power to cure it, unless you leave it to nature.
What if the small bone is corrupted and it is possible for you, then draw it out with fine forceps that fit it.
If however there are many bones, then make a careful attempt to attract them all, and do not leave anything from them with your strength.
If indeed something from the moistures is opposed to you, and there is no fear or mistake in their cutting: then cut those moistures and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
If indeed you do not have preparation for removing the bones from your hour, then place over the wound that will make it rot, and leave it until it rots what is around those bones of flesh and moistures.
And guard the wound so that it may not consolidate. And tighten the incision which you made in your operation. Then place on top of it, if you fear, a poultice submerged in powdered sulfur mixed with oil: or a poultice submerged in Egyptian ointment, or some green ointments.
For when those moistures decay, and the bones separate from them, the removal of these is eased, and their attraction.
If however there is one large bone, such as the shinbone or hipbone, and similar to those, and what has decayed from it is a major part: then scrape it off with the last rasp until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
If however what has decayed from it is a major part, and corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw through it, and cut all the way to the point where corruption has reached: then care for it until it consolidates.
And I tell you of Zukan, what befell a man on his shin: that you may place it as an example and aid to your operation.
There was a young man of around thirty years old who suffered from pain in his shin, caused by something that had moved inside his body, namely the internal parts, until they reached his leg and became inflamed with great inflammation. And it was not caused by an external factor.
Therefore it was extended with him until a certain time, together with an error of physicians, until an abscess is opened, and many materials concurred with him. And after that, his treatment was suspended until his leg was tunneled and many openings were made in him; which all discharged pus and bodily fluids.
Therefore a multitude of physicians treated him for almost two years, and there was no wisdom in their hands until I was summoned, and I saw his leg and the materials flowing from those openings with great flow; also every part of his body had already become gangrenous, and his color was yellow.
So I introduced a probe into one of the openings, and it reached to the mouth.
Then I examined all the openings: and I found that they were communicating with one another from all parts of the leg.
Therefore I hastened and cut above one of those openings until I uncovered a part of the bone; and I found it corrupted: which was already decayed, and denigrated, and had putrefied, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
Therefore I cut away what was revealed to me, and prepared from the corrupted bone.
And I removed what was not in the bone except for that corruption which I cut away, and scraped. And I had already removed it.
Then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidation medicines over a long period of time. And it was not consolidated.
Then I probed again, and uncovered above the bone a second time after the first discovery; and I found a continuous corruption with the bone: and I cut away what appeared to me from that very corruption.
Then I attempted to restore it. And it was not restored.
So I uncovered it again above him, and did not cease to cut away the bone piece by piece, and attempt to restore it: and it was not restored until I cut off from the bone about a hand's breadth, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored him with medicines, and it was consolidated quickly, and healed. And this iteration of the operation on him, and the cutting open of his wound, was not necessary unless due to the debilitation of the sick person, and the scarcity of their tolerance, and my fear for him regarding death, since harm befell him at all hours from excessive purging.
Therefore he was healed by a complete cure. And flesh hard and good grew in place of the bone, and a good arrangement took place in his body, and his strength returned. He turned back to his natural state: and there was no harm or discomfort at all in his walking.
But if the prominent bone is already broken in the body: then it is necessary that you splint it, according to this method: and thus: take a ligament, and tie it at the tip of the prominent bone; and order someone to stretch it upwards; and place another ligament made from coarse wool on top of the first ligament. Then tie it above the flesh that is on the bone; and stretch the end of it so as to pull the flesh down to the lower part; and you will uncover the flesh above the place which you wish to cut, in order not to injure the flesh with the cutting.
Place a piece of wood or a board under the bone below, in the middle: because when you do that, it does not prevent the corrupt flesh from being cut.
And it is necessary that the cutting should be above the corrupted place for a short time, so that the corruption of the bone does not occur; and its appearance does not appear to the sense, and makes another place cut in its turn.
What if the bone is corrupted and is not prominent, but rather continues with another part; and corruption is in its middle or in a part of it: then remove all the flesh from every part. Then place a piece of wood below.
Then cut that from the first part where there is corruption, until the cutting continues to the other part; and let the cutting be according to length for a short time, as we said.
If there is corruption in the joint, cut off the corrupted joint and saw away at the bone where it continues on the other side.
If indeed corruption is in the continuity of two joints, then there is no art except removal.
If corruption is in the hip of the hand or in the hip of the foot: then the matter in that case is very difficult. However, you must investigate how the corruption appears to you. And remove it, and clean the area according to what is possible for you, and with whatever skill corrects itself to you: when it does not cause opposition from the Vein or Nerve.
And know that scalpels and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and subtlety, and size, and smallness, and hardness, and rarity. Therefore, it is necessary that you prepare the suitable instrument for every kind of operation.
And know that these operations mean something to you according to the type of instrument you need when you have a long study with yourself. And knowledge of the methods of this art is a testimony to the modes of illnesses: then indeed you will find through yourself what fits each illness from instruments.
And I am strengthening you with the last number of instruments that you put examples on which you will cut, and proof by which you will test others.
