About the cutting off of extremities and the sawing of bones.
The extremities putrefy, either due to an external or internal cause.
When you have been treated for that corruption with medicines and the treatment does not help: and you see the corruption occurring in a member which does not respond to anything: then it is necessary to cut off that member up to where the corruption ends, so that the invalid may escape through it from death or from the greater defect of the member.
And the sign by which this happens is that the member becomes discolored and blackens until it rots: which fire has consumed or decays after turning black: until the putridity reaches to what follows the member and spreads throughout the entire body.
Therefore, with the incision of this sort, and similarly if the cause of corruption is from the blows of certain venomous things: as of a sea scorpion, and viper, and adder, and similar ones.
Therefore, if the corruption or blow is at the end of the finger, then cut off the finger and do not give space to the corruption to spread until it reaches the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens on the hand, then cut off the hand from the wrist and do not give space to the corruption until it reaches the armpit of the arm.
And if it spreads into that, then cut off the arm at the elbow joint.
But if the corruption passes through and you see him around the elbow: then that person is on the point of death. And to administer beyond that in a cure is a quantity of virtue.
And similarly do the same with the foot, when corruption invades a toe, then cut off that one at the first joint; and if it reaches the calf of the foot, then cut off the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no skill left except for abandonment and leaving the sick person to death.
And the method of cutting off a limb or sawing it is to bind the ligament at the lower part which you wish to cut off and bind another ligament above that place. And have the assistant stretch one ligament down below: and have the assistant stretch another ligament up above upwards. And you separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments with a spatha or broad knife, until the entire piece of flesh is uncovered. Then it should be cut off, or sawed.
And it is necessary that you put a linen cloth around all parts and the saw does not touch healthy flesh: so that the patient suffers additional injury and develops a warm abscess.
If however a flow of blood occurs inwardly while you are operating, then cauterize the place quickly: or put some absorbent powders on top that cut off the blood. Then return to your treatment until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded member with a suitable bandage and care for it until it heals.
And I tell you what happened to a man on his foot, which is the same thing I am telling you.
What is this? For it happened on his foot that a blackening occurred, resembling the burning of fire, and that corruption first affected his finger before spreading to his entire foot.
Therefore the man healed himself after he saw the corruption affecting his member with its madness, which he found from the pain and burning; and it happened to him at the joint, and he was cured.
And after a long time had passed, the same corruption occurred on his index finger of his hand.
So he came to me and I tried to remove the excess with him, using medicines on his hand after purifying his body; and the excess was not removed, and it began to progress to another finger: until corruption spread throughout his hand.
So he called me to cut into his hand: and I did not consent to do so, hoping that the excess would be removed through my treatment; and I also feared for him death at the time of the incision on his hand. For the man's strength was greater than the calamity.
Afterward, since he had lost hope in me, he returned to his land: and came back to me about it, because he was impatient, and cut off his entire hand; and he was healed.
And I did not tell this story except as an aid to what falls under the category of this illness: and that it may be a sign by which it is shown, and according to which it should be acted upon.
On the cure of sinuses and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members and the time for its abscess to form pus lengthens until it bursts or perforates and what is inside it comes out along with the pus, and a hollow space remains as if it were a vessel; and the skin that covers it is like a piece of cloth that has been worn thin: and it does not reach its final stage of corruption nor impress itself on bone, nerve, or ligament. It deserves to be called Sinus; and it is not called Fistula. However, if its space prolongs until corruption sets in something of these members, then it is called Fistula, or abscess.
And its cure is to cut off the entire skin as it peels away; and especially when it has been worn thin and has become like a piece of cloth: and you trust that it will not adhere with the location due to its own corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and does not follow from this its final corruption; and if there is thickness on the skin from flesh, then cure it with an enema: and it is, to consider it at the sinus.
Then if it is large and the pus that comes out of it has a foul odor: then anoint it with an Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, oil, and zinar, parts taken in equal quantities, gathered together in a vessel: and let them be decocted over fire until the medicine becomes red-hot and arrives at a thick consistency like honey.
Then take from it what you need and dissolve it with water and honey, and anoint the sinus with it: and tie off its opening and let the medicine remain in it for a period of two hours.
Then extract it with expression; make him do this daily until the sinus is cleansed and the odor removed.
And sometimes it is cleansed with water of ashes, when this medicine is not present to you: and it is, that you take the ash of wood vines or the ash from ilex woods: and pour water over it. Then anoint it with it, and cleanse with it until you are confident that the sinus is now clean.
But if the patient is not able to tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the water of ashes, then cleanse him with water and honey, in which something has been mixed from crushed Indian fig. Or cleanse him with honey and wine mixed together, because of the property of honey that it cleanses and washes away: and wine makes the sinus adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you do this a few times and are confident that the corruption of the sinus is now removed: then cleanse him with what causes flesh to grow in it.
And it is, that you take defuminated plaster: and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine: or cleanse him with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written in the treatise on ointments.
But if the opening of the sinus is narrow and does not admit the canula of the cleanser: then enlarge it with iron for a short time: or insert into it a linen cloth wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or Basilic ointment, until it is enlarged.
And similarly if the opening is also large: then close its lips with a suture: and let it be as much as the cleanser can enter without addition.
And similarly if the opening from which pus flows is elevated upwards: then cut it in a lower place that is inside, so that the pus will flow downwards from there: because pus when constricted in the depth of the sinus prevents flesh from growing in it.
If it is not possible for you to cut the abscess as low as you like, then try to prepare the member by preparation that will cause the pus to flow out easily, according to how it has been prepared for you. Try to avoid leaving anything of the pus at all in it; and take medicines that consolidate, which are placed on the sign, fitting for what you want. And spread them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such a size that there may be communication with the entire abscess. Then cut with scissors directly into the opening of the abscess, making the hole in the abscess a little larger. Then spread another cloth with soft ointments over it, according to the size of the hole; and place it over it at a distance from the opening of the abscess, so that the exit of pus from it may be eased: and do not remove the cloth completely until several days have passed by.
But the one who is over the opening of the abscess is the one you remove at every hour, so that what gathers in it from the pus may flow out; and to know whether what flows out from it is much, or little, or digested, or undigested.
And with this, repeatedly examine the place of the abscess itself, whether its lord feels pain in it or not, and whether there is an apostema in it or not.
When therefore your treatment proceeds thus, and you see the material that emerges over the opening of the abscess, then you will know that the abscess is now adhering or approaching adherence: then therefore dissolve it in the morning or on the third day: and when you dissolve the wound, then change another cloth and renew the ointment.
But if after a long period a fine pus emerges from the abscess, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, since there are people among whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is delayed, and among whom it works contrary to this.
If however it issues forth from the sinus after many days as a foul discharge, then you will know that the sinus does not adhere.
If therefore the sinus is delayed and prolonged: then add to the drying of medicines that true physicians should make them humid in substance and dry in virtue, just as diaphrenic ointment when excess of addition from myrrh is added to it.
And from the greatest last thing that is treated, it is to take diaphrenic ointment and dissolve it with rose oil, and sprinkle over it an equal amount of old wine in its substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And now it does the same thing in this action, when you do not have diaphrenic ointment available, use honey when you cook it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloes, and frankincense: grind the whole into powder and sprinkle it over honey while it is hot. Then stretch a cloth over it and bind it over the sinus: and stretch with the honey that you cooked until it thickens: and sprinkle powder on top of it, and put it on the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes it is administered with Aristolochia longa and the roots of Liliaceous Lily, and this is called Lily; and wheat flour and Centaury. Use them in simple or compound form, grind them into powder, and sift it; and sprinkle it over the honey that I told you, and use it.
What if the sinus prolongs and does not heal with this treatment: then you will know that there is corruption deep within it; or already it makes an impression on the bones, or in other hard parts, or ligaments: then treat it with Fistula cure, according to what has been said before.
Of the Cure of Fungus and Worn-out Nail, and Additions through the Incision of the Finger, and Section.
Panaritium is a boil that grows under the thumb of the hand or foot: and perhaps it grows on other fingers.
When then the matter of it is prolonged, and its cure is neglected, and a boil forms a hot boil: or it becomes corrupted, and discharges pus, until it corrupts the root of the nail; and perhaps it corrupts the whole thing, and perhaps the corruption reaches to the mouth, until it has a foul odor: and the tip of the finger becomes broad, and its color becomes dark.
When then you treat it, as we said in the division, and your treatment does not take effect: then you must cut off with a knife all the superfluity that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For a cautery is very beneficial.
If however the bone is sound, and the nail is also sound, and the growth of the nail is already pressing on the flesh to the inside, and begins to pierce it, and hurt it: then you should put a thin piece of cauterized radium under the angle of the nail that pierces the flesh, and push it upwards: and cut away the flesh with ease, and place over what remains of the flesh from medicines that burn and corrode, until the whole thing is removed. Then care for it with ointments, until it heals.
If however corruption has already taken hold in the bone, then it is necessary to cut away that bone and remove it: for it never heals as long as the corrupted bone remains.
What if you see corrosion and corruption spreading into the finger, then cut it off at one of the joints, according to what was previously mentioned about its remembrance. Then care for it, until it heals.
If however there is nail attrition or percussion, and a severe pain accompanies it, then it is necessary to bleed the patient first. Then cut the nail with a sharp Spatumilus section transverse from above downward.
And take care lest the incision reaches the flesh beneath the nail, for if you do so, you will cause severe pain to the inferior part and it will be the reason for the added flesh growing in its place.
Then care for the wound with something that soothes pains.
However, the added finger that grows on some people's hands is perhaps entirely fleshy: and perhaps there are bones in some of them; and perhaps it has a nail: and some of them grow at the root of the joint or their birth is in certain knots of the finger. Therefore, that which grows in certain knots of the finger does not move: and that which grows at the joint of the finger, perhaps moves.
Therefore, since the one that is fleshy among them is so, its cutting is easy. What is, to cut it at its root with a broad Spatumilus.
But the treatment of that which grows at the joint of the root is difficult. Therefore, refrain from cutting it.
However, the one that grows on the finger at the end of the knots, then it is necessary to cut into its first flesh with a rounded incision up to the bone. Then file off the bone with one of those files that are suitable for it. Then care for it until it is healed.
However, the formation that occurs on fingers individually, often occurs either from the birth of a person, that is, it grows with him, or from the consolidation of a wound, or burning by fire, and similar things.
It is necessary therefore that you cut off that formation until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then place licinia or cloth soaked in rose oil between them: so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate between them: or place a thin sheet of lead between them, until the location consolidates as needed.
And similarly if consolidation occurs on some fingers with the palm, then cut off that consolidation, according to what is prepared and conforms to the shape of the limb.
On cutting out defects.
Varicose veins are those which are twisted, thickened, and full of superfluous melancholic humors that occur in many parts of the body: and most often they happen in legs, particularly in the legs of travelers and workers, and carriers.
Therefore it is necessary to administer a purgation of the body with black choleric medication several times with strong purging.
Then let a bloodletting be performed on the patient's basilica vein.
However, their treatment with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they are cauterized and the black blood is drawn out. And the other method is that the vein is raised and the entire thing is drawn out.
The cutting of it is according to this method. Bind the leg first with warm water a great deal until the thick, dirty blood has dissolved. Then bind the weak leg from the upper part of the hip down to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three, in a wide section.
Then lift, that is, draw out the blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg up to the top, and it is necessary for the phlebotomist to bleed the patient first. Then cut off the nail as far as the lower part: until a quantity of blood comes out which you see is sufficient and which the strength of the patient can tolerate.
Then bind it and tell him to abstain from foods that generate black bile, and iterate the evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are filled and that hurts the patient.
The elevation of it is according to this manner. Shave the crippled leg, if there are many hairs on it. Then insert him into the bath; or pour hot water over his leg with a sponge, until it turns red and the veins are opened up: or exercise him in strong physical activity, if there is no bath available, until the limb is warmed up.
Then cut the skin along the direction of the vein's section by length: or at its end near the knee, or in the lower part near the heel.
Then open the cut with a scalpel, and strip the vein of all its covering, until it becomes visible to the sense; and it is at that moment when you see it turn from red to dark brown. When it then comes out of the cut, you see it white as if it were a cord.
Then insert Radium underneath it, until when it is elevated and comes out of the cut; tie it with a blind ligature, gentle.
Then cut another section nearby, three finger's length away. Then strip the cut from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then elevate it with Radium as you did, and tie it with another ligature, just as you did at first.
Then cut another section, or multiple sections, if needed. Then elevate and incise the very last section at the heel.
Then remove it to the section that is above it.
And do that until you remove it from the section above, or higher sections altogether, until when it emerges entirely; then cut it.
If however it does not obey you to remove and elevate: then insert a hook with a strong string doubled, and tie it, and pull it out; or insert underneath it Radium: and turn your hand around every direction until it emerges. And beware, lest it be cut. For if it is cut, it becomes difficult for you to lift it strongly, and an evil consequence will ensue upon the sick person from that.
Therefore, when you have entirely extracted it: then place a cloth soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, over the wound, and care for it until it is healed.
And if the vitriol is contracted, having a twisted shape and being wrapped around parts, and it is not as we said, straight: then you must cut through it on all sides of its twistiness, and the places where it wraps itself.
Then suspend it with a hook until you lift it all out: and beware at your cutting through it not to cut the vein or wound it: for it will be difficult for you to lift it up or draw it out. Therefore, preserve your prudence.
The Form of Cutting Whereby the Vitriol is Healed.
The Form of a Blind Knot.
It is not curved like other knots, nor is it sharp at its end, so as not to wound the vein; and it has a thickness of flexuousness, gentle: for if it were subtle, it would cut the vein by its subtlety: nay, let its superfluous thickness be as we said.
On the Extraction of the Vein of the Thigh.
This Vein is generated in the thighs of people on hot earth, like that of Alizeg, and of the Arabs, and on dry, hot lands with little fertility, that is, where no grass grows nor tree.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the thighs.
And its generation indeed is from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, just as it happens within bodies to snakes and ascaris worms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein's outcome is that which happens in the leg a violent inflammation. Then the place is cleaned. Then the vein begins to emerge from that place of cleaning, as if it were a root of a plant or an animal.
Therefore, when the end of it appears from there, then it is necessary to wrap a piece of lead over it, weighing one and two-thirds drachma keil, and bind the lead suspended from the leg.
Whenever then something emerges from it to the outside, wrap that in lead, and bind it. What if it is prolonged greatly, then cut off a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut off its root before it has emerged entirely. Because if you cut it, it contracts and enters the flesh, and causes an abscess, corruption in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary to handle it gently and draw it out gradually, until it emerges from its entirety, and nothing remains of it in the body: and from this vein some people emerge in a length of five palms and ten. And now it has reached me, because it emerged from a man twenty palms long.
But if it happens to you during your medication hour: then insert the tip into its opening, and pierce it with a long opening, together with the body, until everything that is in it is evacuated from the material: and administer putrefaction of the place with medicines for several days. Then care for the cure by treating abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot, or on the foot itself: and it has many openings, and a branch emerges from every opening.
Therefore treat it as we said in division, and with what has been previously mentioned.
About the cutting of worms born under the skin, and it is called a Boar's sickness.
This sickness is called "Boar's sickness" in a certain land among us because it frequently occurs in oxen and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh.
And it moves throughout the body, ascending and descending, and is shown to the sense at every step of its movement from member to member, until it breaks wherever it breaks in the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. Just as worms and serpents, namely, lice and ascarids, occur in the belly.
And fear is to be had from its evil. Because when it goes into the body and rises to the head and reaches a place, then perhaps it opens up there and emerges, and destroys an eye: and that happens many times.
When therefore you wish to cure it and draw it out, then that does not happen except at every step of its movement and appearance to the sense.
Therefore you must tie what is above it and what is below it with a strong binding constraint. Then cut above it and draw it out.
What if it burrows into the flesh and you don't find it: then place a caustic on top of the spot with fire, until it dies.
And greater than that which is to be feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts an eye, as we said.
If you see it has already reached the head near the eye, then tie below it the eyebrow with a good tightening. Then cut above it and draw it out.
And it is necessary that the patient uses the cleansing of his body with medicines dissolving bad, putrid humors.
And let him beware of foods that produce putrefaction.
On the Lancing above Disease Nakir.
This disease is called Nakir in our land; and it is a pain that occurs in certain members, then it is changed from member to member.
And I indeed have seen him myself, according to what I am telling you. I was called to a sick woman in certain villages. And she showed me her arm and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm.
And after I had stayed for one hour, I saw a swelling move with the pulse, just like a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than it can be, so that it is like living silver when it runs from place to place.
The pain then departed from that place and was fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour and it ran to another part of the body until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it was revolving throughout her entire body, according to what I saw.
I was amazed therefore at the rapidity of its changes from member to member. And I indeed had not seen this disease with my own eyes in such a way before that time. However I have seen many who found pain changing from member to member; and I did not see it with my own eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it is anything except that the woman was one of those who are from dry and discovered veins of the body; therefore it becomes apparent to my senses that this swelling is being moved.
And it is necessary that they not appear according to this reason in those who are delicieux and have moist bodies and hidden veins.
So when you want to treat it and the patient feels that pain, then if it appears to your eye as we said, then tie above it quickly, and beneath it swiftly: and cut above it until the constricted swelling goes out: and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes: then cure it by excision of the body, and with that which drives out swellings, and bind them up with fetid pills, and pills of serapeum, and similar ones.
Of the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are differentiated according to their own kinds and according to the places where they fall from the body.
And their variety is this: because there are some that have large points and small ones: and hollow-tipped and pointed. And among them are those that have three angles. And among them there are those with tongues. And among them there are those with stars.
Those however that fall according to the members, is in two modes. Or else the members are from the main concave members, such as the brain, and the heart, and liver, and lung, and kidney, and intestines, and bladder, and similar ones. When therefore it falls on one of these members and apparent signs of death appear to you which I will tell you next, then it is necessary that you extract the arrow from there, for death follows him who has it in many dispositions. And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the member, then extract it and cure the wound.
When a dart falls into the brain and pierces the skull, and emerges above the brain, it causes: convulsions, scotoma, vertigo, redness in the eyes, inflation, redness of the tongue, spasm, and a mixture of intellect, and vomiting with bile. And perhaps blood will flow out from the nose or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and voice is destroyed. And a white discharge like pus flows out from the wound site. And a fluid similar to serum of meat exits from it.
So if these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment and remove the dart if it does not emerge.
The signs of the dart when it falls into the heart are that it is near the left breast, and it feels as if it were fixed in hard substance, not in empty space. And perhaps the dart's movement is similar to the movement of a pulse: and dark blood flows from the wound. And then follows numbness in the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore that death is undoubtedly impending.
And the sign of the dart when it wounds the lung is the emission of frothy blood from the wound: and the vessels that follow the neck become inflamed, and their color changes to that of a sick person. And it exhales a great and deep breath: and seeks the attraction of cold air.
Which if the dart falls into the membrane that is in the chest: then it is near the small ribs; and a great gasping occurs with intense pain, with a sigh: and all the limbs of the shoulders are moved.
And if the dart falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from food will emerge from the wound.
And if an arrow falls into the belly and is stuck there: something foul-smelling exits from the wound, or pus, or intestine that is already torn. Therefore, there is no hope for its cure, nor for extracting the arrow.
For if the arrow falls into the bladder and urine flows out: and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is more intense on the afflicted person: then you will know that it is mortal.
However, other parts of the body, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and rib, and loin, and leg, and similar ones are treated with poultices from the limbs, according to many dispositions: when the arrow does not find an artery or nerve: and the arrow is not venomous.
And I will tell you what I have seen about these arrows, so that you may infer from it about your own cure.
For it is this: a man had an arrow fall into the root of his nose, at the base of his tearful eye. So I extracted it from him on the other side, under his ear fat: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had fallen into the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I did not succeed in extracting more than a small part of it, with which it was stuck in its wood: and the arrow was great among the arrows of Turkish bows, with a soft iron tip, without an ear.
Therefore, the Jew was healed, and no evil happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian's throat.
And the arrow was Arab, and it is one that has an ear. So I secured it above it between two guides: and already it was submerged in his throat. I have skillfully handled her until I extracted it from her: and a Christian has been healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who had fallen into his belly: and we thought that he would die because of it.
After thirty days had passed and nothing had changed in his condition, I observed the arrow; and I examined it, and extracted it; and he was healed, and no further harm came to him.
And I saw a man on whom an arrow had fallen on his back, and the wound was consolidated over it. After seven years had passed, the arrowhead emerged at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman on whom an arrow had fallen into her belly, and the wound was consolidated over it, and the arrowhead remained inside her: and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman did not find any harm from any of her natural bodily functions.
And I saw another man on whom an arrow had fallen on his face: and the wound was consolidated, and remained without finding much pain; and similar things were seen.
And I extracted an arrow from a man of the King's princes, which had fallen into his nose and was already leaning towards the right side; and the arrowhead was entirely hidden.
I was called therefore to treat him after the arrow shot, until three days. I found then a wound of the arrow very tight.
I then probed it with a fine probe and did not feel it; and he himself was finding a puncture and pain under his ear on the right side. I therefore supposed that that puncture was from the tip of the arrowhead.
I therefore applied a poultice to the place with a poultice that had attracting and digestive power, having confidence in myself that the area would suppurate and that I would see a sign of the arrowhead appearing, so that I could cut above it: and no accident occurred at the place indicating that the arrowhead was located there.
Therefore I applied the poultice to him for many days and it did not happen that anything untoward occurred, and the wound was consolidated in the meantime: and he remained lame from the extraction of his space of days until he felt a dart one day inside his nose. And he told me this.
And he told me this. So I applied a sharp, corroding medicine to that wound for many days until it opened up: and when I probed him, and felt the slender extremity of the arrow which was stuck in wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine until the extremity of the arrow appeared visibly to us: and it passed by me along with him for a period of about four months.
Then after the wound had been widened and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and move it, but it did not obey my exit: then I did not cease to whittle away at it and invent methods on top of it with various contrivances, until one day I began to extract it with well-made forceps, according to the shape they would take at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed doctors judge that the nasal cartilage is not restored. So I restored it and the wound was consolidated and the patient recovered with complete and entire health: nor did anything at all harm him.
And I will tell you the quality of extracting certain arrows: so that you may place it as a rule and signification over what I did not say.
Because the parts of this art and their distinctions cannot be comprehended by narrative: nor does that book comprehend it, and a wise craftsman compares little with much: and he also finds what is present over what is absent: and he discovers a new operation and a new instrument in the descent of external matters of this art when accidents occur.
I therefore say that arrows do not exit from the limbs on which they are fixed, unless according to two modes. Or through attraction from the place where it entered. Or through the opposite part of the other side.
And that which exits where it entered is either an arrow proceeding to a fleshy place and being drawn out.
For if it does not obey the exit at the time when it falls: then one must let it be for some days, until the flesh that is around it has putrefied: and its attraction and exit will be eased.
And similarly if it is fixed in bone and does not obey you to exit, then let it be for some days: and repeat the process of attraction and motion every day, until it exits.
For if it does not obey you to exit after days, then one must pierce the surrounding flesh of the arrow in the bone from every part with a fine file, until the arrowhead is enlarged. Then attract it and pull it out.
If however an arrow has already been lodged in the body and is hidden from sight, and is concealed from sense perception: then search for it with probes. If however you feel it, then draw it out with certain instruments that are suitable for drawing it out.
If however you cannot reach it above, due to the constriction of the wound and the length of the arrow deep within, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein: then find a way to reach it until the wound is enlarged and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
And if there are ears that it holds to, then free the fixed flesh from all sides, in every way you can, and with arts, if it cannot be freed by going over the edge of the ear, and by bending them until it goes out.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use a twisting motion with your hand, along with forceps, to all parts, until you make it come out: and with utmost subtlety, so that the arrow does not break and the extraction becomes difficult for you.
And if you cannot remove it from one hour to another, then leave it for a few days, until what is around it begins to decay. Then reiterate it: the swelling will indeed be alleviated. If however blood flux makes contrary to you, then administer what we said in its own chapter: and preserve your strength from cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And apply skill in every way that it is possible for you to extract the arrow: and let it be with ease, and calmness, or slowness, and firmness, just as I told you.
And it is necessary that when you pull out the arrow, the patient be made weak according to the shape, according to which he was at the time of the arrow's impact. Because it is more beneficial. But if it's not possible for you, then do what is possible for you from the shapes.
However, the arrow that emerges from the opposite part of the other [side], either already proceeds something to the exterior or causes a swelling at the end of the Arrow by feeling on the superior part of the nearby skin, and you see it protruding: therefore, cut above it, and let the incision be according to the quantity that the forceps can take in it.
Then draw it out: for it becomes easy to come out by itself.
If it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to the roundness, until the Arrow impresses itself on the bone and enlarges its own depth: then draw it out.
And if not: leave it for a few days: then reiterate the procedure until it comes out.
What if the wood of the Arrow is stuck in it, then push it with that [wood].
But if the wood has fallen and you wish to administer a push: then introduce a concave instrument into it, so that it enters its concavity into the tail of the Arrow: then push it with it.
But if the Arrow is concave, then push it with the instrument entering into it. The Arrow is eased by that.
What if the Arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to remove all the flesh where the venom is, if that is possible for you. Then take care of it with what is fitting for that.
What if the Arrow falls in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side: and it is close to what you touch with your hand, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and beware of cutting into a vein, or nerve, and draw it out.
Then treat a wound, if you see one, and care for it until it heals.
And this is the shape of forceps by which arrows are pulled out.
Let their extremities be like the beak of a bird, made as if from iron; when they take hold above an arrow, they do not let it go.
And many kinds of them are made, great, small, and middle-sized, according to the size, magnitude, and narrowness of the arrow, and the size, width, and constriction of the wound.
The shape of a spoon is concave.
And the shape of an obtuse impeller.
This is like the blunt end of a radius, so that it may be inserted into an arrow and pushed along with it.
On bloodletting of Veins.
The veins which are accustomed to be bloodletted in the body are thirty.
Of which sixteen are in the head: two pulse-beating veins that are behind the ears and are called Herbae; and two arteries that appear in both temporal regions; and two veins that are in both tear ducts of both eyes, which are called Aspicientes; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein that is at the end of the nose. And two Guidigie veins that are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins that are in the lower lip. And these are called Algecherich veins. And two veins that are under the tongue.
The veins that are tapped for bloodletting on arms are five in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic vein, and it is located on the lateral side of the shoulder; and the people call it the "head" vein. And the Axillary. And it is the Median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic: and the people call it the "body" vein. And the Basilic, and it is located on the lateral side of the arm: and it is also called the Tertian. And the people call it the "abdominal" vein. And the Armstring vein, and it is located above the elbow; and it is that which is tapped for bloodletting, and it is the one that is visible above the wrist, with a clear appearance.
And the Cedrella: and it is a vein that is located between the Annular and Median: and it has two branches.
And there are three veins in the leg and foot. One of them is that which is located under the curvature of the toe on the lateral side. And the second one is the Saphena: and its location is at the heel, from the lateral side of the foot. And the vein Nerve: and its location is at the heel, from the lateral side.
And in another leg there are three veins similar to those.
Of Bloodletting from Two Veins after the Ears.

On the utility of Phlebotomy and precautions to be observed in it. The quality however of Phlebotomy and its accidents from which you should reflect on how to correct them.
First therefore of that which you should know is this: that Phlebotomy is not administered except for the conservation of health, and the perseverance of its continuity, and caution against the approach of afflictions.
And that Phlebotomy may be done in one of the three veins on the shoulder, namely, the Cephalic, and the Black, and the Royal. And that Phlebotomy should be done at the beginning of spring after fasting, in March and autumn after eating, when signs of replenishment appear.
And that Phlebotomy should be done on the first or third day, after three hours have passed since sunrise.
But Phlebotomy which is administered for illnesses does not have a fixed hour; but when the occasion and necessity require it, whether by night or day, at any hour, and at any time.
And it is not fitting that children be bled until they come of age at fourteen years. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should old men who have already passed sixty years be bled.
When therefore someone wants to be bled for whatever reason: it is necessary that he cleanse his bowels beforehand with a gentle enema, if much feces has been retained in them: so that the veins may not attract at the time of bleeding the superfluous putrid things from the bowels that are harmful to the principal members.
Nor should a patient be bled who is drunk with excessive eating or drinking, nor one who is vomiting from excess, nor one who has just recovered from drunkenness, until that state passes away from them. Nor should an invalid with dropsy be bled.
And bloodletting should be avoided again after a colic attack, and vomiting, and diarrhea, and frequent coitus, and labor, and exercise, and wakefulness, and fasting, and all that dissipates the vital force from bodily or animal substance.
Then consider the subtlety of humors beforehand, whether the blood is thickened with foods and drinks, and medicines: if it is possible to do so.
Then enter the bath, unless something prevents it; or engage in some exercise, in order that the blood may be refined.
And put his bloodletting in the chest, on the third day, as we said: and strive to purge out his chest that day from all bad animal affections, such as anxiety, anger, fear: and from all bodily afflictions, such as labor, pain, excesses, and those similar things.
And make the remedies of his own kind present with which custom has accustomed people to use through aromaticity, and scenting, and dancing, and similar things in proportion to the possibility.
Then the phlebotomator should sit on a cushion which is higher than the one used for phlebotomy. Afterwards, he should take out his own arm and rub it with his hand twice or three times. Then he should tie a ligament with a belt, and wrap it around twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, for when it is unequal, or there is too much constriction, then it hinders the flow of blood; or if it is loose, then also it hinders the flow of blood.
Then after the constriction, it is necessary for the one to be bled to rub his hands together evenly until the veins are inflated and become apparent to the sense.
Then the phlebotomator should wipe the wound with a little old oil, properly.
Then he should place his index finger of the left hand on top of the vein at the spot where he wants to make the incision, slightly so that the vein does not flee and a blow can be struck. For just as the string flees from the phlebotomist during surgery, so too do the veins flee when one places the phlebotomy instrument on them. And there are some that are full of wind: and when you place the phlebotomy instrument on it, it contracts beneath the phlebotomist, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and the phlebotomy does not open up the vein; and if it does open up, it is constricted. Therefore it is necessary for the phlebotomist to be firm and attentive in all these matters.
Then he should remove the phlebotomy instrument. If the vein opens up on one side, then it is good. And if not, then do the same thing on the other side a little bit away or above it with haste: unless the spot becomes inflamed.
That if it becomes inflamed or swells up with disease, then let him rest for a day or two: and do not bind the bandage. Perhaps it will attract a warm abscess. Nor should he enter the bath.
Then he will repeat venesection, if desired.
But if the aperture is small, or the blood flows thinly, and you fear that not enough blood will come out: then repeat venesection in the same opening, following its natural direction, and add a little to the procedure.

Of Pneumothorax, and its quality.
Pneumothoraces occur from horns, and from air, and from vinegar.
And Pneumothorax indeed occurs in two ways. With scarification and bloodletting: and the other is pneumothorax without scarification.
And this Pneumothorax which is without scarification, is in two ways. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the ventoses that are administered with scarification and bloodletting are fourteen places of the body. One of which is Ventosatio Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head; and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck; and ventoses Alhagdahim, and they are two plates of the neck from both sides simultaneously; and ventoses of the mind, and it is under the jawbone in the lower part of the mouth; and ventoses of the two spatula-like places; and ventoses of Alachosos above the end of the tail; and ventoses of the two forearms, and they are in the middle of the two arms; and ventoses of the two hind legs; and ventoses of the two scapular areas.
And indeed, Pneumothorax does not extract blood from you from small scattered veins in the flesh, and because of that, it does not fall into power, just as is the case with bloodletting. Nor should pneumothorax be administered, whether with fire or without fire, in any disease that arises from excess, until the entire body has been purged.
But if necessity demands pneumothorax due to illness or habit, we administer it at every hour at the beginning of the month, its end, and its middle, and whenever it may be.
What is this? Because there are people who, when blood multiplies within him until he needs its extraction by lancing, find a swelling in their head and then swoon.
And among them are those who find fullness and redness on their face, head, and neck. And among them are those who find itching on their face and forehead, and darkness and dimness in their eyes.
And among them are those who scratch the place of their lancing. And among them is one whose laughter increases. And among them are those who find the taste of blood in their mouth: or their tongue will break out and spit up blood. And among them is one whose sleep increases. And among them is one who sees blood and redness, and killed ones, and wounds, and things similar to those in their sleep.
When we see something of these things, and especially if it occurs on the 15th day: then we prescribe lancing to be done two hours or three after sunset.

What if there is coagulation in the blood, then it is necessary that you scarify him twice. In the first instance, indeed, the way is opened for subtletization of the blood and its moistening: and in the second instance, indeed, a thorough and meticulous extraction of thick blood.
If the blood is very foul, then a third scarification should be performed: so that the outcome may be final.
And finally, when we want to draw out the blood slowly or a little bit, one scarification is enough for us. If we wish to extract a great deal of blood, we will make many scars.
And if we measure, and find that the blood is thick: then it is necessary to incise deeply with a scarification, and the equal end of the scarification is the depth of the skin.
What should be administered from oils at the position of Wind-sores, and what should a wind-sores patient beware.
In that person whose skin is thick, hard, and dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary that the areas of Wind-sores be anointed with open, gentle, resolving oils; if in the time of summer, such as oil of alkeiri, or oil of violet, or oil made from sweet almonds, or oil from cucumber seed.
And if it is in the time of winter, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or sabine oil; and similar things.
If the excess is thick and cold: then oil should be of amaranth, or oil of balsam, or oil of white poppy, or oil of anethum; and similar things.
If the wind-sores patient is of large pores and tender flesh: then it is necessary to prohibit them from oil, and it is necessary that those with such wind-sores wash the areas Ventosarum after ventosation with rose water, or cold water, or sour water, or cucumber water, or purslane water; and similar things.
He whose blood is of much moisture, should wash the wind-sores with vinegar, or with water of myrtle, and sumac; and similar things.
Whoever has thick excesses should wash their wind-sores with old wine, or with water of sammosus, or a decoction of anise, or chamomile; and similar things.
And it is necessary to beware of inflammation in the bathhouse, or near the bathhouse, indeed it is necessary that it be administered after exiting the bathhouse for an hour, or two hours.
And one should not sleep after inflammation.
What is it that ought to be regulated with the Venosandus or Phlebotomatus before inflammation?
And after that it is necessary to consider first: then if the Venosandus or Phlebotomatus is choleric and prevails over his blood's sharpness and inflammation: then it is necessary that he take cooling remedies, such as pomegranate juice: and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and sycamore leaf, and similar things.
And give him foods such as eggs, old meat of sheep, and cooked foods with wild herbs: and similar things.
And to one whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he takes in his drink a syrup made from honey or almond or sycamore seed: and let him take a medium-sized fragrant wine, which is between old and new: and let him be advised to use moderation with food. And give him foods such as eggs, and small birds: and sparrows, that is, small birds; and he should also take dove's eggs.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of inflammation and phlebotomy be more than food: and it is necessary to give in the drink certain things to some people one hour before breakfast, or medicine made from moss, that is, muscata, or alesselithe before inflammation; and before or after phlebotomy, so that the principal members may be strengthened and the blood may be thinned. And one should not take it with a hot drink.
A treatise on Inflammation without Scarification. Those wounds that are without scarification are the ventosas which are placed on the liver, spleen, podagra, and on the belly, navel, and places of kidneys, and pithed anchae: for these members do not suffer scarification upon themselves.
And it is not sought through them, except by attraction of blood from member to member: just as we place a ventosa on the breasts in illness of nasal bleeding. And we use them to remove from the member cold and congested blood in the member: just as we place a ventosa on the belly and navel. For it itself rarifies the member, and warms it up, and removes the pain on account of that, because it dissolves that blood congestion.
And it is also placed on the kidneys when there occurs a blockage or stone. For indeed its power of attraction may perhaps open up the blockage or remove the stone from its place.
And it does the same thing when placed on the liver and spleen with a congestion to be removed from both of them.
And these ventosas are administered empty, with only an incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in pleurisy afflictions; and that is, so that the ventosa may be filled and be large with hot water alone, or with water in which herbs have been decocted that agree.
Form of the Ventosas administered with fire.
The shape of the Ventosas administered with fire is as follows: let the width of its opening be two open fingers, according to what we have formed: and let the quantity of it in depth be half a palm: and let there be on one side of it, about halfway down, a small hole, according to the size that an acus will enter it: make it from air simultaneously, or citron, rounded, smooth, equal, clean edges so as not to hurt the member at its position upon it: and let there be in the middle of it a crosswise rod of air or iron where a candle is placed with fire.
And now this poultice is made large, greater than what we have described, and a little smaller than that, according to the afflictions and age of the person who uses it.
For wind pouches of children and those emaciated are different from wind pouches of men and those with large bodies.
However, the quality of applying this poultice with fire on a limb is as follows: you should light a linen wick or a small candle from beeswax and place it in the middle of the transverse stick which is inside the poultice, so that the ignited part of the fire may be directed towards the lower part of the poultice, lest the sick body be burnt.
Then it should be placed on the limb, and your finger should be above the opening which I mentioned, until we need to remove the poultice; as long as we use it, take away your finger, and a vapor will come out through that opening, and the poultice will loosen immediately.
Then burn the wick according to its nature, and repeat this if you need it.
But in the poultice which is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse stick nor opening; and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and it is placed only on the limb.
And this is its form.
And this poultice is better in proportion as it is larger and can hold much water.
Â¶ On the suspension of Bloodletting Pouches.
Bloodletting pouches are not administered on many parts of the body, except in those where it is impossible to apply poultices due to their smallness, such as the lips and gums, and similar things. Or on a bare member devoid of flesh, such as a finger, and nose, and similar things.
And the quality of using them is: to take the blood from the bloodletting pouches that are in sweet and clean waters, free from corruption.
Then they are let go day and night in sweet water until they are famished, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body should be evacuated first by bloodletting, and then with purgatives. Then wash the afflicted member until it turns red. Then they are placed on top of it.
When they are filled and fall away, and it is possible to suck out the place with a purgative, then it is more beneficial; and if not, then wash the area with vinegar. Then with much water, it is rubbed and expressed.
But if the flow of blood is prolonged after the fall of haemostats, and it is that which drips away; then dip a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top, until the dripping subsides.
But if the blood multiplies, then sprinkle powder of bounded alum or gallnuts and similar things from astringents on top, until the bleeding stops.
Or put middle layers of peeled beans on top and let them be, until they stick to the place, for the blood stops.
And it is necessary if a repetition of haemostats is needed, when possible, to change them.
But if the haemostats are prevented from being suspended, then wipe the area with fresh blood. Or fix a needle in place until something comes out of the blood. Then put them on, for they immediately stick when they feel something coming from the blood.
When you want them to fall, sprinkle something from pounded aloes, or salt, or ashes on top of them: and they will fall immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of medicine, and it is about restoring fractures and dislocations of bones.
Know, ye sons, that foolish doctors and the common crowd boast of this chapter, and those who have never looked at an ancient book nor read a letter from it.
And because of this reason, our method is deprived of science on earth. And I have never found anything good in it at all, and I did not obtain what I obtained unless it was through the length of reading ancient books and my study on their understanding, until I extracted that science from them.
Then I adhered to experimentation and lengthy study throughout my life.
And now I have described to you everything that my science contains in this chapter: and beyond what has passed my experiment, after I shortened it for you, and freed it from the occupation of prolongation, and shortened it at the end of shortening, and showed it at the end of deviation: and I shaped many forms in it from the forms of instruments that are used in it, with the addition of deviation, just as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter 2.
It is necessary that we say in the prologue of this chapter, the summaries and headings, whose understanding requires the craftsman to stand at attention over their truth: namely, so that you and those who are ready may know or learn this noble art from you.
I say therefore, that when any fracture or dislocation or twisting or fall happens to someone: then it is necessary that you hasten first to let blood on him, and dissolve him, or both at once: if nothing prevents, such as weakness of vigor: or he who has something happen to him is a child, or an old decrepit person: or the time is extremely hot, or extremely cold.
Then he should be content with his food, cold herbs, and the flesh of birds and hares; and forbid wine, fatty meats, and foods that fill veins with blood. Until you are safe from the warm abscess and do not fear the discharge of material onto the member; then the sick person should return to his original regimen, with which he is accustomed.
When therefore a broken bone is being restored, then it is necessary for the sick person to be fed nourishing foods in abundance, thick, heavy, and with a slimy consistency: such as food made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads and feet of vegetables, and sheep's entrails, and eggs, and fresh fish, and strong wine, and similar things. For with this regimen, the coagulation of the fracture occurs more quickly and better.
And know that bones which are fractured when the men who are in their first old age and decrepitude are not possible to be continued and consolidated according to their natural state because of drying up of their limbs; and those things which come from them at the end of relaxation, such as children's bones.
However, nature attaches something similar to glue above the fractured bone from all sides, which has thickness, by which it adheres and tightens itself until it is thoroughly congealed and bound together until it reaches the end of strength and firmness, as it was in the beginning, until nothing prevents it from performing its functions: and for this reason it is necessary to provide nourishment to the sick person with food that has heaviness, sliminess, and thickness, as we said.
And know that the species of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of members: for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the skull: and a fracture of the breastbone is different from a fracture of the backbone: and similarly all other members are different one from another: and I come with the reminder of every species of fracture exposed in its own chapter, distinguished from others. And the types of bone fracture are differentiated also within themselves.
Because it becomes a curved fracture, besides the stars that occur in it. And sometimes it happens that the fracture is according to the length of the bone: and there are fractures which are said to be sagittae, that is, stars, and additions proceeding, as they are on a reed when it is fitted with a pen for writing. And not proceeding; and there is a fracture with wound and disruption at the head; and it is a small cleft fracture.
And every species has its own ingenuity or proper instrument for restoring it, according to how its memory comes, distinguished in its own places.
And from the fact that a bone fracture is known, there is its twistiness and its prominence; and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no apparent twistiness in the place, nor a sound when you touch it with your hand as you apply gentle motion, nor does the infirm person find great pain: then there is no fracture there, rather it is possible that it be a twistiness or small cleft: so you should not move it at all with your hand and compression, but rather place something of medicines upon it, whose remembrance comes after that moment which is suitable to the place; then let it be bound with a fine bandage.
And know that the place when it curves and splits into two, besides what occurs in it as stars, yet every part is removed from its companion: then you should start at your hour to correct it, equalizing it before a warm abscess comes upon it.
But if a warm abscess occurs, then let it be left for several days until the abscess subsides. Then equalize it according to any method possible by gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration of it and its adjustment is easier than bone, where endeavors occur.
Then tie it up, according to how its memory comes back to you.
If there are efforts in a bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken member from two parts, if it is an arm or leg; or with your hand, if the limb is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And your position should be such that you place the limb on an equal location, according to its natural form, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then strive to make those additions in every way and by whatever means you can in their places; and beware, according to your strength, lest there occur superfluous pain or injury due to your operation: and strive according to your strength, that you apply one bone with its partner, according to better forms.
And it is necessary at that hour for you to test and touch it with your own hand. If then you see something different there, correct it, and make it equal according to the quantity of your strength.
And beware of excessive extension and strong compression, just as many foolish people do. For indeed they often cause to happen through their own operation aposteme hot or contracture in a member: just as we have already seen many times from their operations.
Then bind after adjustment, and proper operation, and the constriction of that member, and rest, and calmness: and caution the weak person above it. And make it so that it does not move in its own hours of vigil and sleep, and in its own revolution and agitation, and in its own exhaustion, and all its movements with its utmost strength. And that it may beware not to be in a position of the member's position that would follow pain. What is this, because when it feels a pain or injury in the arrangement of a limb: then one should move it to another arrangement with which it does not feel pain.
And beware that the position of the limb is equal and straight, so that there is no twistiness in the member when it is restored.
And the quality of a broken limb's fracture is, as I narrate.
You should know that fractured limbs are differentiated by their smallness or greatness and by their shape.
Therefore, what is small among them, such as an arm, finger, and toe, and similar to these, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, thin cloth. But what is large among them, such as the hip and leg, it is necessary that there be a wrapping of broad, thick cloth.
Because the wide band connects a large limb and tightens it from every part with equal constriction, so that no swelling enters.
Then begin after your evacuation to place the suitable ointment on that, in a thin smear over the location of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the location of the fracture itself with three or four windings, according to what is suitable for the limb; and grasp your hand slightly with the bandage.
Then go with it to the upper part above the location of the fracture and tighten less than your previous constriction by which you tightened the broken place. Then stretch yourself with the wrapping away from the location of the fracture for a little bit and gradually relax the constriction until you reach a large part that is healthy.
Then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with windings. Then go with the wrapping to the lower part below the fracture. And your operation will be on constriction and loosening, and softening of it, according to what we said in the first superior constriction.
Then place between the constrictions a piece of soft cloth or linen that will straighten out the tortuosity of the fracture, if there is tortuosity; and if not, do not put anything into it.
Then envelop above this wrapping another one. Afterward, apply above these wrappings small stones made hot at once, unless there is swelling or warm suppuration.
But if there is swelling or suppuration in it, then place above it what settles that suppuration and removes the swelling; and leave it for a few days. Then tie on top of it these hot stones.
And the stones should be from the middle of a broad cane, smooth and well-shaped; or they should be from wood, such as is used for combs, which are made from pine, or geraid, that is, palm branches, or calingi: and the width of every stone should be according to this form and same figure.
However, it is necessary that the stone which is placed on top of the bandage should be a bit thicker and wider than the other stones; and the length of the stone should be according to what allows it to fit onto the member by its size and smallness.
Then tie on top of the stones with another bandage, according to the first constriction. Then bind on top with threads that are well-centered, according to what we said about constriction; and it is, so that your constriction above the place of the fracture is more: and as much as it is farther away from the fracture, constriction should be less.
And it is necessary that the threads are medium-sized in thickness and subtlety, and they are made of soft linen.
Because if the threads are thick, as I saw from the operations of ignorant people who put their threads together from twisted linen cords, it is a great mistake: because the constriction falls short of its proper quality.
And fine threads do not fit together again: because you do not obtain with them that which you wish.
And it is not necessary for there to be less than a finger's width between one asterisk and another.
But if the patient is injured at the extremities of the asterisks after the stricture in healthy places: then place under them a soft cotton or a woven wool, until nothing is hurt from it.
But when there is a fracture with a wound, and there is a disruption of the skin, I come with the recollection of it in the second chapter according to the particularity.
And know that not every fractured member should be bound with asterisks from the first day. Which is because a member when it is large, should not be placed with asterisks above it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the event of cold pustules.
And this is the method of plasters with which the ancients restored what is placed over fractures and dislocations.
Â¶ Common plaster method abbreviated, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children: because it is on account of which heat does not overcome it, nor cold.
And it is, that you take from the chaff of a mill, and it is the floury pulp that hangs on the walls of the mill near the movement of the millstone: and make it as it is, except that you should sift it with egg white: and put its confection not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
Â¶ Another plaster method, restoring a fracture, and dislocation, and twist. Recipe of alum, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugwort: and the juice of each one anise X myrrh, and aloe both equal V and alethi XX are mixed together, and sifted, and mixed with water of alethi: or with egg white, if the patient's complexion is warmed. Then use this poultice. For it is of good composition, quickly restores broken bones, and benefits many people.
Another way to make a poultice that also helps with fractures and sprains. Recipe: mix myrrh, mastich, and white aloe, all in equal quantities of 10; and aloes, ambrosia, and acacia, all in equal quantities of 5. Mix all the ingredients together with much pounding, and sift them, and make them into a liquid with water or egg-white: and administer them.
Another way to make a poultice for joints and separated bones from their proper places; and it calms sudden pain that has occurred, and splits in the bone, and fractures. Take succulent wool, immerse it in vinegar and a decoction of oil, and place it over the spot.
And in this poultice there is no power of restoration, but it is good for soothing a hot abscess and expelling pains properly.
Another way to make a poultice that restores a broken bone. Take the leaves of mulberry and the leaves of Silkworm tree; and grind all into a fine powder, and make a poultice from both.
Another way to make a poultice, administered for the restoration of a broken bone; and when you wish to resolve any remaining abscess. Recipe: take the root of aloe, and camomile, and the flowers of violet, and the flour of herb, each in equal parts; grind all into a fine powder, and mix with wine, unless the limb is already warmed.
If it is already warmed, then mix that with the moist water of coriander, or with water, and use it.
Another way to make a poultice that is stronger than the first one, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of a limb. Recipe of the root of hawthorn and the seed of flax and balm and melilot and sassafras and violet flowers and chamomile, all equal parts. Grind everything together, and make a mixture with water of willow or sweet water or wine. Use the entire thing according to the heat of the member and the quieting of its heat.
But Hippocrates did not say in his book that something should be put on a fractured member during its restoration, unless it is made from wax and oil: nothing else; and he said that it should be the middle between thickness and subtlety.
However, Galen's opinion is that things should be placed on a fractured member during its restoration which have cooling properties of heat, such as myrrh and aloe and frankincense, and similar substances to those we mentioned.
The method of making a plaster that relieves injury and pain. Recipe of equal parts of mugwort, and cypress, and human hair clippings, or bird's feather, and hawthorn, and salt. Grind them together, sift them, and make a plaster out of it.
But the quantity should be according to how much is needed so that the bandage remains, and then it is dissolved. Consider this: if the patient does not experience pain, or pruritus, or the fractured bone is not moved from its place: then do not dissolve it for many days. But if it happens that a severe itching occurs, or troubling pain, or inflammation; then hasten and dissolve it within an hour, and remove the plaster from him: and take a soft cloth, or a marine sponge, and wet it; and submerge it in warm water, and wash with it the place until the itching ceases and the pain subsides.
But if it happens that a severe itching occurs, or troubling pain, or inflammation; then hasten and dissolve it within an hour, and remove the plaster from him: and take a soft cloth, or a marine sponge, and wet it; and submerge it in warm water, and wash with it the place until the itching ceases and the pain subsides. Then let the member rest for one hour. Then place a piece of cut linen over it, soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose-colored oil: and bind it there for one night, until you are secure from hot abscesses, and the swelling of the member subsides, and the pain is removed.
Then go back to a thin stricture and apply a small plaster: and do not tighten it with your first constriction and subtlety, until it is healed.
But if you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all the accidents are now completely gone, and you still need a plaster and stricture, then go back to doing what you did at first evenly.
But if none of those things happen to the member that we mentioned, then you should not release it until after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is released twenty days later, entirely as appears to you from the dissolution of the member, just as we said, until when the fracture adheres and the coagulation of flesh grows over it: add also then in stricture more than your first constriction altogether: and add also in the nourishment of an infirm person, according to what has been previously mentioned.
But if you see the location of the fracture already drying out and wasting away more than it should: then know that nourishment is being prevented from reaching it. Therefore bathe it with warm water all around as you dissolve it, and let it be so for three days, and a little loosen the stricture.
Through this operation, nourishment reaches the member, and it is healed quickly.
But what the foolish restorers do to a fractured member is different from how it should be restored, and instead they restore it by twisting, which is an error of their operation and great fear: and if they were right, they would have said so in their old books, and acted with it.
And I have not seen anywhere their signs in that at all, and it is better, that it not be done.
On the making of an accident on the head.
The species of fracture are diverse, and its figures many; and its causes are distinct.
For from a fracture is the one that occurs by percussion with an axe: and percussion takes away the bone entirely until it reaches the siphac, which is beneath the bone, just as an axle does in wood: and on account of this, this species of fracture is called Axialis.
Or the axe falls upon something of a bone, and cuts its face, and the cut does not penetrate to the end of it: and this species of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Lateral absolutely.
And it is a wound of two fractures, or great, or small.
And from a fracture is the one that is crushing or grinding: and its cause is percussion with a stone, or falling on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either piercing, which is already approaching the membrane, which is beneath the bone: or it is on the face of the bone: and it is a wound of these two fractures, either small or great.
And from a fracture is the one that is hidden in the bone in subtlety of hair: and it is a small crack, and on account of this, this species of fracture is called Capillaris.
And from these is a fracture that occurs by falling or percussion with a stone, and similar things, which cause the bone plate to move inward and creates a hollow: just as happens in a cauldron when there is percussion, and part of it moves inward.
And indeed most often this occurs in the heads of humid bones, such as are the heads of children.
And all these species of fractures are separate and I will come with the recollection of that whole thing in its place.
And we know all these types of fracture by observation over them and inquiry with probes, and removal of corrupted flesh which is above them.
The species indeed of Capillary fracture is known by this, that it appears above the bone, and is wiped away, and covered with a poultice: for the fracture itself appears black.
In the treatment of the fracture, consider first the accidents of the patient: then if you see from the accidents a sign manifestly indicating fear, such as vomiting of choler, and sweating, and loss of understanding, and loss of voice, and syncopes, and acute fever, and evacuations of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar things to these, arising from accidents in many dispositions, by no means is it cured: and if you do not see threatening accidents, and hope for salvation: then begin its treatment.
What is this, if he comes to you wounded at first when it wounds him: and it occurs on winter days, then you must strive in the removal of bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it occurs on summer days, then you must strive to remove the bone before the seventh day, lest what is beneath the bone from pus corrupts and those accidents which we narrated occur.
What if the fracture of the bone has already reached pus touching the brain, and it is with minute cracks and attractions: then you must cut off the broken and worn-out part, according to what I tell you.
And it is necessary to shave the head of the wounded person: and wash over the bone in whatever way possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is easy on the sick person.
What if it happens to you during the detection of bone a flow of blood or a warm abscess: then apply to him what is necessary. And you should fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil; and make the wound quiet until the swelling subsides, and be sure of no bleeding. Then begin the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And this is done according to one or two methods.
One method indeed is to cut the bone with a narrow and sharp saw: and this is its form.
Then apply another Incisorium, after the first one, slightly broader: and this is its form.
Then apply also another Incisorium, even broader than the second one.
And the second method is that you should have with you a number of different saws: some of which are broader than others, and some of which are shorter than others; and they should be at the end of their sharpness, and made of iron or wood; and use ease in striking on the saw, so as not to shake the head or hurt it.
But if the bone is hard and tough: then you must first pierce around it with Terebras that are not deep-cutting; and they are not so called unless because they do not penetrate to the end of the bony part beyond it, because the edge of the Terebra is rounded above what is under its sharp head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents it from sinking in and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take a large number of these Terebras, each one suitable for the thickness of the bone, until the present situation is such that there is a Terebra for every part of the skull: and the length and shortness of its sharp edge should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of Terebras or Trepanums.
The quality of piercing around a fractured bone is that you place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated. Then move the drill to another place and position it so that what is between every hole and the thickness of a radius, or about that.
Then cut with the incisors what is between every hole in the bone; and make it finer than you can on the edge of slightness, as we said, until you remove the bone, or by hand, or with some other instrument that you prepared for this, such as tweezers and fine forceps.
And it is necessary to beware of all caution, lest the drill or incisors touch anything from the splint.
When you have circumnavigated the bone by piercing and it has been separated from the splint, if it was continuous with it, and you have removed it, it is necessary that you smooth and equalize the extremity of what remains in the bone with another similar instrument, an incisor. However, it should be thin and fine rather than other incisors.
If something remains of small bones and is loose, then take it with ease with the tool that you have among your instruments. Then care for the wound after that with linen cloths and ointments which we mentioned.
And another mode of operation is easy, truly remote from fear, which Galen said and praised with great praise: and this is his saying.
