It is necessary for you first to begin uncovering the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more violent and manifest: until when you have uncovered that part, and placed under it the extremity of this lenticular incisor, which has this shape.
And the lenticular part is not cutting anything smooth: and the sharp part of it goes along its two sides in length, as you see: so that the lenticular part is resting on the splint, with its cut side facing the bone.
Then strike the upper incisor on one side with a small mallet, until you cut through the entire bone with ease, as it yields: and be secure with a cloth, lest something unexpected happen that you would utterly fear.
And if the surgeon were not more foolish than men and more ignorant of his art, nor asleep, then if anything remained attached to the bone from a cloth in certain places, he would cut it off with the end of the incisor's handle and free it with ease. For it is freed by him without harm, and without fear.
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate to the cloth and what is cut off from the bone is its surface, and there remain in it roughness and subtle ridges: then it is necessary that you file away this roughness and remove those ridges with fine rasps which you prepared for various quantities, so that it may be possible for you to apply these in every place more suitable and correct, according to what the operation leads you to, and the fractured bone and its shape. And it is necessary that you apply them in proportion to your own measure, as first you file the bone more widely with those fine rasps. Then use a still finer one after that.
And it is necessary that you apply them in proportion to your own measure, as first you file the bone more widely with those fine rasps. Then use a still finer one after that. And do not cease to do this according to continuity, until you come to administering more finely and subtly than all of them.
In each remaining small crack or fracture of the hair-like threads, and in a subtle break, it is necessary to apply care, according to what leads to its correction.
And it is a thing that is not hidden from anyone, to whom any study is devoted in this art, and stands over what we have written, and has been shown in great fractures.
But if the cloth is detected and retained when you are removing the bone: then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and make it as easily as you can on top of that, so it does not become tight.
Then apply the wide bandage on top of it: and do not tighten it unless by the amount that holds the wound just so: and leave it for one day or two days, until you are certain about the absence of suppurative heat. Then loosen it and apply remedies after that which remove excessive dryness: such as lily roots, plantain flour, and powdered thuris, and aristolochia, and similar things to these: and it is, so you make a powder from these remedies which is powdered over the wound like the dried ones.
And universally apply all medicine to it that has a property to remove and not corrode. And beware according to your power, so that the wound is clear in your treatment: nor should it be in that dirty with ointment, nor befouled.
And beware according to your power, so that the wound is clear in your treatment: nor should it be in that dirty with ointment, nor befouled. Nor should you allow pus to gather there entirely: for when pus gathers on the linen cloth of the brain, it corrupts it and putsrefies it, and a great evil befalls the sick person after that; and indeed it often happens in the head wound, when the mouth is cleared from it, especially when its care is neglected, a blackness appears on its surface.
So then consider, if accidents happen to the sick person which we mentioned, know that it is certainly deadly.
And if blackness does not occur, except from the medicine that is placed on it, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness: then you must take one part from honey, three parts from rose oil, and linen threads with them. Then place it on the wound: afterwards, take care of it with remedies that are suitable, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
You should know that only two parts of the nose above are broken simultaneously, or one of them, because it has two bones. Since what is below is cartilaginous, it does not break and only suffers from abrasion, twisting, and bending.
If one of the two parts of the nose is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the nasal opening and use your index finger and thumb to realign that fracture until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with skill and ease. And beware that no severe pain occurs due to your operation.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then you should use the end of a radius bone with a small knob.
If the fracture is in both parts, then do the same for each one and strive to achieve restoration of its natural shape on the first day possible after the fracture. And if not, then seven or ten days later, in a state of calm and free from suppuration.
Then insert a linen cloth at the tip of the nose if the fracture is in one part, or two cloths if the fracture is in both parts, and let the thickness of the cloth be such that it fills the nasal opening.
And some ancient restorers said that linen cloths should be soaked in butter and changed every day. But that does not seem right to me; rather, it is fitting to soak the linen cloth in egg white mixed with miller's flour. Then let the linen cloth be left until the bone hardens and the cartilage becomes stiff.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of the linen cloth, after it has been wrapped in a soft cloth; and this causes the fracture to be held more firmly, so that an invalid may not be hindered from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, sew with linen.
If an abscess should happen to occur on the nose again while you are operating, apply poultices to the nose with cerate, or with a poultice infused in rose oil, or a small one from diaquylon.
But if no abscess occurs, then it is necessary to bind it with poultices made of sesame flour and the powder of burnt frankincense mixed with white of egg. Then place a piece of smooth cloth over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are broken or crushed, then it is necessary to split them open and remove them with instruments that fit. Then bind the wound with a dressing and care for it with what closes and consolidates, using suitable poultices or ointments.
That if a wound should occur between the nose, then it is necessary to care for it with linen cloths and administer a leaden cannula until it heals.
Of the restoration of the lower jawbone when it becomes disjointed.
When the lower jawbone becomes disjointed and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound: then look to see if it is fractured only on the outside and does not split into two parts and curve towards the inside: then recognition of this is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side of the jawbone, you should insert your left index finger into the injured bone: similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the mandible, then insert the index finger of your right hand: and push with it the prominence of the fracture towards the outside with ease, using your other hand from the inside part of the bone.
That if the fracture of the jawbone has already separated into two parts, then it is necessary to apply extension from both sides, according to straightness: until it becomes possible for them to be equal.
If however a commotion or disruption has already occurred in the teeth: then bind them with those that remain using a gold thread, or silver thread, or silk. Then place a cauter above the fractured jawbone. Then place above it a doubled cloth, and place above the cloth a large plaster, well-made, or a piece of equal-sized sole leather, along the length of the jawbone.
Then bind it above, according to the preparation of its binding and its application suits, until it is not destroyed; and command the patient to use tranquility and quietness: and place their food in gentle sips.
If however you think that something is changing its shape in some way: then hurry and release it on the third day. Then straighten out what has been altered from that and apply a poultice to it with barley flour, or with sheep's milk, after you have removed the cauter; and place wool on top of the poultice.
While therefore that poultice remains attached to it and the bone arrangement does not change, release it, so as not to break it, until it is healed; or bind the fracture. For indeed this fracture is bound many times over a period of three weeks.
But if meanwhile a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times to calm it down, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, take note. Namely if the bone fragment or splinter is already separated from the bone: then take care of this carefully, so that you remove those fragments along with what suits you for their removal using instruments.
If however the wound opening is tight, then widen it with a lancet according to the amount of need.
Then when you have removed those splinters and nothing remains of them, or if the wound opening is large: and if not, place above it one of the plasters that fit for this and flesh out the wound until it heals.
About the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Indeed the clavicle is often broken from the anterior part near the shoulder and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Or indeed it is broken and separated into two parts, except that there are complications involved, and it is easier to restore it.
Or indeed it is broken in such a way that the complications occur in the bone, and it is more difficult to restore it.
Or indeed it is broken with an open wound.
And the operation in this case when there is no wound is to have two attendants, one of whom should hold support as follows after the fractured clavicle and the other stretch the neck towards that side.
Then straighten out the fracture with your fingers until its shape is restored, as it should be, and that there be no prominence or concavity.
And if you need more extension, then it is necessary to place a ball of cloth or wool under the weak person's titillic and make its size according to the quantity of your necessity: and extend and elevate the clavicle and tighten the ball with your hand until the fracture is equal, as it should be.
And if you are not able to pull the end of the clavicle to the outside because it has gone too deep: then it is necessary for the weak person to be raised up onto their own chest and you should place a pillow medium-sized under their shoulder, and have your attendant press down on their shoulder towards the lower part until the bone of the clavicle that is in the deep part is lifted up to the top: and then straighten out the fracture and equalize it with your fingers.
But if you feel a sathaium coming out of the clavicle and it moves: then it is necessary to apply pressure above it and extract the sathaium with ease.
If it is retained in the bone, then use your skills in cutting it with one of the instruments that you prepared for this purpose, after you have placed under the furrow an instrument that saves the flesh: and it is an instrument made of wood or iron. And this is its shape.
It is similar to a snail shell which has no hollowing out.
And let its width be according to that which you need for it from the size of the bone and its flatness. Its length, however, is also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end be broad, and the other narrow.
If, however, the disruption of the wound that you made when you extracted the splintered bone is wide, and you have secured it from putridity by applying heat: then gather together the two edges of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear putridity: then fill the wound with linen and cotton according to the size of the wound's incision.
And if putridity occurs, then pour linen soaked in rose oil, vinegar, and wine over it.
And the binding of the bone when it is without a wound and without cutting is, as you put a plaster made from milling flour with egg white over the bone. And place a soft cloth on top of it. Then place a ball under the tilt of it, if you need to. Then take a hametah, that is, the wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long: and its width be about the length of one's hand or roughly that. Then place a doubled cotton cloth over the cloth and plaster. Then take a needle from a fine board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with a little bit of thread around the fracture and the spot where the bone falls. Then place the bone above the fracture as it has been turned back, and wrap it around his neck and under the titillium of both the sound and the unsound parts: and bring it over all the parts, and as you see that the binding holds the fracture tightly bound: and this is what is not hidden from you, and all, so that the thread does not move away from the fractured bone above. And therefore it is necessary to look at the patient every day. Whenever then the binding loosens and you see the thread is already moving away: then correct it and tighten the binding.
Then place the patient's pillow behind his back, and place a small pillow under the titillium of both sides during sleep, so that when he sleeps, aid may come to him from his own side. The fracture of the wing is therefore lifted up through the elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck: and do not loosen the bandage unless something happens in the place that is inflamed from itching or abscess, up to twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face towards it. And add to the binding, and let him go, until he is restored and has healed.
And indeed the fracture of the wing is bound and comforted according to many, in twenty-eight days: and sometimes it happens in some people in a shorter time.
Â¶ On the restoration of the fracture of the Spatula.
The spatula is rarely broken and it breaks far from its edge: and the edges of it are not usually broken.
When then the break is in that place and it breaks in the middle of it, and it is not known except by touch: then prepare for it according to the shape of the fracture. And bring it back to its natural shape in whatever way your ingenuity makes possible.
Then place powdered linen with white of egg, and gentle poultice, and place a small piece of doubled cloth on top. Then place a wide plate made from thin board over it, according to the size of the spatula as a whole, or slightly larger.
If indeed there is a hollow under the plate in some part of the spatula, then equalize it with a gentle poultice, until the plate descends according to equality.
Then bind it with a linen cloth that is long and smooth, by gentle constriction and firmness of the plate, until it does not move from its place.
And check the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage becomes loosened, tighten it and adjust the plate if it has moved from its place, and let the invalid lie on his healthy side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days or twenty-five days.
When this number is completed, then loosen the bandage: and you will be in safety. For it is from bones whose union is not to be feared, nor their separation.
If however a fragment of bone proceeds and pierces under the skin: then cut above it, and remove it. And do what we said regarding a fracture of the clavicle from cauterizing an apostema hot, if something happens as a result.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the chest.
The chest sometimes breaks in its middle part, and it rarely occurs. Its extremities indeed are broken and shattered by accidents that befall it.
When its middle is broken, which bends downwards and causes a severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing; and perhaps it vomits blood, and the concavity of the fractured bone is revealed, and does not remain hidden from sense, its restoration is that the invalid be raised up on his back: and place between the spatulas a poultice.
Then press his shoulders and bring together the ribs from both sides with your hands: and make fine adjustments in proportion to him, according as it is possible and fitting, until the figure of the bone returns, as it should be.
Then place a poultice and plaster over him, and place a splint made from willow or hazel wood, or something similar, on top of it, after you have wrapped him in cloth. Then fine-tune the binding over the fractured bone so that it does not move; and go with the bandage according to the curvature to the back a few times, and tighten it in a decent manner.
Then consider the bandage at every hour, and whenever it becomes soft, tighten it; and if necessity demands to dissolve it due to itching, or corrosion that occurs in the area, or pain, or abscess: then hasten, and dissolve it, and remove the poultice, and correct what happens according to the method of its treatment.
Then reduce the poultice if you see it is needed; and attach a bandage to it until it heals.
Â¶ On the restoration of Ribs when they are broken.
You should know that a rib fracture does not occur except in thick places which follow the back and from the ends of those ribs on the anterior part. And they are not worn away except because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from sensation during an examination with fingers.
And its restoration is done so that you equalize the fracture with your fingers in a possible manner until the figure becomes equal as it should be. Then apply poultices and bind the broken bone with splints if it needs it.
And if the rib fracture is inclined towards the interior: then it causes the patient severe pain and puncture, just like the puncture that occurs in one who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane, and also difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood happens to him many times: and this is a difficult cure.
And now the ancients have been skilled in this with many skills: and among them are those who say that you should place foods for the sick person which will generate inflammation and swelling, so that the belly may be inflamed, and stretch out, and propel a fracture to the outside.
We however shun it, so that no quick cause may lead to making a warm abscess, unless it happens. But if it has already happened, then we add to it.
And some of them said: let something be placed over the swollen area, then it should be sucked with strength, and it is similar to a reason. However, it is feared that the swelling will attract superfluities to the place due to the condition of weakness.
And some of them said: it is necessary to cover the place with wool that has already been soaked in hot oil: and put small pieces of linen between the ribs, until it is filled: so that the binding may be equal when you wrap it around according to its roundness.
Then let the sick person be cured by the treatment of pleurisy from food and medicine.
What if the vital force is oppressive to the sick person, which they cannot tolerate, and the mouth is pierced by puncture or wound, and we fear for the sick person: then it is necessary that we open up over the place, and uncover the broken rib. Then let us put above it the instrument that preserves the suture, whose description preceded: and let us cut open the mouth with ease, and cut into it.
Then let us bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with a suture: and let us care for it with poultices, until it heals.
What if a warm abscess happens meanwhile, then hurry and pour poultices into rose oil, and place them over the place: and let the sick person be cured with what is suitable for the abscess.
Meanwhile it will be spread over the side where he sleeps, until he is healed.
On the restoration of a broken vertebra of the back and neck.
Indeed, fractures of the vertebrae of the neck sometimes occur, and rarely does attrition happen to them; similarly with the back. So when this happens to someone, and you want to know whether they will recover or not: then you should consult. For if you see his hands already relaxed, stunned, and lifeless; and he is unable to move them, or straighten them, or contract them; and when you contract them with your fingers and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, nor feels any pain in them: then you will know that he is not recovering, and in many cases this is mortal.
But if he moves them and feels constriction with your fingers and pricking: then you will know that the vertebra of the bone is safe, and that the patient will recover with treatment.
But if a similar thing happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you want to know whether he will recover or not: then consider his feet. Then if you see that they are relaxed, and what happens in them is the same as what happens in his hands; and when he lies on his back, flatulence and evacuation occur without his will; and when he is raised up over his belly, evacuation occurs without his will; and when he is raised up over his back, and he wants to urinate, but cannot: then you will know that it is mortal. So do not bother with treating him.
But if nothing like that happens to him, the case is easier.
And the treatment of him who suffers from that is to apply a cooling plaster to the apostematized vertebra; so that you put rose oil or lard on it, alone.
Place it on top of it several times a day until the apostema becomes cool; then place one of the comforting, drying, and binding plasters over the spot, and bind it with a bandage. And instruct the patient to use quietness and tranquility. He should not sleep on the part where he finds pain until he is healed.
If it happens that a bone is broken or something has separated from it: then you must make an incision on the skin and remove it. Then gather two flaps of the wound together, if it is large, with a suture. Then care for it with some ointment from unguents until it heals.
If the last bone of the backbone is fractured and it is the end of the tail: then you must put your left hand into the anus and with your right hand align the broken bone as much as possible, and with a second alignment.
Then place something on top of it and remove it; and care for the wound with the cure that preceded it until it heals.
The Restoration of Anches.
Chapter 11.
The bones of the Anches are rarely broken. For if they are broken, then the break of theirs is not, except for comminution at their extremities; and they split along their length and bend inwardly towards the interior; and it happens to the patient that there is pain in the place, and a puncture: and the leg that breaks becomes numb.
And its restoration indeed is, that you stretch out your hand over it, until you stand over the fracture as its shape is.
For if the fracture is only at its extremities, then align it with the fracture, according to how it is prepared for you from alignment, until it becomes like its natural shape.
If however a fracture is in length, or already inclines towards the interior: then make the patient lie on their belly until you prepare for them the restoration of that fracture. When therefore you equalize it, place a poultice on top of it: then place a splint made from wood or skin on top of it: and bind tightly above so that you do not fear the fracture's permutation, nor the removal of the splint.
And equalize the hollow with him as it fills her, until it is held tight by a binding according to equality: and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
If however a warm abscess should happen to him: then cease from stretching it and its restoration, until the warm abscess subsides: and place on top of it what settles on top of it, as previously, and then return to restoring it: and bind it as is necessary.
If however it happens that bone splinters or is crushed, remove it.
Of the Restoration of a Fracture Assisting.
The Assistant is that which is between the elbow and the head of the Saw.
If therefore it breaks, then its restoration is according to one or two methods.
One of which is: that you take a piece of flat wood of middle thickness, according to this shape, which follows.
And it is bound at both ends with two straps.
Then it will be suspended from a raised place, and the patient will sit on a seat. Then he will throw his broken arm over the wood, until it reaches the notch of its own, where a curvature of the wood is attached.
Then hang something heavy above it, so that the assistant may stretch it down below. Then the doctor will equalize the fracture with his own hands simultaneously, until the fracture returns to its proper state.
And another method is, that the patient be placed on his own chest; and suspend his hand from his neck with a ligament. Then instruct two assistants to have one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and have the other hold below it and stretch out each of them to their own side.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then bind below the fracture with a ligament and above it with another ligament; and have each of the two assistants stretch out to their two sides.
If the fracture is near the shoulder extremity, then it is necessary to place one ligament under the deltoid and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary to place a ligament above that spot and above the elbow itself.
Then equal the fracture with ease and without labor, until the fracture is equal as it should be, and a good setting is applied: then bind it, unless a hot aposteme occurs.
If however a hot aposteme occurs, then loosen the binding until the seventh day. And place a compress made of linen soaked in vinegar and rose oil on top of it until the aposteme subsides: then bind it: and the method of binding is to put an poultice over the fracture.
Then place an inunction of new cloth on top of the poultice. Then bring the arm over to support it, and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And place a cloth and bind it over the supporting arm, so that the arm stands in its stellar position: if nothing prevents you from doing so, nor is anything altered on you from the broken bone.
What if you fear that something from the broken bone will be altered on you, then use stars: and it is to place a star over the fracture itself which is wider and stronger than other stars. And you will place between every stellar width of a finger; and the length of the stars should be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers from all parts.
Then tie tightly over the stars the constriction which I said at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is that your constriction should be tighter over the site of the fracture. And as much as the fracture elongates, let the constriction be less.
So if you see the position of the stars and the constriction as we said, in the hour that you restore the member immediately, then do so; and if you fear a warm abscess, then release the constriction and the stars until the seventh day, as we said.
Then examine the ligament in all three days, so that itching or swelling does not occur in the place, or food is prevented from reaching the member, due to the excess constriction. Therefore correct that entire thing according to what we said.
If you are very confident that nothing will happen from it: then do not loosen the ligament unless after three days or more.
And let the invalid's sleep be over his back, and his hand over his stomach; and place under his support a full pillow of wool equally.
And examine at every hour of the night or day, lest the shape of the broken member is not restored, or the ligament becomes relaxed. Therefore correct that entire thing with your power.
And give food to the invalid according to the order which we have prescribed, so that it may be a subtle diet at first, until his mouth begins to feel inclined; then it is necessary for you to increase his food.
For the habit of the aid and foot is, that it should be tightened for forty days; then therefore it is necessary to release it, and to administer a bath, and to treat with ointments which correct that.
If the fracture is foul, wear it out: then do not release from it a ligament and small bones until fifty days, and until two months.
On the Restoration of a Fractured Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two humeri.
Of which one is small: and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is great, and it is that which is placed under the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore the greater humerus alone is broken, or the smaller one: and perhaps they are both broken together.
When therefore the small upper humerus is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower humerus is broken, it is a bad fracture, and its healing is difficult.
And worse it is, when both bones are broken together.
If therefore the bone which is broken is the small upper humerus, then it is fitting for a doctor to apply in its restoration a small extension with ease, until it becomes equal.
If however the greater humerus is the one that is broken: then it is necessary to apply a strong and very tight splint.
And it is necessary to place the shape of a hand in its restoration, and its extended extension above the elbow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher above all the fingers; and let the wrist joint be below all the fingers, or the rest: and let the patient sit square on themselves, and let the elbow be before him, or directly opposite to it, in height, so that the patient does not incur labor.
Then have the attendant extend the arm from the lower part, either by hand or with a bandage. And another attendant extend similarly above.
Then the doctor straightens the bone until it reduces to its better shape as possible.
If there are splinters in a bone fracture, then try to reduce every fragment into its place with your skill.
If indeed a separated splinter appears and causes a wound, and you have no hope of restoring it; then find something to cover it over, and remove it according to the method we mentioned earlier.
But if there is a fracture with a wound, I have already treated that separately, so take the cure for it from there.
But if suppuration occurs at the beginning of your treatment, then tie a cloth made from rose oil, white wax, and a mixture with a consistency between thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth tightly over it with a subtle string until the suppuration subsides: then remove the cerate and apply a prepared poultice made from miller's dust and egg white. Then place plasters, and let the plaster placed over the fracture be a bit wider and stronger.
And know that the number of plasters on an arm is six in many arrangements: whether there is a fracture in one or two places at once.
Then place your bandage over the fractured area stronger and more tightly, and a bit more so towards the upper or lower part of the fracture: or put a milder bandage on top of it for a little while, according to what was mentioned earlier at the beginning of this chapter.
And let the cloths wrapped over the fracture be soft, moist ones: and not hard, rough ones. And let the thread used for binding be made from pure linen, with a consistency between thin and thick, as we mentioned.
And examine the member and its ligaments after days. But if something occurs that requires correction, such as itching which happens to the member: then it is necessary to anoint the member with warm water until the itching subsides, and do not bind the member tightly until the itching subsides; then reapply the bandage.
If the stricture is already softened, and the bone is removed, and similar things: then correct that whole thing with your skill, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive stricture, then it is necessary for you to soften it slightly and leave it for a few days until it reaches the nourishment, and then bind it.
But if none of what we said happens to the patient, then it is not necessary for you to release it unless after twenty days or approximately those.
Then the patient's hand should be suspended from their neck. And let his neck be steady, and be guarded by his own power against any movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or perhaps in thirty-two days, and possibly even in twenty-eight days. That whole thing is according to the dispositions of temperaments and the dispositions of power.
About the restoration of fractures of the Hand.
Know that a fracture rarely occurs in the palmar aponeurosis and joints of the fingers. And it does not happen unless there is much wear and tear.
When therefore a fracture or wear and tear occurs in the palm, then it is necessary for the patient to sit square, and before him should be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand on top of that one, stretched out.
Then the servant should extend the broken bones and the doctor should set them straight until they are joined together with good connection.
Then it is necessary to apply a poultice and plaster, unless there occurs a hot swelling. Then place a bandage on top according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in a linen cloth.
If however a fracture is near the lower part of the foot around the belly of the plantar region, then make something like a sphere out of cloth: and order the patient to bend down over it with their fractured foot: afterwards be tied with a long piece of cloth.
And let it be a pad made of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather may be applied to the foot of the body: and bind it as needed.
If however the fracture is to the exterior, then it is necessary: that you place a pad above, and another pad below on the foot: and let your hand be open straight. Then place the bandage as your hand is bent: and stretch between the fingers with a strap.
And if there occurs a fracture of any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it will be set straight as needed. Then bind with the foot: and if necessary, place a small straight pad on it, so that the fracture may rise up and not be moved.
If however the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, ring, or little finger, then it will be set straight and bound with the healthy finger that follows it: or all of them will be bound according to their continuity: and it is better. Or place a small pad on top of it, as we said about the thumb.
And consider in your restoration and after the aforesaid disposition of warm poultices: and apply with it what is necessary when something happens from that, according to how its narration goes.
Â§ On the Restoration of Fractures of the Hip.
The hip bone is often broken and declared to the sense: because it turns towards the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a bandage is wrapped above the fracture, and another bandage below the fracture. And the patient should be placed on their face.
Then the surgeon should stretch all his ligaments towards his own side according to equality; and this is done when there is a fracture in the middle of the bone.
But if there is a fracture near the root of the hip, then let one ligament be placed on the side of the groin, so that the extension falls upwards; and another ligament beneath the fracture.
And similarly, if there is a fracture near the knee, then let one ligament be placed near the knee, so that the extension falls towards the lower parts.
Then the doctor should equalize the bone with his two hands, until he reduces it according to the likeness of its natural shape; and the bone is joined together by a good union.
Then it is necessary that you place a poultice and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess does occur in it: then let it remain for several days, until the abscess cools down; and then return to your treatment.
And the bandage should be tied thus. It is necessary that you wrap something hard and firm over the fracture, namely a bandage: and double it twice, or thrice: and let the excess remain. Then wrap the leg, until the heel reaches the root of the foot.
And insert a long thread between the hip and leg near the knee towards the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of both parts. Then wrap it back over the leg and hip, with what remains of the bandage.
Then place on the hip above the fracture small stones, and place one stone above the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and hip with soft cloths, so that the bandage may be equalized.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle above the place of the fracture, in three or four windings. And as much as you stretch with the ligament from the place of fracture, your stricture will be less and softer, and gentler.
Then direct it to both ends of the bandage, that you have inserted between the hip and thigh. Bind therefore with each of the three small stones which are above. Then go with both ends of the bandage to the lower parts, until you reach the ankle of the foot. And with both, bind also the ends of the small stones from the other side: so that the bandage is not moved from its place.
Then release the stricture above that, while it remains, so that no damage occurs to the member, nor swelling, nor inflammation, and similar things. What if something happens from that. Therefore hasten with its resolution and correct what happens afterwards, according to what we have said many times.
If however there is a fragment of bone that hurts: then it is necessary to equalize it, if it is possible for you. And if not, find something above it and remove it: and care for the one whose previous mention preceded, until it heals.
And when this fracture of the hip is bound, besides which small stones are added to it, as we said in aid and arm.
However, in its restoration when thus broken, no lameness occurs to the one who is weak. And if it is restored alone, besides which another leg is applied to him, then it is necessary that he who has it will always limp.
And know that the hip is bound in fifty days, or a little added to, or a little diminished from, all this is according to the diversity of complexions and other arrangements.
About the restoration of a broken kneecap.
You should know that a fracture of the kneecap is rare, but attrition occurs frequently to it.
If a fracture does occur to it, then only by a fissure or an opening can its parts be split. And that is both with wound and without wound. And it is set above it entirely with sense.
And its restoration is such that what has been disrupted from its parts with fingers will be equalized until it is gathered and joined according to what is possible in equality and ease and art.
Then place a poultice made from sesame flour or miller's flour with egg white on it; and place above it a round splint, if you need it; and bind it securely with a suitable bandage over it.
Then consider all the arrangements that we have narrated regarding other fractures: such as applying warm poultices and similar ones; so that you oppose every accident with what is suitable for it, until it heals.
Of the Restoration of a Fracture of the Leg.
You should know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick and is called by the name "Cruris," and the other is thin and is called "Femur.
And it happens to both of them according to the forms of fracture which occurs in two arm bones; and because of this, its restoration is done equally, and the operation is one.
If the two bones are broken at the same time, the leg turns into a mass in all its parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg turns towards the anterior part.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below; and it is one of those that are not hidden from you.
Then apply extension, equality, and binding with splints according to what is equal.
However it is necessary, if there is a foul fracture of the leg, having many sutures: so that the extension be less and the alleviation of your treatment.
And in the leg indeed there is something from an operation above the arm: and it is, that when you equalize the splints, and complete all your operation, then take two pieces of pine wood, which is used in the manufacture of tables, which are placed under benches: and they are made from palm-shaped spatulas: and choose from them those whose thickness is small, nor are they minute; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downward.
Then wrap each piece around with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg from one side, and another from the other side: and let them be from the knee to the lower part of the foot.
Then tie the two pieces together in three places at both ends and the middle. For when a stricture is prevented, the leg inclines to the right and left, and stands in good position.
And now indeed a cradle should be made of wood according to the length of the leg, and placed in it, so that the leg may be preserved from movement.
And very much indeed it is necessary to do this when there is a wound properly with the fracture.
Then consider the leg every day and strive with it with utmost study against suppuration, or inflammation, or other things. If therefore something of those happens, then oppose it with what is necessary until it heals, if God wills.
On the fracture of bones of the foot and fingers.
Indeed, a complete fracture does not occur in the calcaneus.
The bones of the foot sometimes experience fracture, and also the fingers experience a little fracture: and it does not happen to them unless by attrition in many dispositions.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and you see those bones elevated above themselves, then the invalid should place his foot on prepared ground as one walking.
Then stand still, and place your foot on what is raised from those bones; afterwards, press or trample it: and straighten it until it returns to its place.
Then apply a poultice and a bandage on top; and place a small flat board under the foot, with two flat heads.
Then bind it to the foot with a binding that is fitting, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things that are needed.
When therefore three or four days pass for him, then loosen the binding. For you will find the bones equalized, whether they are fractured or displaced. The operation therefore is as you see.
But if some fingers are broken, then restore them and straighten them as I have narrated to you for the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint under your finger according to the length of the finger, and make it slightly wider; afterwards, place this board under the foot as I have narrated to you, and bind it with a binding that is fitting.
But if what is broken is from two or three or more bones, then place a splint over the entire finger of its own size, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board to the lower part of the foot; and let it stand on one head of the board outside the belly of the foot, in order to hold good retention.
And it is fitting for you to remember, and not forget, but rather consider all that I have told you about other fractures from the accidents we mentioned; and oppose each accident with what is appropriate.
Of the fracture of a woman's womb, and the bone of her pelvis, and the man's spine.
When a woman's vulva is broken, then make her sit square. Then incline her towards the side of her own back for a little while, and hold her back.
Then the midwife should fill up her vulva with cotton until it is full; and let it be in the vulva as a sphere.
Then the woman should be shaken, and her back lifted gradually. For that cotton will come out when it becomes like a sphere before the entrance of the vulva, and the broken opening returns.
Then place a poultice on her back. When she wants to urinate, remove the cotton with ease, then let her urinate; and return it according to the manner in which you first inserted it: then let it return to its original poultice.
Let that be done for seven days or about, for the opening is being restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and tie it over the hole with a reed tube, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the tube with force, until the bladder is inflated inside the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then it will be filled with cotton, and remain for a few days, as we have narrated, until she is healed.
And when the opening of the scrotum of a man or woman is broken, then apply what we have narrated for the restoration and equalization of that which was said about bone anchors.
And the rectum is not hidden in these nearby fractures which touch only slightly, especially for someone with a certain study or use.
And understand my book thoroughly, because the fracture itself signifies to you many ways of straight paths in restoring and constraining its own. Therefore, understand.
In the phallus of a man when it is broken, take a goose's gizzard and insert it into it. Then place a wrapping of cloth over it and wrap it up. Let it be for three days or nearly so, and it will heal if God wills.
On the restoration of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
It is fitting for someone who has experienced a fracture with a wound, especially if it is a major one such as the hip bone or some other similar area, to bleed immediately and then proceed to apply the remedies we have previously mentioned.
But if the wound flows with blood, then you should hasten to apply a poultice to it, crushing powdered verdigris over the top of it; or, if that is not available, then use something else.
Then begin to restore the fracture on the same day and do not delay it unless an abscess occurs.
But if a warm abscess occurs, then put off the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause bad accidents to occur.
But if the fractured bone is prominent and visible above the skin: then it is necessary that you gently bring it back into place with your hands, equalizing it with a small amount of ease and extension.
But if there is no preparation for its extension or equalization by your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is one made of iron, whose length is seven or eight fingerbreadths, and its width is according to the size of the wound. And for that reason it is necessary for a doctor to take from them three or four, according to the size which is needed for its cure, in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round; and let it have a small thickness so that it is not doubled upon itself under pressure during the operation; and let its sharp extremity have a rim at its ends. And let it be above him to the thickness from the middle of him up to the lower parts.
And this is its shape.
It is necessary in this operation that the acute and curved end of it be placed over the projecting bone; and push it with him together, until the bone returns and becomes equal by some measure.
Then therefore make the ends of the fracture equal to each other.
But if the ends of it are broken and fine, and do not receive it with good reception, then it is necessary to cut off that end of the bone until the instrument has power over it.
But if you are not able to reduce them as we described completely, then cut it off with the saws that suit it from the ones we mentioned: or saw it through with a saw in whatever way is possible for you.
Then afterwards remove what remains on the bone from roughness and small fragments of bark.
When therefore the bone is reduced and the invalid finds a severe and painful pain after the reduction of it, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Make it so. For you are doing a great benefit to the invalid when your restoration is completed which you do for the bone.
Then immerse the cloth in black wine that is stiptic. And properly if it is in summer. And do not place a cautery or anything containing oil on the wound, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption to occur within it.
Then administer honey in the hour that completes your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound uncovered: thus, you will cut with forceps a hole in the swellings according to the size of the wound.
And beware of every caution, lest you sew up the wound with the fracture. Many times indeed foolish doctors have done this: and they have brought harm to their sick patients, or death, or putrefaction, or gangrene. And let your constriction be soft, gentle, different from the other constrictions of broken bones.
But if the wound is bad or a great one, and you fear certain evil consequences that we have mentioned, and you find pain suspended in the place: then it is not right to apply poultices to it. And make them swellings from hard cloths in the place of poultices, and bind it with them.
But when it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound already beginning to generate pus: then remove from above it the cloth that you placed on it with wine. Then administer lycium and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds, as the four-pharmaceutical ointment and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every day morning and evening, until it consolidates and heals.
And it is necessary to prepare the limb in such a way that pus flows from it to lower parts with ease.
Which if many days pass over the wound and it does not consolidate nor is pus cut away from it: then you will know that there are small bone splinters inside. Therefore, it is necessary to explore the wound with a probe. As many of those bone splinters are separated, remove them and take out the others. And as for those that are not separated and pierce the member, causing pain and making it suffer, try to cut them away and remove them in any way that is possible for you.
Which if it happens to the wound to become gangrenous, or ecchymotic, that is, putrid, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction: then you should apply to each one of them what suits it for its treatment, as was narrated in its own chapter.
And as for that which is necessary so that my speech stands firm, and to whom you should present your mind when a bone breaks out and protrudes above the limb, just like the hip bone, and similar ones from major bones, it is, do not presume its attraction, for many times death occurs because of this, indeed let it be, until it putrefies.
Perhaps indeed it will fall by itself after twenty days or thirty.
Then therefore take care of the wound if you think fit; and if not, let it be.
About the treatment of a knot (or swelling) in the track of a fracture.
This knot often occurs in the track of the healing of a fracture. Especially those near joints: and it is corrupted from the figure of the member. And perhaps prevents the member from performing its natural function.
Then consider, if the knot is recent: apply medicines to it that have astringent properties, such as aloe, and olibanum, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar ones.
So that you take some or all of them: and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them on the knot in a poultice, and bind them tightly over it.
And let the stricture be, nor loosen it for many days. Then loosen it and repeat another one, until the knot is removed.
Or bind over it a lead plate decently. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the body.
But if the knot has already become hardened into a stone, and is tightly fixed, and necessity presses urgently to remove it: then find above it on its upper part, and cut off the projecting excess: or shave it with certain rasps, until it is removed: and take care of it, until it heals.
Of the healing when it is restored and the member remains after that subtle, not according to its own nature.
When the bone fracture is restored and the member remains after that subtle and weak, then it does not become sound unless many causes intervene. One of which is the multitude of ligament solution and binding not according to what is necessary; or because of the excess constriction of the ligaments, until nourishment is prevented from reaching the member; or because of excessive embrocation at an unsuitable time; or because of a lack of blood in the weak body and its debility.
And the cure for that is to feed the sick person and make their body tender until the blood is multiplied within them. And administer a bath. And bring joy and gladness upon him.
Then place pitch on the member, so that it may draw the pitch to itself and receive much nourishment. And frequently apply an embrocation of it with warm water, until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural shape.
Of the healing of bones fractured when they are restored in a twisted manner.
When it happens to a member that has already been restored after its healing and becomes twisted, or the bone is fractured, or swelled up; and is marred in this way by the form of the member, although the member is not prevented from performing its natural function: then one should not take heed of his speech who thinks that the member should be broken again.
And already many foolish doctors and restorers have done this on our earth.
And this operation is very reprehensible, leading to death or great fear, so that it becomes mortal.
But if the swelling and knotting are recent: then it is necessary to apply an embrocation with warm water in which soothing herbs have been decocted, such as the leaves of althea, its root, and melilotus, and similar things. And a poultice shall be made for him with a soothing poultice, as a diaper well-made.
Or the pulp of the tall-root shall be taken and pounded with hen's fat or sesame oil, and applied with it.
Or they shall take figs that are fat, and pound them with dove's down, and similar things which are called from medicines diminishing consolidation.
And sometimes the swelling is dissolved by frequent gentle friction with wet hands. And motion of the member should be administered to every part at all hours.
But if the curvature has already occurred and has become severe and has turned into a knot, and necessity demands its correction with iron: then it is necessary to cut above it, and to break the continuity of the bone, and to remove what exceeds from the swelling or bone with fine instruments; and facilitation should be administered in that place with solicitude and diligence.
On Speech in Dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of some joints from their place. Therefore it is prevented by its own motion, and the figure of the member is disfigured. And it causes great pains and injuries to happen to the weak person.
When therefore dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to hasten to restore it: lest it be utterly delayed.
For if it is delayed, the place will suppurate, and perhaps with that, the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore one should not move or stretch it at the hour of its suppuration. For it often causes spasms and painful injuries.
But when that happens, then one should hasten to bleed the patient. Then let it be given a rest until the suppuration subsides.
Then let the member be anointed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And every member should be treated with that which its memory will come to its place.
I have indeed arranged the chapters on dislocations, and also according to what preceded on fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Lower Jawbone.
Two jawbones are hardly dislocated except rarely.
And the dislocation of both is done according to two methods. For either they are removed from their places with a small removal and are made soft for a short time. Or they are dislocated with a complete and full dislocation until they become soft up to the chest, until the patient's saliva flows, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped around his speech.
When however the dislocation of it is small, then it returns to its natural state with a few movements by itself.
If however the dislocation is complete and full, then it is necessary that reduction be administered quickly and not delayed at all: and it is for the minister to hold the patient's head; and the doctor should insert his thumb into the root of the dislocated jawbone between the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he should insert both thumbs simultaneously if the dislocation is from both sides. And the remaining fingers of his hand should be outside, with which he can apply pressure.
And order the patient to clean their jawbones and prepare them for eating in all directions. And the doctor should apply pressure to the jawbone and push it until it returns to its place.
But if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jawbones are simultaneous: then administer a poultice with warm water and oil until the easy reduction of both is accomplished: nor should you delay the reduction of both entirely, as we said.
When therefore they return and are equalized, and the patient's mouth is closed, and they do not soften: then place over them wool pads with a mixture made from wax, and rose oil.
Then it will be bound with ease with soft ligaments.
And let the patient's sleep be above their back; and their head be placed between two pillows, so that they do not move to the right or left: nor should they attempt, nor be forced, to chew anything: rather, their food should be given by spoon, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is relaxed: then let them eat what they want, and administer it with ease.
Let them not exert themselves over their own mouth's operation during eating and drinking, and also during yawning, until the jaw is relaxed and healed.
But if the reduction of the two jawbones becomes difficult when they are dislocated for some time and do not turn back to their places: then often fevers occur, and constant headaches; and perhaps the patient's bowels are loosened, and perhaps pure bile is vomited.
When therefore you see that: then you will know that it is a loss, and often he who experiences that dies within ten days.
Chapter on the Reduction of Dislocations of the Clavicle and End of the Shoulder.
The clavicle indeed does not become dislocated from the inward side, due to its continuity with the breastbone. And it becomes dislocated sometimes to the outside and is declared by feeling.
And its restoration is when the patient lies on their back and stretches out their arms. Then compress the place with your palm in strong compression, for it will return of itself.
Then place a poultice and wool pads over it, and bind it.
The extremities of his which follow the elbow and continue with it are not dislocated unless rarely. When he is dislocated on some day: then it is necessary that he be reduced and set right according to what we said, and what is prepared for you.
Then place a poultice and bandage over it. And command the patient to adhere to tranquility and quiet until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the shoulder extremity is reduced when it also separates from its place.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the Shoulder.
Know that the shoulder does not become dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it becomes dislocated towards the lower part of the scapula. The second is that it becomes dislocated towards the breast. And perhaps it becomes dislocated towards the upper part of the shoulder: and this happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated towards the back due to the spatula. And it is not dislocated towards the front due to the nerves.
And indeed it becomes dislocated and comes out towards the lower part of the scapula, especially in those whose flesh is meager. Because it goes out quickly from them and goes back in quickly.
In those however whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite of that, namely because it goes out with difficulty and comes back in with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people that they are struck or fall, and the shoulder becomes inflamed with a warm abscess, and is considered to be because it has already become dislocated.
Therefore, you must try that until you are certain of it, and then proceed to its cure.
And it is known as a dislocation when it turns towards the lower parts in a tilting manner. And distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a sound one. For you will find a manifest difference between them, and you will find a concavity on the head of the shoulder, and under the tilting part at the touch of the head of the shoulder as if it were an egg: and an infirm person is not able to reduce his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the breast or upwards: then you will find a manifest touch, nor does it hide.
And this kind of dislocation is easily reducible when it is recent or the patient is an infant.
And its reduction is by lifting your hand up above. Then place your fingers underneath the tilting part of it, and lift the joint with strength upwards to its place. And the minister will lift his hand and stretch it upwards. Then place it back down, for it returns quickly on its own.
But if it does not return as we said, and there are many days since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary for the infirm person to bathe in warm water, and a poultice should be applied that softens and soothes, just as if roots of elder, foenugreek, and mellilot were being decocted in water, and it should be applied.
Then the infirm person should be placed on his back: and a ball and wool should be placed underneath the tilting part of it, which is equal to both softness and tightness.
Then the doctor should place his heel on top of the ball and lift the head of the shoulder with strength, and draw the infirm person's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method: and it is, that you make the present man who is longer than the patient, stand at the side, and insert his shoulder under the armpit of the patient; and lift up the armpit of the patient to the higher parts, until the patient is suspended in the air. And let another assistant pull the hand of the patient down to his lower abdomen. But if the patient is light: then it is necessary to suspend something else with him, so that he may be weighted down: for the dislocation will return immediately.
And he is also reduced according to another method: and it is, that a long piece of wood be placed on the ground, whose head is rounded in shape, like the sleeve of a mortar, which is not too thick nor too thin. Then a soft cloth should be placed under the patient after soft cloths have been put on his head, and the patient should stand according to the length of the wood.
Then his hand should be stretched out to the lower part on one side, and his body should be extended on the other side with strength; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But if his reduction is difficult through all that we have said, then use this cure: and it is, that you take a piece of wood whose length is the measure of two arms, and its width the measure of four fingers, and its thickness the measure of two fingers: which has a rounded head, so that its entrance may be eased into the depth of the armpit, according to this shape.
Then soft cloths should be wrapped around the rounded head, so that it may not hurt the patient.
Then it should be placed under the armpit of the patient, so that his hand or arm may be extended up to the lower part over the wood; and the wood should be tied above the assistant's help and arm and end of the hand.
Then the arm should be placed over the step of the stairs in width, and the hand should be extended down, and the rest of the body should be let go suspended from the other side. The suture indeed enters immediately.
So when the entrance is completed in whatever way possible: then it is necessary to place under the titillium a ball of wool of equal size. Then place the prepared poultice made from mill dust with frankincense and egg white on top of the shoulder, as it is wrapped over.
Then bind the string under the titillium with a tight knot that is fitting; and let the ligament be wrapped over the poultice above: and let his hand be attached to her neck, and let it be left undisturbed for seven days.
And it is necessary to place a light diet upon the weak person until the limb is comforted; for it is more helpful for its healing.
Then a larger one should be placed after seven days or five, and exercised with movement. If it is strengthened and not softened, then he is already healed.
If the suture is dislocated many times, due to moisture that happens to it, or due to another cause: then it is necessary to apply a cautery with three openings, according to what was mentioned earlier in the chapter on cautery.
But if this is done and the ligament is released after seven days and the suture does not hold firm, and the poultice and bandage are reapplied several times and still do not hold, and it falls and cannot be lifted to its higher position: then you will know that the nerves of his shoulder which are related to the head have already been cut or stretched or softened: and you will know that the suture will never take hold in its proper place.
But the dislocation that occurs towards the chest and breast, or towards the back, happens with a push and stretching with one's hands: until it returns and a remaining bandage and care are administered, until it is healed.
If it happens after healing that heaviness occurs in a member and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use a bath many times until this heaviness is alleviated and returns to their natural state.
Of the cure of cubital dislocation.
Know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly it returns to its place with difficulty as well; and indeed it dislocates towards all parts, and particularly towards either the front or the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: for it falls under sight and touch, according to whatever shape it may be dislocated. And when you bring together the dislocated elbow with a healthy one, it is made clear to you. For the joint is hollowed out, and cannot bend the arm, nor touch its shoulder with it.
And it is necessary to hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess occurs.
For if a hot abscess occurs, it makes the reduction difficult: and perhaps it will not be cured at all, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocations, and more violent in its pain; and often descends with it to death.
And the restoration of it when it is one of those that can be reduced is, that his hand should be stretched out with two hands: and his arm should be expanded, and the doctor's hands should be above the elbow and below it, and he should push the joint with the tips of his fingers or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed the dislocation is to the front: then it returns in such a way that he doubles his hand with his own, until he strikes the dislocated elbow with the base of his palm.
What if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then apply a violent and strong stretching: and it is, that two assistants should stretch out his arm: and they should also hold him so that he is not moved during the stretching.
Then the arm is turned towards every part after it is wrapped around his hand with a doubled cloth, or a wide band.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his own hand, he should anoint it with oil, so that it may be one of those things which aid in the ease and facility of the joint. Then he should push the joint with a strong thrust until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary that a poultice be placed over it, which has purging and cooling properties with white of egg: and it should be bound tightly with a suitable bandage. And the arm should be suspended from the neck of the invalid, and let it rest for several days.
Then it should be released. If the joint is stable in its place, then release the bandage from it and let it go.
And if you see the joint not well bound, then apply a poultice, and bandage, and let it rest for several days until it is bound. Then release it.
But if heaviness occurs to the joint after the reduction, and it is in motion: then administer a moistening treatment by bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is relaxed, or place a poultice of fat goat's milk over the joint. Then bind it, and let it rest on it by day and night. Then remove it, and introduce him into the bath. When he sweats, then stroke the joint with a tempered friction. Then apply over it an ointment of aloe, one way, and secondly, and thirdly, with the entering into the bath, until it is relaxed.
And if you wish, place over it warm cow dung, moist and heated with butter, and bind over him.
Do that several times. This is itself soothed and returns to its natural condition.
On the treatment of a dislocation in the shoulder.
The shoulder of Mahassem is frequently dislocated, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of his dislocation is easy. On the contrary, it is not so with other joints. However, it is necessary that the speed of reducing his dislocation be in the hour when it is dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed: or a hot abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of his dislocation is, to place Mahassem's weak hand on a table, and have the attendant extend it; and the doctor should put his own palm on the prominence of the joint, and push it until it returns.
However, it is necessary to consider whether the palm has already receded towards the front of the hand; then the weak person should place the back of their hand on the table for extension and reduction: and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be inward on the table, that is, the inner part of his hand should be placed on the table so that the doctor's hand falls on the prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaster over the abscess: and let him go, without repeating that.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you effect a reduction after the days have passed, but the joint remains according to its own twisting: and it harms the patient nothing, except that the hand is softened and cannot grasp anything: then you will know that the nerves are cut or rubbed.
It is not suitable for it to be treated with an operation, except to cauterize it near its fall. And perhaps it does not matter.
So when Almahassem does not return, then place upon him the poultice that we have described. Then it should be bound and left for five days: afterwards it should be loosened and left, and the hands should be exercised.
But if it is deprived of movement and something heavy happens to it; then soothe it with warm water and gentle friction a few times, until it becomes soothing.
On the healing of finger dislocations.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, or from one of them towards the back of the palm or its belly; then stretch out the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger and suspend it towards the direction in which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it and stretch it out until it becomes straight on that day itself.
When night falls, bind it as previously described: and do not cease to loosen it by day; and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do that for several days until it is bound.
And similarly do the same with him if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; and bind him to that part, and perform your initial operation with him until he is healed.
And similarly do the same with him when it is dislocated to other parts.
On the healing of dislocation of the spine.
When it happens to someone that the spine of their back or neck is fully dislocated, or many vertebrae are moved from their places; then there is no cure for this, since death hastens the invalid.
And the sign of this is that the invalid's discharge flows out without his will, nor can he restrain it; and many times certain parts of his body become soft, or one of his legs, or one of his arms, or one of them: or one vertebra is moved from its place.
Many times it is removed because many times it is removed to four parts.
Therefore, that which is removed towards the back, is called Humpiness.
The cure of it is that you consider then if Humpiness happens from childhood: then there is no cure in it nor any healing whatsoever.
But in that which happens by chance or striking or similar things, the old methods of treatment have already been described at length. Of which most do not return with benefit.
But I have already shortened it from that which has little excuse due to its proximity to my intention: and the explanation of mine in which I explained it, and shaped the instrument differently than they showed it, and explained it.
Therefore, I say that in humpiness which occurs from the anterior part, there is no remedy nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which occurs on both sides as well.
And it is not cured from them, except that which occurs on the back properly, as I narrate: and it is, that the infirm person lies on his face on a level platform near the wall: and a damp cloth is spread under him, so that his chest is not hurt.
Then a straight piece of wood is placed and fixed in the hollow of the ground at the end of the platform, near his head: and another piece of wood around his feet at the other end of the platform: and the attendant holds onto the wood and stands not steady in the hollow. And the other attendant hold the other one.
Then wrap a soft and firm bandage around the head of the infirm person, and extend the end of the bandage to the wood that is by his head: and tie it to it.
Then tie another bandage above his ankles, and above his knees, and at his calves. Then gather all the ligaments and tie them to another wood that is near his feet.
Then let every assistant stretch out to the part of the wood where the ligament is, and the two woods should not be removed from their places where they are fixed; yet they should not be secured as we said.
And let the doctor place his palms on the spondyle with strength until he returns; or place them on it on a board. Then let him connect his feet to the board until he returns.
If however he does not recover through this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms' length. And make an opening in the wall of which we spoke, which is near the sick person, to where the end of the board will go. Then place the middle of the board on the spondyle. And let the doctor place his feet on the other extremity. And tighten well, until the spondyle is compressed, and he returns to his place.
Then if you wish, work with a drill that is wrapped around your hand; and it is, to fix two wooden poles in the ground at the head of the sick person, one foot long each, and at his head.
And let the length between all the wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in every place where the drill runs: and let each piece of wood be firmly fixed in the ground very much, so that it will not move at all.
And insert a round wood: and the drill is where the ligament is twisted in two holes of two woods.
And at the end of it is a hole where the wood is secured, whose length is one palm along with what twists around: and similarly on two other woods.
Then tighten the ligaments that you tightened in the chest of the sick person with the drill that is at his head: and the ligaments that you tightened in his legs with the drill that is at his feet.
Then let him stand by all the sawing ministers, who tightens his hand with a linen cloth, with which the saw is turned; and the surgeon adjusts the spongy thing, as we said.
This is the shape of the saw that follows.
Then after the sponge returns and the place is adjusted, then it is necessary to put on an absorbent plaster with egg white. Then a poultice. Then place on top of the plaster a plate from wood, whose width is three fingers, or roughly that. And its length should be as long as to fit the space of the gibbousness and above the healthy part of the sponge.
Then it will be bound with the bandage that we said. And the patient should use a subtle diet until he is healed.
But if something remains prominent in the place at the end of the healing process: then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and soothe, with application of the plate which we narrated over a long period. And now it is administered with a lead plate.
And sometimes it happens that prominence occurs at the end of the back's sponge and is thought to be through it a displacement, and that bone which now protrudes. Therefore one should not presume on him with this medicine: perhaps indeed it will cause death.
A cure for a dislocated Anca.
Know that the joints of the Anca and the joints of the shoulder do not occur except by dislocation alone; nor does it happen to them what happens to other joints due to slight removal and hollowing.
And indeed the joint of the Anca is dislocated into four parts. Which is because it is dislocated to the front and to the back.
And indeed it is dislocated very much towards the inside; and a little towards the front and back.
And the cure for this dislocation towards the inside is: because when you join a sick leg with a healthy one to it, it is longer; and his knee is prominent more than that of a healthy person, and the sick person cannot double his foot at the groin; and the next place following the groin is inflamed by a hot apostema, because the head of the femur has already reached there.
And the sign of it to which a dislocation occurs towards the outside is, that its accidents are contrary to these accidents.
And the sign of it to which a dislocation occurs towards the front is, because it stretches a healthy leg according to its complement; yet it does not double it without pain in the knee: and if it tries to walk, it cannot step forward beyond that point: and it retains urine, and the groin becomes inflamed, and during walking there is a bruising over the heel.
And the sign of it to which a dislocation occurs towards the back is, that it does not stretch the knee, nor can it double it before doubling the groin: and its leg is also shorter on one side: and the groin is softened, and the head of the femur is at that place.
The reduction indeed of this kind of dislocation is, that you should consider then, whether the dislocation is old and has already become chronic with the person having it.
