Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its natural disposition: then there is no cure at all. So therefore it should not be presumed of him entirely.
However, in the one whose dislocation is recent and belongs to one of the four kinds of dislocations: then hasten to do this, that you will loosen the joint, and stretch it towards the inside and outside, and move it right and left: perhaps it will return, and not require another cure.
If it does not return in this way, then one should prepare a strong servant, and have him stretch his leg from the lower part: either with his hands or with a band that will be fastened above his knee on his leg. And let another servant stretch out his hand above, so that he may insert his hands under the patient's thighs. Then let him bind with a soft band over the root of the hip joint, and hold the extremity of the other band.
And let his extension be either from the front part of the groin to the part of the fork, or from the back to the part of the back.
And let all their extensions be one by one, until the patient is lifted up with his body from the ground and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common type among four kinds.
So if the dislocation returns as we said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for all four kinds of that which I shall say about proper cure.
Indeed, in the proper reduction when the dislocation is to the inside, it is necessary that the patient be raised up over his healthy side. Then he should place a band above the root of the hip joint, in that which is between the head of the hip and the place that is below the groin. Then he should extend the band upwards from the part of the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the fork.
Then let another strong servant take his arms and reduce the swollen part of the weak hip joint, and stretch it out to the outside with a vigorous extension. Now it is returning to its place.
And this kind of treatment is easier for the other kinds of cure by which this member is reduced.
But if it does not obey you and does not enter with this kind of treatment altogether: then one should bind the two legs of the patient at the same time with a strong, soft band, over both heels, and over both knees; and let each length be as long as four finger-widths from its corresponding part: and let the weak leg be stretched out longer by an amount equal to two finger-widths.
Then the patient is suspended above a wooden head, which is located in the house: and its length from the ground is the measure of two arms.
Then instruct the strong youth to support the patient's head with his shoulder blade; and let another young man suspend himself along with the patient; and let the other youth push with all his might, supporting the hip joint with vigor, for the juncture returns to its place quickly.
But when the reduction is done by his own effort, and there is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the patient lies on a platform, as we have described for one with a hump. And let the ligament be stretched over his leg, by himself, and over his chest.
Then two pieces of wood are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then a piece of wood is placed in the middle of the platform, fixed well, on which wet cloths are wrapped so that the patient is not hurt; and let the wood be between his hips, so that he is not drawn down to the lower parts during the stretching.
Then every assistant stretches out to their side, and the doctor equalizes the reduction with his own hand.
If then it yields to reversal, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the table and press it down, as we said for a humpback, evenly. However, it is necessary that the patient lies on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the patient's leg be stretched quickly; and it is according to this same description on a platform: and let the doctor place his right hand on the patient's groin. Then pull him with one hand, and with that traction place him extended to the lower part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the back: then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended downwards, and let him be raised above the ground: rather, let him be placed on a hard surface, just as it is also necessary when his ankle is dislocated outside, according to what we said about his lying on two anchors: and it is above his face and stretched ligaments: just as we said recently.
And it is necessary that a compression be applied with a splint also on the place where the joint will emerge, when the reduction is complete according to what is needed.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is, that you stretch the two legs of the weak person; and when you see them equal, and the weak person tightens his leg and expands it without resistance: then you will know that the member has already returned as needed. Then join the two hips, and apply a poultice, and tie with an emplastrum, that is, a bandage: so that the groin does not move to any part; and the weak person should remain quiet for three days or four.
Then loosen the bandage and poultice, and compare the leg with the other one; then if you see them equal in status, then you will know that the dislocation has already been stabilized. Therefore, let the weak person go to walking.
And if you see something softening: then go back and reapply the poultice to it, and tie it according to your initial tightening; and leave it also for three days.
Then loosen it and make it slow down in walking on both sides for several days, until it is well comforted.
Of the Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwardly, inwardly, and downwardly, namely, to the rear.
And it does not dislocate forward.
And the sign of this dislocation is that you hasten the weak person to place his leg against his own hip. But if it does not adhere to the hip: then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all three forms of this dislocation is that you make the weak person sit straight, so that he already stretches his leg if he can over it, and adheres your back to his face; and place his foot between your feet.
Then attach your face to his knee with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers over his knee. Then apply both sides of his knee with your palm with force. And let another assistant stretch out his foot until it returns to its place.
And the sign of return is when the leg adheres to the hip with gentleness, not with difficulty.
Then bind him and attach the leg to the hip. Then bind them together with a bandage for three days or four. Then loosen them.
And the sick person should not use walking for several days until he is healed.
But if the return is refused to you, then apply a strong extension with bandages, of which the previous account was given in the cure of ankylosis, until it returns.
Of the Cure of Dislocation of the Heel.
The heel is sometimes moved slightly and sometimes dislocated according to its nature. And the dislocation occurs either towards the inside or towards the outside.
And the sign of this dislocation is that you see the Heel inflated, proceeding to the part to which it dislocates.
But the cure of this displacement is easy and simple: and it is, to stretch with ease with your hands, and equalize, until it returns.
And in the cure of this when it is displaced according to its nature, it is necessary that you make the sick person sit straight: and let a strong assistant hold him very firmly from behind at the middle part of his back.
Then hold with your right hand his foot from the upper part of it, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot like a cauls.
Then draw his foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left hand two fingers.
Then stretch with the left and push the ankle bone of the foot with the right towards the knee without strain.
Do that twice as we have told you.
Then push the ankle bone of the foot towards the knee on the third side and you pull back the ligament.
So if it returns to its original position or to one of the two sides according to this method, and you see the foot is straightened, that is good; and if not, then iterate the procedure on it again, since it will return.
But if its reduction is prevented from you as we have told you: then make the patient lie on his back on the ground and place a wedge of earth firmly fixed between his thighs: and you wrap yourself around him with a cloth so that he does not hurt himself.
Then let the assistant take hold of his thigh: afterwards let the assistant straighten out the other foot, either with their own hands or with a bandage that ties above the ankle. Then let all assistants stretch out against the stretching of their counterpart: and the wedge shall stand between the two thighs of the patient holding him so that his body is not drawn towards the lower parts at the stretching.
Then let the doctor straighten out the dislocation with his own hands and have the assistant hold the healthy leg towards the lower parts. For the dislocation returns quickly.
When therefore the dislocation returns and its reduction is declared to you as healthy: then apply a poultice and bandage, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or fear of the nerves which are above the posterior heelbone, lest the bandage be too tight and hurt them.
Then let him go for two days, or three. But if the bandage becomes loose, bind him again: then release him on the third or fourth day: and prohibit the patient from walking for forty days. If indeed he is attempting to walk before that space, there will be no security lest a dislocation occurs on top of him, and it is corrupted, and does not receive subsequent treatment.
What if a warm abscess should happen to him: then you must administer to him that which our previous account told of in another place for treatment, and an embrocation, until it is removed.
On the Dislocation of Fingers and Foot, and Curing.
It is necessary that what has been dislocated from them be reduced with a little extension without effort: and indeed it is not difficult, nay rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in certain knots of the back of the foot: then it is necessary that the sick person should sit with his foot on an equal spot on the ground, or on a board: and he should stand as one walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot over what protrudes from those joints. Then press that with your foot with strength, until it returns, and you see it is now equal, and does not appear in its elevated place.
Then place a board under his foot that will take the whole foot, which has two ends. Then bind him with a bandage to a decent tightness for three days. Then loosen it and make him rest from walking for many days, until he is bound and secured by the reduction.
On the Kinds of Dislocations with Wound or Fracture.
When something like this happens, and you attempt to cure it and restore the person, death often follows many times: and because of that, one should not assume about curing it, unless he is wise in long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not bold or presumptuous: and use remedies for incipience, which soothe warm abscesses only: and leave the sick person to divine judgment. Unless you see, or hope for salvation from destruction with the gentleness of illness, and where something appears to give rise to hope. So then perform immediately at the beginning of the thing, before a hot abscess occurs.
So then if the member returns as we wish, then administer a treatment that soothes the abscess; and care for the wound with ointments that are drying.
What if there is a dislocation with fracture, and the separated fragments are situated in bone, then try to remove them; and do as we said for simple illnesses in those places from which it preceded.
And beware according to your strength, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear: just as my preceding prediction has come to you.
For it is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and more safe for your body among those in your time.
